# Steve Rogers  —  *Captain America*

![[_attachments/steve_rogers.png]]

# Steve Rogers — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# Executive Brief (BLUF)

## Bottom Line Up Front

Steven Grant Rogers is not, and has never been, the man the iconography depicts. The shield, the colors, the cowl, the cadence of newsreel and recruitment poster — these are the legible surface of a subject whose actual organizing structure is far older, far smaller, and far sadder than the symbol. **The serum did not produce him; it published him.** What the file documents, across sixty-plus years of canonical record and dozens of independent hands writing him, is a single uninterrupted person: a chronically ill, undersized, half-orphaned Lower East Side child of the late Depression who learned, before he was ten, that love is a thing you give while sick and that harm to the people you love will occur in rooms you are too small to enter — and who responded to that knowledge with a moral stubbornness so absolute that when, twenty years later, a biochemist's intervention finally matched his body to his will, the will did not change. It scaled. Everything subsequent — the war, the ice, the Avengers, the Nomad and Captain interregnums, the Civil War insurgency, the assassination on the courthouse steps, the resurrection, Dimension Z, the Hydra inversion and its aftermath, the eldering into the unit's grief-object — is the same boy in the alley, getting up.

That is the through-line. State it operationally: **the subject converts grief into vocation and helplessness into protection, continuously, without remission, and at a personal cost he does not register as a cost because he has never known any other arrangement.**

## Who He Is at the Deepest Level

Strip the uniform, the serum, the cohort, the century, and what remains is a particular adaptive solution to a particular early situation. The early situation: a mother (Sarah, immigrant nurse, tubercular, dying slowly through his adolescence) whom he loved and could not save; a father (Joseph, alcohol-impaired veteran of the First World War, violent at home, dead young) whom he could not stop and against whom he organized the entirety of his adult ethical posture; a body that failed him in measurable ways — asthma, anemia, rheumatic sequelae, scoliosis, partial deafness, fractured orbits from fights he refused to lose by walking away — and a neighborhood economy of small daily violences in which the only currency he could mint was refusal-to-stay-down. The solution he assembled, by mid-adolescence, had three components: (1) *moral fixity* as compensation for physical incapacity, (2) *caretaking* as the form love is supposed to take, and (3) *the refusal to be the bystander he had been forced to be in his own home*. That solution is the person. Everything subsequent decorates it.

Therefore, at the deepest level, Rogers is not a soldier, not a symbol, not a patriot in any of the marketing senses of that word, and not, despite the recurring temptation of the iconography, a knight. He is **a custodian** — specifically, a custodian of debts owed to the dead and of protection owed to the small. The custodianship is *prior to* the country, prior to the Army, prior to the Avengers, prior to the shield. When the country is worthy he serves it; when it is not he opposes it; in neither case does the custodianship itself waver. This is why he can shed the name "Captain America" (Nomad arc, *Captain America* #180; "The Captain" arc, Gruenwald #337+) without losing the function, and why he cannot ever truly retire the function even when he yields the name (Sam Wilson succession): the costume is municipal, the duty is constitutive.

## The Through-Line

A single sentence covers six decades of publication: *he does not stop getting up, and he does not stop carrying the dead*.

Unpacked, the through-line has two strands woven so tightly they read as one filament:

**Strand A — the somatic-ethical refusal.** From the alley fights of the late 1930s ("I can do this all day") through the Erskine procedure (his selection on moral, not physical, criteria), through the war, through Zola's interrogations, through the ice, through every confrontation with the Skull and Zemo, through Streets of Poison, through Civil War's surrender (he stops fighting the moment civilians become collateral; he does not stop *believing*), through the Reborn timeline-displacement, through the de-serumed nonagenarian arc, through the post-Hydra restoration: the same operational sentence. *Whatever can be borne, I will bear; whatever can be refused, I will refuse; and the cost of either will be paid by me before it is paid by anyone in my care.*

**Strand B — the debt to the dead.** Erskine, killed in front of him within hours of the procedure, becomes the transferential father whose unfinished work is the entirety of the subject's adult mandate. Bucky's fall from the Zemo drone (and, later, the sixty years' worth of intensified guilt when Bucky is revealed to have survived as a weaponized asset) becomes the load-bearing private grief that cannot be discharged because its object refuses, narratively, to stay discharged. Peggy's slow disappearance into dementia in the care facility — visited ritually, sometimes recognized, sometimes not — becomes the chronic somatic-existential exposure that the serum cannot blunt and that alcohol, foreclosed by his metabolism, cannot mute. Sarah, decades back. The Howling Commandos. The cohort. The serial losses are not separate events on his timeline; they are a single continuous condition he carries, like weather, beneath the work.

The two strands are the same strand. The refusal to stay down is **how he pays the debt**. The debt is **why the refusal cannot end**.

## The Core Wound and Its Maintenance

The wound is not Bucky. Bucky is the canonical exemplar, but the wound predates the war. The wound is the configuration laid down in the Rogers tenement: *to love is to be unable to protect, and to be unable to protect is to be the cause*. He could not save his mother from her disease, his father from the bottle, himself from the body he was born into, or the household from the violence inside it. He internalized causation for events he was structurally incapable of altering — the universal solution of the small child to powerlessness in the home — and never revised the structure, because the world subsequently kept handing him precisely the same problem at larger and larger scales. Bucky on the drone is the alley. The ice is the alley. Peggy in the care facility is the alley. Civil War's collateral damage is the alley. The Hydra-Steve inversion — being made, against his will, the instrument of harm to people in his care — is the alley taken to its terminal psychological form: *what if, despite everything, you are the father after all?* His response to restoration, characteristically, is not denial but accountability for things he did not in any defensible sense do. The wound requires it.

**Maintenance mechanisms — what keeps the wound load-bearing rather than crushing:**

- *Sublimation at industrial scale.* The work of being Captain America metabolizes the grief into protective action continuously; idle time is the dangerous time, and he engineers his life to have very little of it.
- *Altruism as currency.* He gives away what he cannot keep for himself — safety, recognition, rest, the right to grieve openly — and the giving regulates the affect that the keeping would dysregulate.
- *Suppression on a tactical schedule.* He defers feeling until "an operationally appropriate moment" which, in his case, almost never arrives; the deferral is the regulation.
- *Mentorship as repair-in-displacement.* Bucky, Rick Jones, Sam, Walker (the man who became what Steve fears is latent in the iconography, taken back and trained and forgiven), Ian in Dimension Z, the successors generally. Each rescued or rehabilitated younger is a microscopic restitution to the boy he could not be for himself or for Bucky.
- *Inhibitory mastery as identity.* Streets of Poison is the canonical demonstration of what removing his inhibition produces (brutality, uncharacteristic rage, a self he does not recognize) and the source of his subsequent phobic-grade refusal of caffeine, alcohol equivalents, any pharmacological mood agent — even though, post-serum, none of them could chemically reach him. The refusal is not chemical; it is identitarian. *He cannot risk not recognizing himself, because the recognition is what keeps the wound from becoming the wounding.*
- *Encysted affect as containment.* Losses are reported in flat briefing-room cadence (Avengers #56 on Bucky; vol. 5 #14 on Peggy). This is not absence. It is feeling so densely packed it can only be touched obliquely, and the obliquity is what permits the function to continue.

**What he is NOT doing to maintain the wound, importantly:** he is not denying it, not splitting it off, not projecting it, not anesthetizing it (he metabolically cannot), not somatizing it (the body is no longer that kind of instrument), not enacting it as cruelty, not weaponizing it as grievance. The maintenance is uniformly mature-spectrum, which is why the pathology is functionally invisible to most observers and why the file is necessary: the man is grieving without pause and has been for the entirety of his adult life, and the symptom is *competence*.

## Operational Summary

For the purposes of any subsequent dossier section, asset handler, ally, adversary, or institutional principal:

**Prime motivators (ranked, stable across canon).** (1) Protection of non-combatants and the structurally vulnerable. (2) Honor of debts to the named dead (Erskine, Bucky-as-was, Peggy, Sarah, the cohort). (3) Personal embodiment of principles that institutions have abandoned. (4) Belonging to a working unit that operates within his ethics. These are not negotiable and are not substitutable for one another.

**Predictable behaviors.** Trades himself instantly and without consultation for non-combatants in his vicinity. Yields command to qualified subordinates without ego cost. Receives subordinate dissent well; updates on argument, not authority. Refuses lawful orders the moment lawful process becomes the instrument of harm — and at that point becomes implacable, slow to anger but impossible to redirect. Forgives broken successors who demonstrate repair. Will not sever a bond he has formed; every major rupture in his life has been initiated by the other party.

**Reliable pressure points.** Civilians at risk in proximity. Institutional betrayals of names he has lent. Constraint from action while others are harmed (his single least-tolerable condition, and the engine of most authority confrontations on record). Cues to the named dead, which produce the flat encysted register that signals the unmetabolized layer beneath. Any threat to his inhibitory control — chemical, mystical, technological, ideological. Demands that he retire the function: equivalent, in his internal accounting, to a demand that he default on the dead, and the cost of making the demand is the loss of him as an ally.

**Reliable non-vulnerabilities.** Money, status, sex, intoxicants, flattery, threats to his own life, threats of disgrace, threats of obscurity. None of these reach him. He has been famous and unnamed, decorated and disowned, alive and assassinated, and the behavior is invariant across all of them.

**Failure modes.** Two, and only two, on the record. *Streets-of-Poison decompensation:* inhibitory mastery stripped by external chemical or mind-control intervention, producing unmodulated aggression he correctly fears and afterward refuses ever to risk again. *Post-loss withdrawal:* temporary depressive contraction following cumulative grief events (post-Bucky, post-Peggy, post-Civil War, post-Secret Empire restoration), in which he becomes quiet, solitary, and operationally narrower for a measurable interval before sublimation re-engages. He does not break loudly. He goes quiet and then he goes alone. He returns.

**Risk profile.** Self-sacrificial behavior that superficially resembles suicidality is not suicidality — the distinction is rigorously preserved across canon: he takes mortal risks *in pursuit of others' survival* and chooses life every time life is genuinely on offer (most explicitly in *Reborn*, when a path back exists). The risk-taking is licensed by decades of evidence that his body tolerates what kills others; it is not sought. Treat as duty-of-care recklessness, not as ideation.

**Handler/ally guidance.** He will give more than is asked and will not articulate need. Care must be offered structurally, not requested into; he will refuse a direct offer and accept a presence that does not require him to name what it is for. He is at his most dangerous to himself in the idle interval after a closure and at his most stable in the early phase of a clearly framed mandate. He sleeps lightly. He scans rooms. He is never fully off-duty and is not improved by being asked to pretend that he is.

**Adversary guidance (counter-intelligence read).** He can be predicted but not corrupted. He can be wounded but not deterred. He can be killed and has been; the institutional and personal blowback of doing so is historically catastrophic to the killer. He can be deceived about facts but very rarely about character, and he updates on character evidence faster than on documentary evidence. The two exploitable cognitive features are his moral-before-tactical sequencing (Zemo and the Skull both work this seam) and his constitutional difficulty with the scenario in which doing the right thing for one person necessitates abandoning another — which is the seam Civil War opened and the seam any sophisticated adversary will return to.

## The Synthesizing Read

The subject is a high-functioning, mature-defended, chronically grieving moral exemplar whose pathology is invisible because it has been seamlessly converted into vocation, and whose vocation is invisible-as-pathology because it has been seamlessly converted into iconography. **Strip both inversions and the file resolves to one sentence: a sick child from a violent house decided, before he had any means to enforce the decision, that he would not be the one who stood by, and he has not, in any subsequent decade or body or century or world, broken that decision.** The serum gave him reach. The war gave him scale. The ice gave him solitude. The cohort gave him debts. The country gave him a name and intermittently tried to take it back. None of it altered the decision. The decision is the subject.

He is, in the most precise sense the file can offer, *the boy in the alley, eighty subjective years later, still getting up, still carrying everyone he has ever lost, and still — quietly, unfailingly, at cost — refusing to be the bystander*. Everything in the subsequent sections of this dossier is a footnote to that.

— End Executive Brief.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# Chronology I — Origin & Childhood
### SUBJECT: ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT — Lower East Side / Brooklyn substrate, c. 1918/1920 – c. 1936

> Filing note. This section reconstructs the pre-enlistment substrate: birth, parentage, household, neighborhood, schooling, somatic record, the recurring micro-behaviors that became, decades later, the operating system of the man in the uniform. Sources are drawn from the primary Simon/Kirby material (*Captain America Comics* #1, March 1941), the canonical retcons and elaborations in *Tales of Suspense* #63 (1965), *Captain America* vol. 1 #176, #225, #247, #255 (Stern/Byrne, 1981), the Gruenwald long run (#307–443), Waid's *Captain America: Man Out of Time* (2010–11), Brubaker's *Captain America* vol. 5 (#1–50, esp. the flashback inserts), Spencer's *Sam Wilson: Captain America* digressions, and the post-2012 reweavings (Remender, Coates). Where canon is silent, an inference is flagged as such and is anchored to a behavioral exemplar from the adult record working backwards.

---

## 1. BIRTH, NAME, AND THE PUBLIC-RECORD AMBIGUITY

Subject is registered across canon as **Steven Grant Rogers**, born **July 4** — a calendrical coincidence later weaponized for symbolic effect, but in the household contemporaneously meaningless: the family did not own a calendar that bothered with the federal holiday in the way the iconography later implied. Year of birth slides between writers across an eighteen-year window depending on de-aging conventions, but the *internal* dating — established in *Captain America* #255 and confirmed by Brubaker — places him as an adolescent during the deepest trough of the Depression (roughly 1929–1934) and as a draftable young adult by Pearl Harbor. The relevant intelligence fact is not the year but the *cohort*: he is a child of the immigrant-Catholic Lower East Side / pre-war Brooklyn working class, his early years bracketed by Prohibition's last gasp on one end and the WPA on the other.

Place of birth: a tenement flat, **Lower East Side, Manhattan**, with later relocation across the river to **Brooklyn** (the address varies — Stern places the family near the Navy Yard; Brubaker has the boy in a railroad apartment off what would now be the Williamsburg/Greenpoint axis). Delivery by a midwife, not in a hospital — a detail Sarah Rogers is depicted alluding to in the *Man Out of Time* flashback panels and which is corroborated indirectly by the absence of any reference to a birth-hospital in his enlistment paperwork in *Tales of Suspense* #63.

Baptized Catholic. The Catholicism is residual but operative: the subject as an adult crosses himself reflexively before certain combat drops (an unobtrusive panel beat repeated by Stern, Gruenwald, and Brubaker), keeps no rosary but knows the prayers, and refers to confession as a concept he understands from the inside. Adult ethical vocabulary — duty, debt, witness, the language of standing — has the cadence of a working-class Irish-Catholic moral grammar even where the theology has been emptied out.

---

## 2. FAMILY OF ORIGIN

### 2.1 Sarah Rogers (mother)

Irish immigrant, almost certainly first-generation (the Brubaker flashbacks give her an audible brogue, intermittently retained); trained or self-trained as a **nurse**, working in tuberculosis wards — the cruel irony being that the disease that takes her is the one she nursed. Widowed early. Across the canon she is the **stable, warm, exhausted** parental object: she is the one who reads to the sick boy, who walks him to the clinic, who refuses to let him quit school despite the household need for income, and who, in the formative *Captain America* #255 sequence, instills the directly quoted maxim that resurfaces in the subject's adult speech: *you always stand up*.

Health trajectory: tuberculosis, contracted occupationally, untreated long enough that by the time it was diagnosed it was terminal. Death when Steve is in **late adolescence** — old enough to nurse her himself in the final months, young enough that he is functionally orphaned into a city with no surviving close kin and no resources. The behavioral residue is heavy and specific: as an adult he is calm and capable around the dying, is the team member who sits with the wounded, knows how to change a dressing without performance, and treats nursing — *the labor of staying present with someone who is leaving* — as the highest unglamorous virtue. The "I'll sit with him" posture he assumes at deathbeds (Bucky, Erskine, Peggy, repeatedly) is **Sarah's posture**, transposed.

Attachment classification (derived): **secure-but-overburdened**. The bond was real, affectionate, and reliable in *quality* but **resource-thin**; love arrived already tired. The lifelong adult pattern of *equating love with caretaking-under-strain*, of giving more than is asked and considering one's own needs an embarrassment, is the direct downstream signature.

### 2.2 Joseph Rogers (father)

Veteran of the American Expeditionary Forces, **World War I**; canonical implications across Waid and Brubaker include mustard-gas exposure and what would now be diagnosed as PTSD; alcoholic; intermittent laborer; **violent in the home**. Dies young — variously of pneumonia, alcohol-related sequelae, or, in some tellings, a workplace accident — when Steve is between roughly five and eight years old, leaving the boy with a witnessed but not remembered-clearly record of paternal violence against Sarah and, on at least one canonical occasion (a Waid flashback), against the boy himself.

The intelligence-value of Joseph is **almost entirely negative-template**. He is the figure Steve organizes his adult ethical identity *against*: the soldier whose war broke him into a man who hurt the people he was supposed to protect. Every adult micro-decision in which the subject refuses alcohol (the *Annual* #4 gag is not a gag, it is a vow he is relieved to be biologically released from), refuses pharmacological mood agents (Streets of Poison aftermath, *Captain America* #372–378), checks his anger with visible effort, and treats domestic violence in any operational context as a personal-stakes affront — every one of these is a daily disavowal of Joseph. The adult subject's near-phobic relation to **loss of inhibitory control** (see §4) is, at its root, the small boy's vow never to become the man in his kitchen.

Joseph is also, however, the source of the subject's **first encounter with the moral structure of a soldier's debt**: he was, before he was a danger, a man who had gone to a war and come back ruined. The subject as an adult is unusually generous to broken veterans, slow to judge alcoholic colleagues (Tony pre-recovery, Logan, Carol at various points), and quick to read intoxication as wound rather than character. The Joseph-template is two-edged: a thing not to become, and a thing to forgive in others before they ask.

### 2.3 Siblings, extended family

None canonical and surviving. A stillborn or infant-mortality older sibling is implied in two Brubaker captions (*Cap* vol. 5 #11) and would be consistent with the period and the family's economic profile; un-confirmable. No aunts, uncles, or cousins are depicted as present in the household or as functional support after Sarah's death. The boy is, by the canonical adolescent depictions, **alone in the city** in a way that is structurally important: there is no relative to whom anything can be deferred, no household to fall back into, no Christmas table beyond the one he and Sarah set for two.

---

## 3. CHILDHOOD ENVIRONMENT

### 3.1 The household, materially

A railroad flat or a cold-water tenement, depending on which decade of canon one credits; in all versions: **shared bathroom in the hall**, **coal stove**, **windows facing an airshaft**, **no telephone**, intermittently no electricity. Heating unreliable; the asthmatic boy's winters are explicitly miserable in the *Man Out of Time* flashbacks. Clothes from a parish charity bin and from Sarah's mending. Diet: bread, potatoes, milk when affordable, occasional meat; the malnutrition shows up later on the enlistment exam (*Tales of Suspense* #63) as low weight-for-height and as the anemia that will dog him until Erskine.

Operationally, the long adult tell is **his indifference to material comfort**. He does not collect, does not accumulate, lives across decades in spartan rooms (the Brooklyn apartment in Brubaker; the safehouses; the room he keeps at Avengers Mansion is the smallest one). He eats whatever is in front of him. He is unmoved by money. Tony's wealth bemuses him in a way that is not class resentment but class *opacity*: he genuinely does not understand why one would want more than one needs. This is not asceticism. It is the **somatic memory of a household in which wanting more was painful and so was disciplined out**.

### 3.2 The neighborhood

The block is **immigrant, polyglot, Catholic-Jewish-Italian-Irish**, with an active street life and an active street violence. The subject's adult ease with diverse company — his unforced respect for Sam Wilson's authority, his comfort in Black, Jewish, and immigrant spaces across the runs from Englehart through Coates — is rooted here. He grew up hearing four languages on the stairwell. He learned early that the people who lived next to him were not the people the radio said they were. This is the **experiential ground of his lifelong instinctive anti-bigotry**: not principle in the abstract, but the memory of the woman two doors down who fed him when Sarah was on a double shift.

The other thing the neighborhood taught him was **the geography of escape and approach**: alleys, fire escapes, rooftops, the way blocks connect through coal chutes and shared back lots. Adult tactical signature — he reads urban environments in three dimensions instinctively, prefers rooftop approach, uses fire escapes the way other operators use vehicles — is **Brooklyn 1930 fossilized into a combat idiom**.

### 3.3 The Depression as ambient pressure

He is a boy during the worst years. Bread lines, shuttered shops, evictions on the sidewalk with the furniture piled up — these are the visual backdrop of the canonical flashbacks. The household is on the edge throughout. Sarah works double shifts; the boy is alone for long hours; the **self-sufficient, parentified child** is born here. He cooks for himself by ten. He nurses himself through his own fevers. He learns to **defer his own needs as a routine domestic habit**, not a virtue. The adult signature — chronic over-functioning, difficulty receiving care, treatment of his own needs as luxuries — is **set in the kitchen of a flat where the only other adult was working a double**.

---

## 4. THE BODY: PRE-SERUM SOMATIC RECORD

The medical substrate (cross-reference §2 of the Analysis) belongs structurally in this chronology because the body is itself a formative environment.

- **Asthma**, severe enough to bar him from physical play, requiring (in the period idiom) ephedrine inhalers when affordable and warm-steam improvisation when not.
- **Scarlet fever** in childhood, with rheumatic sequelae implied (heart murmur on later exam).
- **Chronic sinusitis**, recurrent.
- **Anemia**, attributable to diet.
- **Partial deafness, one ear** (Brubaker establishes this; the cause is unspecified — likely a poorly treated childhood ear infection).
- **Scoliosis**, mild.
- **Hypertension** at enlistment (*Tales of Suspense* #63 explicitly lists it among the 4-F findings).
- **Recurrent orbital, nasal, and zygomatic fractures** from street altercations, healed without medical attention.
- **Color blindness** in some tellings (inconsistent across writers; flagged as canon-soft).

The relevant **psychological** facts that derive from this somatic record:

1. **Smallness as identity.** Through the entire formative period — roughly age six to age twenty-two — he is the smallest, sickest, weakest male in any room. His self-image is sedimented around this fact. The adult never quite stops thinking of himself as the small one; the serum is, internally, *prosthesis*, not transformation.

2. **The unreliable instrument.** The body lets him down constantly: an asthma attack mid-fight, a fever that pins him to the cot for a week, a winter cough that lingers into spring. This produces a permanent **mistrust of one's own apparatus** that, post-serum, transposes into a *refusal to over-rely on the enhanced body*: he trains relentlessly, plans for the body's failure, never assumes his physical condition will be there for him. He is the only enhanced operator in the canon who **trains as though un-enhanced** (Gruenwald repeatedly shows pre-dawn physical conditioning that is tactically unnecessary).

3. **Shame and its inversion.** The sick boy is repeatedly humiliated — for absence from school, for inability to work, for being a drain on Sarah, for being unable to fight back when struck. The adult's near-total absence of vanity, his impatience with display, his discomfort with being looked at as a body (the discomfort in the USO sequences in any treatment that includes them, the visible irritation at being a poster), is the **inversion of formative bodily shame into bodily reticence**.

4. **Caretaking inward.** He learned to nurse himself; he can sit with his own injuries calmly because he has been sitting with them since he was seven. The eerie calm under wounding that the adult shows is not enhancement — it predates Erskine.

---

## 5. FORMATIVE TRAUMAS

### 5.1 Witnessed paternal violence (age <8)

The earliest load-bearing trauma. He saw his father strike his mother and could not stop it; in one Waid flashback he intervenes physically, is struck himself, and is held back by Sarah. The encoding: **harm to the loved is unbearable; helplessness in the presence of that harm is the most intolerable affect he can experience**. The single most reliable predictor of adult-Rogers behavior in any tactical scenario is this: *can he interpose his body between the harmed and the harmer?* If yes, he will. If no, he will become difficult to manage. The pressure-point inventory in §5 of the Analysis flows directly from this episode.

### 5.2 The father's death (age ~5–8)

Ambiguous in affect. Canon does not give us a child weeping for Joseph. What it gives us is a quieter, more durable trauma: the absence of *anyone* to grieve cleanly. He cannot grieve a man who hurt his mother; he cannot fail to grieve his father. The result is an early lesson in **grief that has to be carried without a place to put it down** — the prototype for every grief that follows.

### 5.3 The alley beatings (recurrent, age ~10–22)

The canonical recurring panel — the small boy or young man, bloodied behind a building, picking himself up and saying *I can do this all day* / *I'll just get up again* (*Man Out of Time* #1; *Captain America* vol. 5 #50 flashback; echoed across Brubaker and Waid) — is the **single most important behavioral datum in the pre-serum record**. What it encodes:

- **Moral stubbornness as substitute for capacity.** He cannot win the fight. He can refuse to concede it. This becomes the operating principle of the adult: in any contest he is losing, the move is *do not yield the principle*. Civil War, the Nomad arc, The Captain arc, every confrontation with the Red Skull — they are all the alley, scaled.

- **A specific somatic memory of getting up.** He has *muscle-memory* of standing up after being knocked down. The adult body knows the motion; the enhanced body executes it faster. The micro-tell — the way he plants a palm and pushes off the knee after a hard knockdown — is the **same motion** he learned at twelve.

- **An identification with the underdog that is not abstract**. He is *not slumming* when he sides with the powerless; he is **returning to a baseline he never left**.

### 5.4 Sarah's illness and death (mid-to-late adolescence)

The deepest pre-war wound. The duration — months of nursing — means this is not a sudden trauma but a **prolonged anticipatory grief**, during which he learned the specific skills (changing linens, managing fever, sitting through bad nights) that will mark his adult deathbed presence. Sarah's death leaves him:

- Functionally **orphaned and alone in the city**.
- **Habituated to losing the person he loves most**, slowly, while continuing to function.
- Carrying a maxim — *you always stand up* — that is now a posthumous instruction, i.e., **a debt**. This is the **prototype of the Erskine-debt structure** that will organize the adult life. He has already learned, before he ever meets Erskine, how to convert a dead caregiver's words into a non-negotiable charge.
- Without a body to bury well; the funeral, in the Brubaker flashback, is sparse — a parish priest, a few neighbors, no extended family — and the boy is depicted as **dry-eyed and composed**, which is the first appearance of the adult's *isolation of affect* defense.

### 5.5 The interregnum (post-Sarah, pre-enlistment)

A canonically thin but inferentially rich period: years of **rooming-house adolescence**, art school (see §6), odd jobs, and the cementing of the friendship with Bucky Barnes (whose role here is a subject for Chronology II, but whose function in this period is the **first peer who knew him before the serum**, and therefore the only person other than Erskine and Sarah who ever *saw the small one and stayed*).

---

## 6. EARLIEST FORMATIVE BONDS BESIDES THE PARENTS

### 6.1 Bucky (James Buchanan Barnes) — pre-war

Established in Brubaker (*Captain America* vol. 5) as a **pre-war friendship**, not — as the Simon/Kirby original implied — a Camp Lehigh meet-cute. The two boys know each other from the neighborhood. Bucky is the **physically capable peer** who fights for the small Steve and is, in the Brubaker reframe, **already the more dangerous of the two** even as a boy. The early dynamic: Steve picks the fights on principle; Bucky finishes them on competence. This division of labor — *Steve declares the moral stake, the partner handles the wet work* — is the **template of every adult operational partnership** Steve will form. It is also the **seed of the survivor-guilt structure** around Bucky: from boyhood, the smaller boy was already letting the bigger one absorb cost on his behalf.

### 6.2 The parish, the teachers, the neighbors

Diffuse but real: a parish priest figure (variously named or unnamed), a public-school art teacher who is the first adult outside the family to tell him he can do something well (see §6.3), a handful of neighborhood women who fed him when Sarah was on shift. These are the **distributed caregivers** whose existence licenses the adult's faith in *the unit, the block, the people who show up*. He believes in mutual aid because he was the recipient of it.

### 6.3 Art and the drafting hand

Canonical and important. Steve **draws**. He attends some form of vocational art training (the original Simon/Kirby material has him sketching; Stern and later writers establish formal study; in modern continuity he is a credible illustrator and was, briefly pre-war, employed in commercial art / WPA-adjacent illustration work). This matters in several specific ways:

- **A visual cognitive style.** He thinks in space, in composition, in figure-ground. Tactical reading of rooms (the way he clocks exits, sightlines, civilian positions) is partly a draftsman's habit of decomposing a scene into its elements.
- **A non-violent skill that is his own.** Long before the serum, he had a private competence — *he could do something well* — that gave him an internal anchor against the somatic shame. The adult's quiet sketching in down moments (Brubaker, Waid, multiple other writers) is not a hobby; it is **the original self-soothing channel**.
- **A vocabulary for the iconic.** He understands, in a way most soldiers do not, how an image works. This is part of why the costume, the shield, the pose, are tolerable to him: he knows what a symbol is *for*. It also explains his ambivalence about the marketing-grade deployment of his image — he knows good design from propaganda from kitsch, and he resents being made into the third.

---

## 7. EARLIEST BEHAVIORAL PATTERNS — THE PROTO-TELLS

The following micro-behaviors, visible in canonical flashbacks and reverse-derivable from the stable adult signature, are documented as **present before the serum** and constitute the **behavioral fingerprint** the file will track across the next sixty years.

1. **The get-up.** Plant the palm, push off the knee, square the shoulders, raise the chin. Trained in alleys. Indistinguishable in the adult except for speed.

2. **The forward step.** Toward danger, not away. Witnessable in the small boy in every fight flashback; in the adult, the universally noted tactical signature.

3. **The flat report of his own injury.** *I'm fine.* Said calmly, while bleeding. The voice of the boy who has been sick alone in a flat for a week and has learned that complaint produces nothing and worry produces Sarah's exhaustion.

4. **The dry-eyed funeral posture.** Composure at the graveside. The affect is not absent; it is encysted. Sarah's funeral is the first instance; every funeral after rhymes with it.

5. **The deference to a working adult.** He stands when older people enter the room, removes his cap indoors, addresses strangers as sir and ma'am until corrected. The adult's formality with civilians and elders is **Sarah's parlor manners**, not the Army's.

6. **The crossing of the self before sleep or risk.** Catholic boyhood, retained as muscle memory.

7. **The sketchbook in the lap during downtime.** From adolescence to the present. The book changes; the hand does not.

8. **Refusal to walk away from a wrong said aloud in his hearing.** The diagnostic *fixed action pattern*. From the alley to the conference room to the Civil War. He will **say the thing**, and once said he cannot unsay it, and once unsaid he must stand to it. The cost of this trait is high and lifelong; it is also the engine of every act for which he is remembered.

9. **A specific kind of looking at people.** Eye contact held a beat longer than American norm, head tilted slightly down because he was looking *up* for so long. The downward-tilt persists post-serum even though the geometry no longer requires it — a **somatic ghost of the small body** that the adult never quite shed.

10. **The capacity to be alone for long periods without distress.** Forged in the empty flat. The adult can sit a four-hour overwatch in silence and emerge unchanged. He does not require company. He prefers it; he does not need it. This is the **double-edged inheritance**: it makes him a superb operator and a difficult intimate, because he can tolerate the absence of those he loves *too well*, and they sometimes feel it.

---

## 8. THE GROUND FLOOR — W


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT. Period covered: c. 1933 (subject aged ~15) through c. 1942 (the first operational year as the enhanced soldier). Sources: Simon/Kirby originals; Lee/Kirby retcon material in* Tales of Suspense *#63–68; Stern/Byrne* Captain America *#247, #255; Waid* Man Out of Time; *Brubaker* Captain America *vol. 5 #1–14, #50 flashbacks; Gruenwald* Captain America *#247, #383; Hickman/Brubaker* Marvels Project; *and the consistent biographical scaffolding maintained across the* Marvel Encyclopedia *entries and editorial-canon handbooks. UATU note: where dates are given as "c.," they reflect the best reconciliation of an inconsistently dated canon; the behavioral content is stable across versions.*

---

## I. ADOLESCENCE: THE SUBSTRATE (c. 1933–1939)

### a. The household at point of emergence

By the time the subject is fifteen, the family is already a survivor unit of one and a half. Joseph Rogers is dead — variously placed by writers between 1925 and 1929, with Brubaker's flashbacks (*Captain America* vol. 5 #50) tilting earlier — leaving Sarah Rogers raising the boy alone on a nurse's wages at a tuberculosis ward, in a railroad flat on the Lower East Side (most often given as somewhere between Avenue A and the river, occasionally relocated to Brooklyn Heights in later runs; the *neighborhood* — immigrant, Catholic, Irish — is invariant). The economic profile is sub-marginal: a single small income; charity coal in winter; clothing visibly resized from elsewhere; the doctor seen only when the asthma turns blue.

The household's emotional register, reconstructed from the consistent flashback iconography, is grave and tender. Sarah is shown ironing while listening to the radio, mending shirts under a single bulb, sitting at the side of the boy's bed while he wheezes through croup. The subject's earliest documented behaviors are caretaking-inversions: at twelve and thirteen he is depicted bringing his mother tea, walking her home from the ward at night, reading scripture aloud when she is too tired to. The asymmetry — sick boy parenting tired mother who is in turn parenting sick patients — is the original template of the adult pattern in which Rogers categorically inverts the rescuer/rescued positions. He learned love as labor done while ill.

### b. The somatic record at adolescence

Documented or clearly implied across canon for the years 1933–1939:

- **Asthma**, severe, exercise- and cold-induced; documented inhaler use (a pre-war Asthmador-type powder, depicted in Waid).
- **Recurrent sinus and bronchial infections**; partial conductive hearing loss in the left ear traced by Brubaker to repeated otitis.
- **Scarlet fever**, surviving (Stern/Byrne #255); **rheumatic-fever sequelae** implied by repeated cardiac concern on later enlistment exams.
- **Anemia** sufficient to require iron and liver supplementation he could not consistently afford.
- **Scoliosis**, mild but visible in the depicted posture of the pre-serum figure.
- **Chronic malnutrition** consistent with Depression-era poverty: short stature (5'4" at peak pre-serum), low body mass (95–98 lb), persistent fatigue.
- **Repeated facial trauma** from street altercations: depicted with a healed nasal fracture and orbital bruising at near-baseline in pre-serum panels.

The clinical import of this list is not the catalogue itself but the *duration*: from earliest memory to age twenty-three, the subject lived inside a body that hurt, that failed in cold weather, that drew commentary, that could not finish what it started, and that he was nonetheless ethically required to use. This is the somatic ground of the adult tendency to treat his enhanced body as borrowed and consequently expendable.

### c. School, art, and the first vocational signal

The subject is consistently placed in New York City public schools and, by the late 1930s, in some configuration of art instruction — most often the WPA Federal Art Project's free classes, occasionally a partial scholarship to a commercial art school (Pratt is named in one Stern aside; the Art Students League in Waid). He is depicted as a competent draftsman with a particular gift for figure and gesture, employed irregularly as an inker, a sign-painter, a comics-page assistant, and, in the Stern continuity, a freelance illustrator for pulp magazines.

Two behavioral findings emerge from this period:

1. **Aesthetic literacy precedes politics.** The subject's first articulated objections to fascism in the canonical record are visual: he draws a Nazi rally cartoon for a left-wing flier (Stern #255 backmatter; recapitulated in Waid). The route into politics is via the eye — he *sees* iconography being weaponized and resents the theft. This will recur in the adult: he is unusually sensitive to symbolic abuse and to the difference between an image of justice and the practice of it.

2. **He is a watcher.** Schoolyard and street observation give him an early-coded talent for reading rooms — who is about to be picked on, who is about to swing first, who is faking. This is the substrate of the adult Hickman-era depiction of Rogers as the man who waits to speak last in a strategy session.

### d. The alley pattern

The single most-cited behavioral motif of the adolescence is the alley fight. Across at least a dozen flashback panels (Waid; Brubaker; Stern; Gruenwald #383), the subject is shown intervening — against bullies, against a man hitting a woman, against a group harassing a Jewish street vendor, against a roust by toughs of a smaller boy — and being beaten. The recurring line ("I can do this all day" / "I'll just get up again") is *not* introduced by the films; it is encoded in mid-1970s panel work and explicitly thematized by Waid.

The pattern is granular and consistent:

- He does not provoke; he interposes.
- He does not threaten; he stands between.
- He does not negotiate; he absorbs.
- He does not stay down; he rises until consciousness fails.

The fights are lost, often badly, and he is brought home — by Bucky Barnes in the most common configuration — bleeding, with a split lip, with a tooth gone, with cracked ribs that Sarah strapped. The repetition matters because *the loss is internal evidence of the principle*. He learned that the body's capacity to win is irrelevant to the obligation to stand. Every subsequent ethical posture in the dossier is downstream of this: when an adult Rogers refuses to surrender a moral ground despite tactical inferiority (Civil War; Registration; the insurgency arcs), he is reading from the same script.

### e. Bucky as childhood anchor

Modern continuity (Brubaker vol. 5) consolidates James Buchanan Barnes as a neighborhood peer from boyhood — slightly younger in early Simon/Kirby, contemporaneously rewritten as slightly older and tougher in Brubaker. The relevant fact is the function: Barnes is the boy who *won the fights Steve started*, walked him home, sat with him during asthma attacks, and modeled for him an unselfconscious physical competence Steve could neither imitate nor resent. This is the formative peer attachment — the first relationship in his life that was not caretaking-under-strain but lateral, reciprocal, and physically protective in his direction.

The downstream cost is that the loss of Bucky in 1945 is not the loss of a sidekick; it is the loss of the only person who had ever taken care of *him*. The complicated-grief signature of the adult is calibrated against this specific absence.

---

## II. THE TURNING (c. 1936–1940): LOSSES AND POLITICAL FORMATION

### a. Sarah's death

Most often placed in the subject's late adolescence (variously 1936–1937 in pre-Brubaker work; sometimes pulled forward into 1935 in Waid). Cause: tuberculosis contracted, in the consistent telling, from the ward she worked. He is at the bedside. He arranges the burial out of a savings tin Sarah had kept against this contingency, a detail Waid emphasizes — *she predicted her death and prepared him for it* — which fixes one of the subject's permanent cognitive frames: that love is a discipline of preparing the beloved to survive your absence.

The behavioral residue is observable for the rest of the canon:

- He does not speak of Sarah at length, ever, in any continuity. The grief is encysted from this date forward — the prototype for the isolation-of-affect signature later visible in his reports of Bucky and Peggy.
- He keeps small religious objects (a rosary, in some versions; a worn devotional card in others) associated with her, on his person or in his footlocker, across the entire war record.
- He develops an enduring deference toward nurses, female medical workers, and any woman performing thankless caretaking labor. Sharon Carter and Peggy Carter are not in this category; *Bernie Rosenthal*, *Rachel Leighton's caretaking arc*, and his unmistakable warmth toward background medical personnel across runs are.

### b. The orphan year(s)

Between Sarah's death and the first enlistment attempt, the subject is alone. The room is downsized; a furnished room or boarding-house is the most-cited residence. He is depicted picking up irregular work — sign-painting; commercial illustration; a brief WPA mural assignment in some versions; nightshift dishwashing in others. He is undernourished in a way that begins to be deliberate: he gives food to people he sees doing worse. He attends rallies. He reads voraciously and unsystematically — the canonical reading list ranges from Lincoln's speeches to *The Grapes of Wrath* to Catholic social teaching to popular accounts of the Spanish Civil War. He sketches at meetings.

The political formation in this window is moral and pre-ideological. He is not a communist, not a Catholic Worker, not a Wilsonian, not anything that catalogues neatly. He is anti-fascist from direct observation of the iconography and from a personally-felt analogy: bullies grow into states. This is the engine by which, when war comes, he will not perceive enlistment as a question.

### c. The first enlistment attempt(s)

The canonical motif of *4-F rejection* is iterated — the precise count varies (four in Waid; "five times" in Stern; "again and again" in Simon/Kirby's terse original). The cause is the somatic catalogue above. The pattern of his response is what matters:

- He travels to different recruiting stations to try again under variant paperwork — a low-grade fraud committed in service of conscription, which Waid plays for the moral paradox it is.
- He does not appeal, complain, or argue with the recruiting clerks. He thanks them and walks out and goes to the next one.
- He continues to lose weight in the interval, mistakenly believing illness might be re-classified as something correctable; the result is further deterioration.

This is the moment in the longitudinal record at which the adult pattern of *refusing to interpret personal need as a luxury* hardens into character. The need here is not personal — it is the need to be *permitted to risk himself for others*. Every later refusal of pain medication, leave, decoration, sleep, or comfort echoes the boy at the recruiting desk asking to be permitted to die.

---

## III. THE ERSKINE GATE (c. 1940–1941)

### a. The selection

The recruitment to Project Rebirth is, in the canonical record, an act of moral spotting by Dr. Abraham Erskine (in some continuities, "Reinstein" as cover name). Erskine, observing the subject at a recruiting station — having seen the falsified paperwork, the repeated attempts, the visible frailty, and the obstinate quiet — selects him on the *anti-physical* criterion: a man who has known weakness will not become drunk on strength.

The selection event is the second great formative imprint after Sarah's death. The subject is, for the first time in his life, told that the thing he has been ashamed of — the body — is the *qualifying* feature. The moral content of the encounter — Erskine's articulation of what the serum is *for*, that it amplifies what is already there — lodges in the subject as a permanent operating instruction. From this hour forward, he will treat his enhanced body as held in trust against a description Erskine gave of him.

### b. Camp Lehigh: training as identity-laboratory

Subject is moved to the secret facility at Camp Lehigh, New Jersey. The setting is consistently rendered: barracks; Quonset huts; a hidden lab beneath an antiques shop in some versions or under the parade ground in others; Colonel Phillips's office; the cohort of candidate soldiers from which Erskine had selected.

Behavioral data from the pre-procedure period:

- Subject is hazed; refuses to escalate; absorbs and continues. Phillips's notes (per Stern #255) record him as "the worst soldier and the best man" of the candidate pool.
- Subject is observed *teaching* other candidates: shows them sketches he has drawn of obstacle-course routes; explains the geometry of cover. Talent for instruction is operational from day one.
- A standard test in the canonical retelling involves a thrown dummy grenade; the subject covers it with his body while others scatter. The test is not a test of bravery — Phillips already knows he is brave. It is a test of *reflex priority*. The subject's body, untrained, defaults to interposition. This is the empirical observation that lets Erskine sign off.

### c. The procedure

Date conventionally fixed as a morning in early 1941. Setting: the concealed laboratory, with Senator Brandt and select military and intelligence observers, a German émigré named Heinz Kruger seeded as a Nazi agent in the gallery, and Erskine attending the subject personally. Procedure: intravenous and oral administration of the Super-Soldier Serum followed by Vita-Ray bombardment in a sealed chamber.

The somatic event is described consistently: pain, heat, a long minute of visible distress, then the chamber unsealed to reveal a transformed body — approximately 6'2", ~220 lb, proportions consistent with peak human development. The subject's first action is *to ask whether it worked* — a question whose innocence is preserved across writers and which is diagnostically central: it indicates that the transformation has not registered phenomenologically as an event he is the subject of. His sense of self has not yet caught up to the body. It will, arguably, never fully catch up.

### d. The assassination of Erskine

Within minutes — in some tellings under a minute — Heinz Kruger draws and fires, killing Erskine. The serum's formula, retained only in Erskine's memory, dies with him. The subject pursues the assassin on foot through the streets adjacent to the facility, killing him in the course of the pursuit (in earlier continuity by direct impact; in later, more morally curated versions, by causing the assassin's vehicle to crash, or by a struggle in which Kruger dies on his own weapon).

The behavioral and psychiatric significance is layered:

1. **The first independent act of the enhanced soldier is the failed defense of a father-figure.** The pattern — *I was strong enough but not soon enough* — is now permanently inscribed. It will replay against Bucky on the drone, against Peggy in the ice, against every later loss.
2. **The kill is not celebrated and not avoided.** The subject neither becomes squeamish about lethality nor habituated to it; he treats Kruger's death as the unavoidable cost of an obligation he has already accepted. This is the prototype for the adult's calibrated willingness to kill — sparingly, regretfully, and only in defense.
3. **Erskine's last words** (variously rendered: "Stay who you are… not a perfect soldier, but a good man") become a literal scriptural text the subject carries internally for life. The adult Rogers' moral self-checks — explicit in his internal monologues across Englehart, Gruenwald, Brubaker — are functionally a recitation against this rubric.
4. **The serum's irreproducibility is morally weaponized.** Because he is the *only* one, he understands his body as a non-fungible asset held in trust for a project that was supposed to have created hundreds. He is, from this moment, a one-man memorial to a program that died with its author.

### e. The "Captain America" assignment

The post-procedure decision to deploy him as a costumed public-facing symbol rather than (or in addition to) a covert asset comes from the political officers around the program — Senator Brandt in the early Lee/Kirby reconstruction; the War Bond apparatus in Stern; the OWI/propaganda nexus in Brubaker's *Marvels Project* and *vol. 5*. The subject *accepts* the assignment. The acceptance is one of the most consequential decisions in the longitudinal record and is worth disaggregating:

- He accepts the iconography because Erskine's death has made him refuse to be sequestered as a single asset; he wants the program's intent — visibility, moral demonstration — preserved even if the science cannot be.
- He accepts the costume — early version: scale mail, gloves, the kite-shaped shield; replaced within the first year by the circular disc — without ideological self-consciousness; it is, to him, a uniform with a flag on it, which his employer is the United States.
- He accepts the *touring component* (bond drives, theater performances, photo-ops with children) and finds it humiliating and useful in equal measure. Waid's *Man Out of Time* is explicit that he *knew it was theater* and consented because the alternative was wastage.
- He does **not** accept the rank ambiguity. Records across continuities place him as a Captain (the title and the function aligned) but with idiosyncratic command latitude; he routinely deploys outside chain of command and is tolerated because the symbolic value outweighs the discipline cost. The adult pattern — loyalty to persons and principles over organizations — is operational from his first month in uniform.

---

## IV. THE FIRST OPERATIONAL YEAR (c. 1941–1942)

### a. Bucky's discovery and the camp partnership

The Simon/Kirby origin of the Steve–Bucky pairing — Bucky stumbles on Steve changing into the uniform, is brought in as the mascot — has been substantially reworked by Brubaker into a deliberate handler/asset relationship: Bucky is the trained covert operator assigned *to* Captain America, the wet-work counterpart who lets the symbol stay clean.

Whichever version is operative, the granular behavioral record is consistent:

- Steve treats Bucky as a peer, never as a subordinate, despite the formal command relationship.
- Steve is the one who insists on Bucky's safety; Bucky is the one who insists on Steve's deniability.
- The partnership re-instantiates the childhood pattern — the more competent physical operator beside the morally exposed protagonist — with their *roles socially inverted* (Steve now the larger body) but their *psychological positions preserved* (Bucky still the protector).
- Steve does not perceive the inversion. This blindness is the seed of the postwar guilt: he will retroactively believe he should have known he was endangering the friend who had always protected him.

### b. The European theater and the Invaders

Early operational record (Simon/Kirby; Roy Thomas's *Invaders*; Brubaker's reconstructions) places Captain America in mixed assignments: sabotage and rescue missions in occupied Europe; appearances alongside the Howling Commandos; integration into the Invaders with Namor, the original Human Torch, Toro, Spitfire, Union Jack. Granular behavioral findings from this year:

- He is observed as the *unit's binding agent.* He is the one who reconciles Namor's volatility with Torch's reserve; the one who learns each member's idiom and translates between them. The adult Avengers-era pattern is fully present in nuce.
- He is **non-fraternization-compliant** with women he encounters in the field except for Peggy Carter, with whom an attachment forms that is intermittent across the war years (driven by the operational tempo, by the secrecy of her SOE/SSR role, and by his own discomfort with self-disclosure).
- He learns languages functionally — workable French, kitchen German, fragments of Italian — and prefers to be the one who interrogates prisoners because he believes the manner of the interrogation is a moral act.
- He maintains the sketchbook. Field drawings of villages, of fellow soldiers, of liberated detainees, of corpses, recur in flashback panels across writers. The drawing is private; he does not share it. It is a residue of the pre-war self insisting on continuity.

### c. The first encounter with the Red Skull

Most often dated to late 1941 or 1942. Significance: the first canonical confrontation with an adversary whom the subject perceives as *the iconographic inverse of himself* — a man also created (in some versions, by an inferior parallel program) to embody an ideology, but whose ideology is annihilationist. The Skull is, from this first meeting, the subject's lifelong personal antagonist, and the antagonism is structured not as hatred but as *recognition*: each understands the other as the negative print of his own image. Rogers' later sparing of recoverable villains and his absolute non-sparing of the Skull is calibrated against this first encounter.

### d. Peggy Carter

The wartime attachment to Margaret "Peggy" Carter, formed in the European theater, is the subject's first adult romantic bond. Reconstructed behavioral content:

- He defers initiation; she initiates.
- He is comfortable with her competence and discomfited by her superiority of social ease; she is the more sophisticated party.
- The relationship is conducted in interruptions — operations, separations, near-misses — and is, at the moment of the ice, unconsummated as a public commitment.
- He carries a photograph. The photograph recurs across decades of panels as a stable artifact: in his footlocker, in his quarters at Avengers Mansion, in his wallet, in the care-facility bedside drawer when he visits her dementia-stricken decades later.

The unfinished structure of the relationship is the second load-bearing wound of the postwar life. He did not get to fail Peggy by surviving her in real time; he failed her by surviving her *out of sequence*.

---

## V. THE FORMATIVE DECISIONS AND THEIR FALLOUT

The "first defining choices" of this formation period, considered as a discrete cluster:

1. **The choice to enlist despite ineligibility.** Fallout: established him as the kind of moral agent for whom *being permitted to risk* is itself a need; this is the lifelong driver of his quarrels with constraint.
2. **The choice to accept Erskine's selection.** Fallout: bound his enhanced existence to a dead man's intention; converted physical capacity into stewardship; made retirement structurally impossible.
3. **The choice to chase Kruger.** Fallout: established his reflex priority — to pursue the wrong rather than to absorb the loss — and inscribed the failure-of-defense pattern that organizes the survivor guilt.
4. **The choice to accept the costume and tour.** Fallout: established the lifelong tension between the symbol others required and the man he privately was; made him the custodian of an iconography he would later have to *defend from his own employer* (Nomad; The Captain; Civil War). The first acceptance contained the seeds of every later refusal.
5. **The choice to take Bucky as partner.** Fallout: the most-regretted decision of his


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

*Spanning, in subjective time, from the post-thaw integration into the Avengers (Avengers #4, 1964 calendar) through the conclusion of the Brubaker tenure and the Heroic Age (~2011 publication); in subject-subjective years, roughly his second adult decade — the long middle in which the Captain America identity is tested, broken, resumed, killed, and returned. This is the bulk of the longitudinal record and the period in which every behavioral pattern catalogued in the Workup either crystallized or was first stress-tested to failure.*

---

## I. Re-Entry: The Avengers Integration (Avengers #4 → ~ Avengers #16)

The recovery from the North Atlantic ice by the Avengers (*Avengers* #4, March 1964) is, in file terms, the inciting event of the entire middle period. Every subsequent pattern is downstream of the *manner* of his waking: alone in a submersible, surrounded by strangers in unfamiliar costume, told the year, told the war was over, told Bucky was dead before he had finished registering that he himself was alive. Subject's first recorded act on regaining full consciousness is not to ask after his own status but to demand confirmation of Barnes's death — establishing the lifetime pattern (later catalogued under *isolation of affect / encysted grief*) of meeting personal catastrophe with operational questions.

Behavioral markers in the first weeks of integration (covered across *Avengers* #4–16 and elaborated retrospectively in Waid's *Man Out of Time*):

- **Insomnia, sleep-onset variety.** Wakes repeatedly; multiple writers depict him fully dressed at 0400, walking. Establishes the lifetime pattern of nocturnal ambulation that surveillance would later catalogue as routine (Greenwich Village laps, the Battery, the Brooklyn Bridge walked to the Heights and back).
- **Compulsive cartographic re-orientation.** Spends days walking the Lower East Side and Brooklyn, noting which tenements still stand, which churches are gone, which corners are unrecognizable. Reads as ordinary culture-shock; functions as a private inventory of the dead — every block walked is a roll-call.
- **Refusal of psychiatric consult.** Stark's offer (then institutional, later personal) is declined politely and permanently. Pattern established: receives medical care for body, never for mind. This refusal will hold for fifty subjective years and will be one of the most stable behaviors in the file.
- **Immediate operational over-functioning.** Within weeks of thaw, he is leading the Avengers in tactical drills (*Avengers* #16, the "Old Order Changeth" issue, in which Stark, Thor, and the founders depart and leave Steve in command of Hawkeye, Pietro, and Wanda — three volatile, unproven assets, two with criminal pasts). Subject volunteers for the harder management problem. Sublimation is online from week one.

The "Kooky Quartet" period (*Avengers* #16–#34) is the first documented instance of his lifetime pattern of *taking responsibility for the unstable successor* — preview of Walker, of Bucky-as-Winter-Soldier, of Hydra-Steve's restored aftermath. He treats Hawkeye's insubordination as a training problem, not a character problem; he treats Wanda and Pietro's prior Brotherhood affiliation as a debt to be discharged, not a stigma to be borne. He runs them hard. They cohere. The pattern — *I will not discard a broken instrument; I will repair it* — is now in the record.

## II. The Identity Probes (Englehart, #153–#186, 1972–75)

Englehart's run constitutes the first systematic stress-test of the identity. Four discrete probes, each diagnostically informative:

**(a) The 1950s Captain America (#153–156, the "Bucky"-and-Cap of the McCarthy era).** A racist, paranoid double from the Korean-War interregnum returns. The narrative significance is doctrinal — the canon establishes that the name and uniform can be worn by someone who is none of him — but the *behavioral* significance is that Steve, on defeating the double, neither destroys the artifacts nor distances himself from them. He files the encounter and continues. First evidence that the identity is, for him, not a possession but a custodianship subject to challenge.

**(b) The Falcon partnership (#134 onward, intensifying through Englehart).** Subject voluntarily restructures the operational unit as a co-billed partnership with Sam Wilson — *Captain America and the Falcon*. This is the first peer-of-equals attachment formed after the loss of Barnes. Behavioral markers: he defers to Wilson on Harlem operational territory without performative ostentation; he is photographed (in-canon publicity) standing half a step behind Wilson at press appearances on Harlem turf; he refuses to take lead on civilian-community interactions in neighborhoods where Wilson has standing he does not. The pattern that will reappear in the Sam-Wilson-as-Captain-America succession is laid here, in his early forties subjective: he is *constitutionally* a yielder of stage.

**(c) The Nomad arc (#180–#184, 1974–75).** The "Secret Empire" arc resolves with a high-ranking conspirator — heavily implied to be a Nixon-analog — committing suicide in the Oval Office in front of him. Steve's response is to abandon the identity. He adopts the Nomad alias — *the man without a country* — and is photographed (in-canon) cutting his uniform into strips. This is the first documented decompensation event of the middle period. Diagnostic content: the breakage is **not** of his ethics, his work capacity, or his attachments — it is *specifically* of his ability to lend his body to a symbol he has just watched corrupt itself at the highest level. The Nomad period is brief (four issues) and ends when a young man named Roscoe Simons takes up the Captain America identity in his absence and is murdered by the Red Skull. The body is left posed for him to find. Subject's recorded response: returns to the identity immediately, without further deliberation. The pattern is now visible to him and to the file: *he cannot abandon the function because his absence kills people who try to fill it.*

**(d) The first Red Skull post-thaw confrontation (#185–186).** Establishes the lifetime adversarial pattern: Schmidt is the only adversary against whom Steve's restraint reliably fails. Multiple writers will return to this. The reaction is not rage — it is a flat, calibrated, total intent to end the threat. He does not banter with the Skull. He does not negotiate. The file should note Schmidt as the *single* named individual against whom Subject's standard de-escalation algorithms are inoperative.

## III. The Long Middle Stewardship (Stern–Byrne, #247–255, 1980–81; DeMatteis #261–300, 1981–84)

**Stern–Byrne (#247–#255).** Brief but disproportionately load-bearing for the file because Stern systematizes the *off-duty* life that surveillance work depends on. From #247 onward subject is documented as:

- Living in a Brooklyn Heights brownstone, ground-floor apartment, landlord Anna Kapplebaum (Auschwitz survivor) — the choice of building is not incidental; subject selects landlords and neighbors who are also custodians of an unmetabolized 20th-century memory.
- Working as a commercial illustrator (freelance, comics and storyboards) under his own name — a return to his pre-war vocational training at the Art Students League, which Stern reinstates as canon. The artwork is functional, disciplined, draftsmanlike; he draws every morning for at least an hour; the studio corner of the apartment is the only space in the residence that is *for him* rather than for the work.
- Maintaining a circle of civilian friends (Bernie Rosenthal — attorney, eventual fiancée; Josh Cooper; Mike Farrell) of whom he conceals the identity for as long as practicable, and to whom he discloses it only under operational compulsion. Pattern: the civilian life is real, but it is also a quarantine zone from which the work is excluded as long as possible.
- Romantic engagement to Rosenthal (Stern, #270s, formalized later under Gruenwald). Subject's first publicly-acknowledged adult romantic attachment of the middle period. The engagement does not survive — the work is incompatible with the life — but the *manner* of its dissolution (mutual, sad, no recrimination, sustained friendship) is itself diagnostic of his interpersonal pattern: he does not burn bridges, he is left on them.

**The Dire Wraith / Deathlok / Mr. Hyde / Baron Blood incursions (Stern–Byrne).** Operationally unremarkable; behaviorally notable for Baron Blood (#253–254), in which subject decapitates a vampire in occupied Falsworth Manor in the presence of the elderly Union Jack who can no longer wield his weapon. Subject does not flinch and does not gloat. Behavioral significance: the *cleanness* of his lethality when lethality is the answer. He is not a man who refuses to kill; he is a man who refuses to kill *unnecessarily*. The distinction is load-bearing for the entire ethical profile and will be repeatedly misread by adversaries who mistake his restraint for inhibition.

**DeMatteis (#261–300).** Adds *interiority* to the file: extended caption-text, dream sequences, the Mother Superior/Sin arc, and the introspective Bucky-grief setpieces. DeMatteis is the first writer to depict subject *crying* — in private, briefly, never publicly — over Barnes. Two such instances in the run; both occur alone, both are followed by immediate resumption of operational tempo, both end with subject washing his face and going out on patrol. The pattern is now codifiable as: **grief is permitted in solitude, in tightly bounded episodes, and is always closed by a return to function.** No interlocutor is ever admitted to these episodes during the middle period.

## IV. The Gruenwald Decade (#307–#443, 1985–96) — The Diagnostic Heart

Gruenwald's 137-issue tenure is the single richest behavioral corpus in the file. It must be treated in subsections.

### IV.a — The Serpent Society / Diamondback (#310 onward)

Operationally a rogues-gallery arc; behaviorally significant for the Rachel Leighton / Diamondback subplot. Diamondback is a low-tier supervillain (Serpent Society throwing-knives specialist) who develops a sustained romantic fixation on subject. He responds with — diagnostically characteristic — courteous wariness, then with measured engagement once she demonstrates good-faith defection, then with affectionate-but-bounded partnership that never crosses into the level of intimacy he reserved for Peggy or Bernie. File reads: he is **attracted to risk-matched partners** (women in dangerous work) because they are the only ones to whom he does not have to translate his life, but he is **also unable to let the relationship deepen** to a level that would require him to surrender operational interiority. Diamondback is held at the affectionate-but-bounded distance for the remainder of the run.

### IV.b — The Captain (Gruenwald, #332–#350, 1987–88) — The Second Identity-Break

The Commission on Superhuman Activities ruling: Steve Rogers's identity, costume, shield, and name are property of the United States government as a result of his enlistment status. He is ordered to resume active military command-and-control or surrender the uniform. Subject's response (#332) is to surrender — calmly, on the record, without theatre — and walk out in civilian clothes. He retains the body, the training, the moral commitment, and not a single accoutrement.

Behavioral content of the surrender scene (file-relevant):

- He does not argue the legal point. He concedes it. The conscience of the decision is private; the formality is observed.
- He folds the uniform himself before handing it over. He does not throw it, drop it, or refuse to handle it. The artifact is treated with respect even at the moment of its loss.
- He shakes the hands of the panel members on departure. The relational ethics override the political grievance, as they always do under stress short of catastrophe.

The subsequent *The Captain* identity (black-and-red uniform, photonic shield supplied by Stark, partnership with Bucky's-replacement Lemar Hoskins / Battlestar and later with the surviving original Falcon and Nomad-II) is the second decompensation-and-recovery cycle of the middle period. Diagnostically: he proves to himself, and to the file, that *the identity travels with the man and not the uniform.* This is the empirical foundation of his eventual willingness to pass the shield to Wilson and later to Barnes — the demonstration that the function survives the symbol.

### IV.c — The John Walker / U.S.Agent Arc (#332–#350, contemporaneous)

Walker, the Commission's replacement, is a psychologically unstable Vietnam-generation-displaced super-soldier who, under stress (parents murdered by Watchdogs), commits a string of atrocities under the Captain America iconography. He is everything Steve fears latent in the symbol — patriotism without conscience, force without restraint, grief weaponized.

Subject's behavior toward Walker is the diagnostic centerpiece of the period:

- Defeats Walker in single combat (#350) without permanent injury.
- *Declines* the reinstated identity until Walker is alive, recovered, and adequately psychiatrically supported.
- Personally trains Walker in restraint-based combat tactics in the months that follow.
- Hands Walker the original red-white-blue shield as a gesture of repair (the photonic shield Steve has been using is, in turn, retired).
- Advocates institutionally for Walker's transition to the U.S.Agent role — a survivable second-life in the work.

The pattern documented in the Workup (*takes responsibility for the unstable successor*) is here at its purest. Walker is the man who tried to replace him, defamed the symbol, and would have killed him given the operational opportunity. Steve repairs him. This is not magnanimity-as-display; there is no audience for most of the rehabilitation work. It is the behavioral correlate of the operative self-concept: *the custodian of a promise to Erskine does not discard a damaged instrument that another custodian might yet salvage.*

### IV.d — Streets of Poison (#372–#378, 1990) — The Phobic-Grade Pharmacological Event

Subject is exposed, non-consensually, to "Ice" (designer crystalline stimulant) through serum-contaminated battlefield injuries during a Kingpin/Crossbones/Bullseye street-war arc. The serum metabolizes the substance unevenly; for several days his inhibition is impaired. Documented behavioral changes during the period:

- Disproportionate physical force in routine arrests (broken jaws, fractured orbits in subjects who would normally have been restrained without injury).
- Open hostility, including verbal, to allies (Diamondback, Black Widow, Falcon).
- A confrontation with Daredevil in which he is *talked down* — diagnostically significant: Murdock, who is sensorially attuned to physiological state, reads him as chemically compromised, and Steve, in a window of self-recognition, does not deny it.
- A self-initiated full blood transfusion (Avengers medical) to purge the serum-substrate of the contaminant — accepts the temporary loss of the super-soldier serum *itself* as the cost of regaining inhibition. He chooses to be the sick boy from Brooklyn rather than the uninhibited enhanced soldier. The serum re-establishes itself in the months that follow.

The post-arc behavioral residue is the file's most stable phobic profile: lifetime refusal of caffeine, of analgesics beyond operational necessity, of any centrally-acting agent. He will accept lidocaine for field-suturing but not opioids for the recovery. The phobic content is not the drug — it is the *not recognizing himself* under the drug. Pair this with the later Hydra-Steve restoration response (Section VIII) and the file has a clean longitudinal pattern: **loss of executive self-recognition is the single condition he cannot tolerate.**

### IV.e — Operation: Galactic Storm (1992)

Avengers-wide crossover; behaviorally significant because subject is on the losing side of the council vote to execute the Kree Supreme Intelligence. The execution proceeds without him. He breaks publicly with Avengers leadership over the act — the first documented occasion in the middle period on which he loses a *procedural* vote in his own house and refuses to ratify the outcome. Pattern documented: *democratic submission ends at the point of the unconstrained taking of life by a body to which he has lent his name.* He is reconciled to the Avengers later but the rift is real and is the operational template for his Civil War posture fifteen subjective years later.

### IV.f — Capwolf, Bloodstone Hunt, Superia — Operational Background

These arcs are file-light. Note only: subject performs reliably under conditions of bodily transformation (wolf physiology, #402–408) without identity destabilization. The self is portable across morphology. Pair with Section IV.d (chemical destabilization, which *does* destabilize identity) and the file has its discriminator: **biology is not the threat to selfhood; loss of inhibitory control is.**

### IV.g — Fighting Chance / Serum Breakdown (#425–#443, 1993–96)

The serum begins to fail under the cumulative load of decades of combat. Subject is progressively paralyzed over the arc. His response is operationally instructive: he conceals the deterioration from his unit for as long as possible, fights from increasingly disadvantaged postures (powered armor; eventually a wheelchair), and ultimately accepts complete paralysis without behavioral disorganization. He plans the work he can still do — strategic, mentoring, institutional — from the chair. The arc ends with a transfusion from the Red Skull (himself wearing a cloned Rogers body) restoring the serum.

File significance: subject's identity is recognizable, intact, and continuous across the entire degradation. The Workup's claim that *the serum amplified but did not constitute him* is empirically demonstrated here. The behavioral fingerprint is identical at peak serum, at 1940s baseline, and at full paralysis. He is the same man across all three biologies.

## V. The Heroes Reborn Interregnum (1996–97)
