The militarized-aggression profile of long-service special operators (instrumental violence comfortable to self) is absent; violence is consistently effortful, narrowly applied, and followed by withdrawal rather than appetite.

### 6.4 Not Depressive Personality Disorder / Not Persistent Depressive Disorder

Baseline mood is engaged, warm, dryly humorous — particularly in close-quarters team contexts (the Avengers training-room banter across multiple eras; the gentle teasing of Sam; the easy affection with Natasha where written). The depression is reactive and episodic, not characterological. Persistent Depressive Disorder requires depressed mood more days than not for two years; Subject does not meet this between MDD episodes.

### 6.5 Not Dissociative Identity Disorder, Not Other Specified Dissociative Disorder

Despite extreme dislocations (ice, Dimension Z, Reborn temporal displacement, de-serumed reversion, Hydra-Steve restoration), **identity is continuous**. He reliably returns to the same self with the same values, attachments, and procedural memory. Dissociative micro-phenomena (the grief-trigger absences described above) are sub-syndromal and PTSD-attributable. The Hydra-Steve incident is *not* dissoci


## Behavioral Atlas

# BEHAVIORAL ATLAS — SUBJECT: ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT

*Compiled from longitudinal observation across the published behavioral record (1941–present). Tells are catalogued by state; each is keyed to canonical exemplars where possible. The Atlas assumes the reader has the Analytical Workup in hand and proceeds directly to granular pattern.*

---

## I. BASELINE — THE CALM-STATE FILE

The first thing to understand about the subject is that his calm is not the absence of activity; it is a load-bearing operational posture maintained at considerable metabolic cost. He is never *off*. What looks like rest is reduced gain.

**Posture and somatics at rest.** Sits forward more often than back; weight distributed evenly across both feet when standing, even in social contexts where most subjects shift to one hip. Hands tend to rest open on the thighs or loosely clasped rather than crossed; arms-crossed posture appears only when cold, bored, or quietly disapproving — and the disapproval variant is distinguishable by a slight downward angle of the chin. Shoulders sit lower than the public iconography suggests; he carries no visible chronic tension in the trapezius. The breath pattern at baseline is slow diaphragmatic — a residual asthmatic's discipline retained long after the asthma was cured (see pre-serum medical record, *Captain America* vol. 5 flashbacks; the breath-control habit is depicted explicitly in Waid's *Man Out of Time* #2 sketching sequence).

**Spatial habits.** Enters every room and immediately performs an unconscious sweep: exits, sightlines, civilians, hard cover, elevated positions, the location of every other enhanced or armed individual present. This sweep takes under two seconds and is invisible to most observers; it is depicted in Brubaker's #6 (the diner scene) and Hickman's *Avengers* throughout. He sits with his back to a wall whenever possible — not from paranoia but from the comfort of not needing to think about it. In an Avengers conference room he gravitates to the seat that puts the door in his peripheral vision and the windows behind him.

**Gaze.** Direct, level, slightly longer-than-average duration of eye contact, but without the dominance-claim quality such gaze usually carries; it reads as *attention* rather than *assessment*. He looks at the person speaking, then briefly at whoever they are speaking *about* or *to*, then back. He almost never looks at his own hands while speaking. When listening to bad news he looks slightly down and to the left, jaw set, before raising his eyes to respond.

**Voice and speech.** A baritone with Brooklyn vowels that surface under fatigue, emotion, or in conversation with anyone from the original era; he has consciously softened his accent in formal settings since the 1960s (a Mark Waid tell). Cadence is measured, sentences complete, mid-length; he uses fewer filler words than the people around him and tolerates longer pauses than they do. The pauses are diagnostic: he is the only person in most rooms who is willing to let a silence sit while he finishes thinking. Hickman in particular writes the tactic — Steve is repeatedly the last to speak in *Avengers* and *New Avengers* strategy sessions, and the silence before he does is itself a behavioral act.

**Idiomatic verbal habits.** Says *son* and *ma'am* and *sir* without irony, including to people younger than his apparent age; says *language* when irritated rather than when offended (a Whedon caricature that the comic-canon Steve actually does in a milder form — he registers profanity, files it, and lets it shape his read of the speaker). Defaults to first-name address with allies (*Tony, Nat, Sam*), rank-and-surname with new military contacts, and full name with adversaries he respects (*James Buchanan Barnes* in a key Brubaker beat; *Mr. Stark* during the Civil War break). Uses *we* rather than *I* when describing successful operations and *I* rather than *we* when describing failures — a consistent ownership/credit asymmetry visible from Stern through Coates.

**Daily routine, inferred and depicted.** Wakes pre-dawn, between 0430 and 0515, without an alarm — a wartime habit he has not unlearned and has not tried to. First action is to drink water, not coffee (the latter has no effect on him and he has gone off it since Streets of Poison anyway). Runs long — the canonical D.C. Mall run depicted in Spencer and earlier in Brubaker's tie-ins is real: 8–12 miles at a pace civilians cannot match, used as moving meditation. Returns, showers cold (a habit traceable to the ice — he is comfortable in cold water in a way that reads, on close observation, as *familiarity* rather than masochism). Breakfast is large, simple, protein-heavy; metabolism demands roughly 5,000–8,000 kcal/day at baseline and he eats with the unembarrassed steady efficiency of someone who was once hungry.

Mid-morning to mid-afternoon: training, briefings, fieldwork, or — when none of those — drawing. The sketchbook is one of the most reliably documented private behaviors in the canon (Waid, Brubaker, Remender, Coates). He draws faces from memory; the recurring subjects are Sarah, Bucky, Peggy, Erskine, the Howlers, and — increasingly across the modern runs — the dead of more recent vintage. The sketchbook is a grief-processing instrument disguised as a hobby. He shows it to almost no one; Sam Wilson is one of the few who has seen it.

Evening: paperwork, reading (history, biography, the occasional novel — he is catching up on a century of fiction at a rate of roughly two books a week, dog-ears pages, writes in margins in small all-caps engineering print). Bed by 2230 unless on mission. Sleep is light, segmented, and interruptible; he does not appear to require the eight hours a baseline human would.

**Tells of contentment.** Subtle. The corners of the mouth lift without the lips parting. A slight slow exhale through the nose. He leans back — and the lean-back, rare for him, is the most reliable single index that he feels safe in a room. With intimates he will rest a forearm along the back of an adjacent chair, a gesture of casual physical inclusion he uses with Sam, Bucky-restored, and (historically) Tony. With Bucky specifically he reverts to a shoulder-bump greeting that predates the war.

---

## II. UNDER LOW-GRADE STRESS — THE WORKING DAY

Operational stress — the daily friction of being who he is — produces an increase in *control*, not a decrease. The signature is *quieting*.

**Speech narrows.** Sentences shorten. The Brooklyn vowels recede; the register tightens toward briefing-room formality. He stops volunteering information not directly responsive to the question. Humor goes dry and infrequent rather than disappearing entirely — a dry one-liner under stress is diagnostic *of* the stress, not its absence (Brubaker is particularly good at writing this).

**Movement economizes.** Gestures disappear almost entirely; hands settle. He stops pacing if he was pacing. The sweeping environmental check that takes two seconds at baseline takes less than one and recurs more often. He begins, almost imperceptibly, to position himself between any non-combatant present and the most likely threat vector — a tell he cannot suppress and seems unaware of.

**Decision habits.** Defers tactical decisions a beat longer than seems comfortable to subordinates, which produces in others the (correct) impression that he is actually thinking. Solicits dissent explicitly — *what am I missing* is a verbatim Hickman-era line and a real pattern. Takes the riskier role for himself by default; this is so consistent across writers that it must be recorded as a decision *habit* rather than a moral choice in any given instance.

**Sleep and recovery.** First thing degraded under sustained low-grade stress is the willingness to rest. He will skip meals before sleep and sleep before training; training is, for him, a form of regulation he will not surrender.

**Interpersonal tells.** With intimates he becomes harder to reach in proportion to how much he is carrying. Sam Wilson and Natasha Romanoff are the two characters in the canon most reliably depicted as *noticing* this and naming it (Brubaker, Remender, Spencer). His response when named is characteristic: a small rueful smile, an acknowledgement, and no actual disclosure. The acknowledgement is meant as the gift.

---

## III. UNDER ACUTE THREAT — COMBAT AND CRISIS

The combat profile is the most extensively documented and the most stable across writers.

**Pre-engagement.** Roughly thirty seconds before contact, breathing shifts to a deeper, slower diaphragmatic pattern (the asthmatic's discipline doing tactical work). Pupils visibly dilate. He goes *quieter*, not louder — the rallying-cry iconography is mostly post-action, not pre-. The shield is held in the relaxed two-point grip he was taught in 1941 and has never modified, though he changes carrying position depending on expected threat vector (low and forward for projectile, high and across for melee).

**Tactical decision tree.** First priority: locate and shelter non-combatants. This is not negotiable and overrides every other strategic consideration; adversaries who understand it (Zemo, Red Skull, occasionally Stark during Civil War) exploit it. Second: identify the highest-skill threat and engage personally. Third: orient the unit around that engagement so that less-experienced operators handle predictable secondary threats. Fourth: communicate sparely on comms — call signs, vectors, target priorities; no commentary, no narration.

**Movement.** Closes distance rather than holding it. He fights forward. When forced to retreat he does so with the unit between him and the threat, never the reverse. He absorbs blows that could be slipped, on the calculation — explicit in Gruenwald, implicit elsewhere — that he can take what others cannot. This is the behavior most commonly mistaken for suicidality and most decisively distinguished from it by context: he absorbs damage when doing so shortens others' exposure, never when doing so satisfies a private impulse.

**Use of the shield.** Defensive first, offensive second, communicative third. In a crowded melee the shield is used to redirect — to put an ally behind it, to deflect a projectile away from a civilian line — more often than to strike. The thrown shield is a precision tool; he tracks ricochets with a spatial mathematics no one else in the canon has demonstrated competence in (consistent across writers, central to the Stern–Byrne run). Throwing the shield is also, for him, a small act of trust — it leaves his hand and must come back; he throws it more often when surrounded by allies and almost never when alone among adversaries.

**Voice in combat.** Drops in pitch slightly, gains carrying volume without losing modulation. Commands are short, declarative, and addressed by name. *On your six. Get them out. Hold the line. Down.* Reassurances to civilians are different in register — slower, lower, with eye contact. He has, repeatedly, talked frightened children into walking out of fire zones without raising his voice.

**Pain response.** Minimal vocalization. Breath catches, jaw sets, the line of the mouth flattens. He continues whatever he was doing. When the injury is severe enough to require triage he reports it factually and late — *I took one in the side, it can wait* is the verbatim register (Brubaker, repeatedly). Medics across the canon describe him as a difficult patient because he understates.

**Post-engagement decompression.** Walks the perimeter once before standing down. Counts the unit physically — not by comm check but by laying eyes on each member. Sits, often on the ground rather than on furniture, while doing the after-action mental review. Removes the cowl or mask within five minutes of the all-clear, runs a hand back through the hair, exhales audibly. This is the only time his exhaustion is allowed to show, and it shows for roughly ninety seconds before being put away.

---

## IV. UNDER MORAL/INSTITUTIONAL PRESSURE

A separate stress class, and the one in which his most distinctive behavior emerges.

**Early signs of moral injury.** He goes formal. Calls superiors by rank. Asks for orders in writing. Begins documenting. Becomes, paradoxically, *more* compliant on procedural minutiae as the substantive disagreement grows — a tell visible from the Gruenwald Commission arc through Civil War. The hyper-compliance is not capitulation; it is the construction of a clean record against the moment he will refuse.

**The refusal itself.** Quiet. He does not shout it. The Civil War refusal (Millar #1–7) and the earlier Commission refusal (Gruenwald #332) are both characterized by a single short declarative sentence and the placing-down of an object — shield, badge, papers. He does not argue afterward. He leaves. The argument, if it comes, is the other party's to bring.

**Post-refusal behavior.** Withdraws to a small trusted unit, reorganizes around principle, and prepares to function without institutional cover. Across Nomad, The Captain, and Civil War the operational pattern is identical: minimal footprint, careful target selection, scrupulous avoidance of civilian harm, public visibility calibrated to keep the principle in view rather than the person. He does not enjoy this work — there is no evidence of insurgent romanticism in him — but he is unusually good at it because he carries no personal grievance that could be exploited to compromise it.

**Reconciliation behavior.** When institutions repair themselves he returns to them without recrimination and without victory-claiming. He does not say *I told you so*. He resumes function, and the resumption is itself the most pointed possible commentary, which he leaves the institution to read.

---

## V. INTIMATE AND DOMESTIC REGISTERS

The least-documented file, by design — he protects the intimate domain — and therefore the most diagnostically valuable.

**With Bucky (pre-fall, post-restoration).** The peer register, and the only one in which he is observably *played*. Trades wisecracks at speed; uses childhood nicknames; reverts to the Brooklyn cadence completely. Physically demonstrative in a way unique to this relationship — shoulder bumps, arm clasps, the occasional half-hug. He is the only person Steve will laugh out loud around without first checking whether laughter is appropriate. Post-Winter-Soldier the register is grieved-tender rather than easy; he watches Bucky for distress signs the way a parent watches a recovering child, and Bucky knows it and tolerates it.

**With Peggy Carter.** Across timelines and across her decline: formal, courtly, deeply attentive. He stands when she enters a room. He brings small specific gifts (a particular tea, a book she once mentioned wanting). In the care-facility visits (vol. 5 #14 and elsewhere) he sits at her bedside and tells her about his week as if she is following, regardless of whether she is. When she does not recognize him he introduces himself by his given name only — *Steve* — never by alias, never by rank. This is the most consistent grief-ritual in the canon.

**With Sharon Carter.** Adult, equal, slightly guarded — guarded *for her*, not from her. He is the partner who insists on the conversation about risk, who declines to romanticize the job, who is uncomfortable with the parallel to Peggy and refuses to lean on it. Their breaks are initiated by external trauma and his reconnection is tentative.

**With Sam Wilson.** The closest adult peer-friendship of the modern era. Sam is one of two or three people in front of whom Steve will admit fatigue without prompting. The register is warm, ribbing, mutual. Sam is also the person Steve hands the shield to and the only one he hands it to without reservation — a behavior that requires reading against decades of identity-as-debt to see how large a trust it is.

**With Natasha Romanoff.** Sibling-coded, low-disclosure-high-comprehension. They communicate in glances and shorthand. He does not lecture her; she does not perform for him. He has been observed (Brubaker, Remender) to relax fractionally in her presence in a way he relaxes for no one else outside of Bucky.

**With Tony Stark (pre-Civil War).** Genuine affection complicated by chronic worry. Steve watches Tony's drinking, watches his sleep, watches his health, and never names any of it — a protective opacity that Tony reads (correctly) as concern and (incorrectly, sometimes) as judgment. The collapse of the relationship is the most painful interpersonal event in the canon for him and the one he is least able to discuss.

**With successors and proteges (Rick Jones, Walker, Ian, Miles, Kate, Sam-as-Cap).** Patient, generous, slow to criticize, quick to credit. Will train past his own exhaustion. Will take blame for their errors publicly and address them privately. Maintains contact across years.

**Romantic register more broadly.** Slow to initiate, attentive when in, faithful to a fault, gracious in endings. He has never, in canon, ended a relationship out of boredom or for a more attractive option; the endings are always structural (her death, her departure, the work, the ice). He grieves them and does not replace them quickly.

**Physical intimacy tells.** Touch-cautious in public, touch-comfortable in private with established intimates. He is the partner who will sit with a hand at the small of the back, who will brush hair from a forehead, who notices a shiver and offers the jacket without comment. He sleeps on the side of the bed nearer the door.

---

## VI. GRIEF REGISTER

The most encysted and most diagnostically important state.

**Acute grief — first 24–72 hours after a loss.** He becomes operational. Tasks are identified and executed: notifications, arrangements, succession decisions. The face is composed; the voice is flat; the briefing-room register described in the workup is the surface. Sleep degrades immediately. Appetite holds (the metabolism demands it) but the eating becomes mechanical. He does not seek company and does not refuse it; people who arrive are accommodated without effusion.

**Subacute grief — the first weeks.** Sketchbook activity intensifies. Solo runs lengthen. He volunteers for more fieldwork. He visits places associated with the lost (a bench, a grave, a building). Conversation about the deceased is brief, factual, and reverent; he will share one specific memory and then change the subject. The flat affect when speaking of the loss is the *signal* of the grief, not its absence.

**Chronic grief — months to decades.** Sediments into ritual. Anniversaries are kept silently. The sketchbook contains the face. The work continues. He does not cry in public and rarely in private; the canonical depictions of him crying (Brubaker post-Bucky-revelation, certain Waid beats) are rare enough to be calendar-worthy and are characterized by a contained, almost apologetic quality — head bowed, single hand to the bridge of the nose, brief and quiet.

**Tells of unprocessed grief surfacing.** A particular silence mid-conversation, eyes unfocused for two or three seconds; a hand going to a specific object (Bucky's dog tags, Peggy's compass-photo in some continuities, the original 1941 shield); the Brooklyn accent rising unbidden; a reluctance to leave a room he has been sitting in. These are the leakages of the encysted affect and they are the most reliable indicator that he is not, in fact, as composed as he appears.

---

## VII. VICTORY

Underweighted in popular reading of him. He is bad at victory.

**Immediate post-victory behavior.** Credits the unit. Names specific contributions. Does not pose for the cameras and physically extricates himself from photo opportunities when he can (Coates is particularly good at writing the discomfort). Checks on the wounded, both sides. If there are detainees he insists on their humane handling and personally verifies it.

**Private response.** Subdued. He does not celebrate. The post-victory affect is closer to the post-funeral affect than to elation — he tallies cost. Across the canon he is repeatedly depicted alone after victories: on a rooftop, in a hangar, on a Helicarrier deck. The image is so consistent it is iconographic.

**Tells of genuine satisfaction.** Rare. A small private smile when someone he has mentored succeeds without him. The lean-back, in safe company. A specific Brooklyn-cadenced *atta boy* or *atta girl* under his breath. He is more pleased by others' competence than by his own.

---

## VIII. DEFEAT

The most behaviorally distinctive state and the one his adversaries study least and should study most.

**Tactical defeat (mission failure with survivors).** He absorbs the after-action review personally, names his own errors first and at length, names others' errors briefly or not at all, and rebuilds. There is no externalization. The unit is rebriefed; the next operation is planned; he sleeps less.

**Moral defeat (an outcome he could not prevent and considers a failure of principle).** Withdraws to solo work for a period — days to weeks. Long runs lengthen further. The sketchbook fills. He does not discuss the defeat. He returns when he has constructed a way to act forward that honors the failure rather than denying it. Civil War aftermath (his surrender on the courthouse steps and the long subsequent silence in the post-Brubaker books) is the canonical case.

**Personal defeat (loss of a loved one, betrayal by an intimate).** The grief register, deepened. He functions; he degrades quietly; he does not seek consolation.

**The single behavior that signals deep defeat.** He goes to a body of water. The Hudson, the Potomac, the North Atlantic in extremity. He sits, often for hours. Across writers from Englehart through Coates the water-sitting is so reliable a tell that surveillance with predictive intent should treat the appearance of him alone near water as a high-confidence indicator of internal crisis. The ice is the originating association and he has not, in eighty subjective years, broken the habit.

---

## IX. DECEPTION, INTERROGATION, AND CAPTIVITY

**As deceiver.** Bad at it, and knows it. Will execute deception under operational necessity (undercover infiltration, the Nomad and Captain identities, the Secret Avengers period) but the tells are visible to intimates: a fractional slowing of speech, a reluctance to elaborate, a preference for lies of omission over lies of commission. He will not lie to a child or to a person grieving, even tactically.

**As deceived.** Slow to suspect intimates and quick to suspect institutions. The asymmetry is exploited by the Hydra-Steve arc and by every adversary who has gotten close (Sin, Mother Night, the Red Skull). Once he suspects, he investigates rather than confronts, and the investigation is meticulous.

**Under interrogation.** Name, rank, serial number, and silence. Across multiple canonical captivities (AIM, Hydra, the post-Civil War detention) the pattern is the same: cooperative on procedural matters (eating offered food, accepting medical care if non-narcotic), silent on operational matters, courteous to individual captors, contemptuous of institutional ones. He does not bargain. He does not plead. He waits.

**Under torture.** The pain-response profile described above, sustained. He has, in canon, survived torture sessions that broke peers. The mechanism is not stoicism per se but the same load-bearing structure that organizes the rest of his life: the suffering is offered against the debt he believes he owes, and the debt is, for him, in


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS — SUBJECT: ROGERS, S.G.

*A week of continuous surveillance, reconstructed from canon and inferred at the granular resolution a long-term tail would yield. All times Eastern unless noted. Subject's residence of record across the modern era oscillates between a Brooklyn brownstone walk-up (Heights or the southern edge of DUMBO, depending on era), a small apartment near the Avengers facility upstate, and a barracks-grade quarters inside Avengers Tower / Mansion / Compound when on active rotation. The granular pattern is consistent across residences; the address is incidental.*

---

## 1. THE SHAPE OF A DAY

### 0430–0500 — Wake

Subject wakes without an alarm and has done so consistently across every run in which sleep is depicted (Englehart, Gruenwald, Waid, Brubaker, Remender, Coates). Two operational facts to note: (1) he does not require the alarm because his sleep is shallow — a hyperarousal residual consistent with the chronic-PTSD profile, harnessed rather than florid; (2) the wake time is anchored not to circadian preference but to *pre-dawn quiet*, which is the window in which he can move through the city without being recognized and without being asked anything.

First action on waking: he sits up, places both feet on the floor, and remains seated on the edge of the bed for roughly thirty to ninety seconds before standing. Surveillance would interpret this as orientation behavior — the small daily reconfirmation that the room around him is *this* room, *this* year, *this* life. After the ice, he reportedly woke for years confused about the decade (Waid, *Man Out of Time*); the seated pause is the vestige.

He makes the bed immediately. Hospital corners. This is invariant.

### 0500–0530 — Ablutions, hydration, the first sketch

Cold-water face wash. Shave with a safety razor and a brush — period-correct rather than affected; he has used the same model since the 1940s and replaces only blades. Teeth. He does not shower yet; the shower follows the run.

Hydration: approximately one liter of water, room temperature, drunk slowly while standing at the kitchen counter. He does not drink coffee. (Across Gruenwald and into modern runs the gag is recurrent — offered coffee, he accepts the cup for politeness and lets it cool; he does not metabolize caffeine usefully and has, since *Streets of Poison*, a phobic-grade aversion to ingested stimulants. He will accept tea — black, no additions — but rarely makes it for himself.)

Before the run, on the kitchen table or a small side desk, he opens a hardback sketchbook and works for ten to fifteen minutes. Subject matter is consistent: an object in the room (a chair, a window mullion, a hand), a face from memory (canonically: Sarah Rogers, Bucky, Peggy, occasionally Erskine), or a single architectural detail of the neighborhood. He works in graphite — 2B, sometimes a 4B for shadow — and never in color in the morning. The morning sketches are not for anyone. They are not posted, shown, or referenced. A surveillance team would, over months, be able to plot the cycle of which face is being drawn against the proximity of the relevant anniversary — Bucky's apparent death, Sarah's death, the date of his enlistment, V-E Day.

### 0530–0700 — Run

Route varies by residence but the structural pattern is invariant:

- A ground-eating pace that for a normal subject would constitute interval work and for him is roughly aerobic baseline (~5:30/mile sustained, ~4:40 when pushed; the metrics are inferred from feats depicted across Gruenwald and Brubaker).
- Eight to twelve miles depending on the day; longer on Sundays, shorter on days that include a scheduled mission brief.
- He passes the same landmarks at roughly the same minute on each circuit, which is operationally a security liability he accepts because the run has a ritual function he is unwilling to compromise. From Brooklyn the canonical loop touches the East River, the Brooklyn and Manhattan Bridges (he prefers the pedestrian deck of the Brooklyn Bridge eastbound at dawn — sun rising over the harbor), and returns via Atlantic Avenue or the Heights promenade. Upstate, he runs perimeter and the wooded fire roads.
- He nods at the same recurring runners and dog-walkers. He learns their names within two weeks. He notices when one is absent for more than three days and, if it persists, will quietly verify the person is alive (Gruenwald-era characterization: the neighborhood awareness is granular and unprompted).
- A surveillance tail would record that he does not wear earbuds. He runs to ambient sound: traffic, gulls, the river, the city waking. This is consistent across writers.
- He carries no phone for the run. He carries a folded twenty-dollar bill in an inner pocket — for someone else, not for himself.

Two recurring incidents are statistically near-certain in any given surveillance week: (a) he stops at least once to give directions or help someone with a stalled vehicle, a fallen bicycle, or a fallen person; (b) at least once he intervenes — briefly, almost unobtrusively — in something escalating (a shove, a raised voice, a young person being followed). He does not announce himself; he positions his body and the situation resolves. He resumes the run.

### 0700–0730 — Return, shower, dress

Shower hot, finishes cold. Forty seconds of cold at the end — invariant. He does not soak; he is in and out in under seven minutes.

Dress depends on the day. Civilian baseline: chinos or dark denim, plain T-shirt or henley, leather jacket (1940s-cut, restored and re-soled rather than replaced; he repairs rather than discards), brown lace-up boots, a watch on the left wrist that he winds every morning. He carries: a worn leather wallet (folded photographs inside — Peggy, Sarah, a Howling Commandos group shot — confirmed by multiple writers depicting him opening it), a small notebook (Moleskine-equivalent), a mechanical pencil, a folded handkerchief, and, since the late-Brubaker era, a phone he uses reluctantly. He does not carry a wedding ring. He does not carry a weapon when not on duty; the shield is the weapon and the shield stays where it belongs.

### 0730–0815 — Breakfast

Cooked, sat down, eaten at a table with utensils. He does not stand and eat. He does not eat in front of a screen. He does not skip.

Caloric load is substantial — inferred from the ~95% efficient metabolism of an enhanced 240-pound frame engaged in sustained aerobic and anaerobic work; canonically depicted as four to six thousand calories distributed across three meals plus snacks (the in-joke is consistent from Gruenwald through Aaron). Morning composition is consistent: eggs (four to six), some form of meat, oatmeal or bread, fruit, water. He cooks in a cast-iron pan he has owned since the early 1990s. He washes the pan immediately after eating. He puts it away.

While eating, he reads. *Print*. Three rotating items: a newspaper (he subscribes to at least one daily in physical form — depicted in Brubaker and Coates), a book (usually nonfiction — history, biography, civics; in modern runs we see him reading Coates's own essays, Baldwin, period military histories, and biographies of figures he has met or whose work he is trying to understand — including, notably, civil-rights-era figures he missed in real time and is methodically catching up on), and a single piece of correspondence (he answers letters by hand; volume varies; the pile is processed at roughly five per morning).

A surveillance team would note: he reads slowly. He does not skim. He marks margins with pencil.

---

## 2. THE WORKING DAY

### 0830–1200 — Operational block

Variable in content, invariant in structure. The morning block is the day's "work" block: training, briefing, mission, administrative correspondence, or — when none of those — community labor (canonically depicted across runs: he volunteers at a VA, he assists with neighborhood watch and youth programs, he donates physical labor to a local restoration project; the Coates and Spencer-era runs depict this most explicitly).

When on Avengers rotation: he arrives at briefing first. He brings coffee for someone else (the recurring gag — he doesn't drink it but he tracks the team's orders). He reviews briefing materials before the briefing rather than during. He lets others speak first; he speaks last (Hickman *Avengers* — established with surveillance-grade consistency). When he speaks, he speaks briefly. He does not repeat himself.

Training: solo block of approximately ninety minutes, then partnered work if a partner is available. Solo block is structured — shield drills (throw lines, ricochet geometries, recovery footwork), heavy bag, body-weight calisthenics, mat work. Partnered work is sparring, sometimes instructional. He coaches more than he competes. The training space, wherever it is, is left cleaner than he found it.

He takes no day off training. He takes lighter days. The streak — across canon — extends to "every day since 1941 he has not been comatose or frozen."

### 1200–1300 — Lunch

Eaten at a table. Cooked or sourced from one of three or four invariant local establishments (Brooklyn: a Polish deli, a diner that has known him since the eighties under one identity or another, a Lebanese counter near the courthouse, a Sicilian bakery for bread). He tips heavily. He learns the names of the staff and remembers the names of their children. He does not flirt with the women who work the counters, including the ones who flirt with him; he is courteous and slightly formal and they remember him fondly without expectation.

If lunch is solitary, he reads. If lunch is with the team, he listens more than he talks and asks the youngest member at the table something specific about their life.

### 1300–1700 — Second operational block

Mission, meetings, mentorship, or correspondence. The pattern of distribution across a typical seven-day week, inferred:

- Active mission/operational time: two to three days.
- Training and team-coordination: two days.
- Civic/community/personal: one to two days.
- Travel/transit consumes parts of nearly every day.

When there is nothing tactical to do, he does not invent tactical work. He goes to the VA, to the gym at a community center where he runs a free youth boxing class under a low-profile identity, to a hospital ward, or — and this is a recurring, surveillance-confirmable behavior — to a courtroom to sit in the gallery on a case involving a person without resources. He does not intervene. He sits, he listens, he leaves.

### 1700–1800 — The pivot

End of the working day is marked by a deliberate pivot, not a drift. He changes clothes if he hasn't already. He takes ten minutes alone — usually at a window or on a small balcony or fire escape — and does nothing visibly. A trained observer would recognize this as decompression; he would describe it, if asked, as "just thinking."

---

## 3. EVENINGS

### 1800–1930 — Dinner

The most variable block. Three modal patterns:

1. **Alone, cooked at home.** Same cast-iron pan, simple protein-and-vegetable, water. He plays music — vinyl when at the brownstone, a small Bluetooth speaker upstate; he has gradually built a contemporary musical literacy (Sam Wilson is repeatedly depicted as the curator) but the music he chooses for himself when alone tends to be from the 1930s and 40s, jazz standards and big band, with a few Marvin Gaye and Otis Redding records he was introduced to in the seventies and never let go of.
2. **With one other person.** Usually Sam, Bucky in eras when Bucky is reachable, Natasha when she was alive in continuity, Sharon, occasionally Tony in eras of détente. Conversation is unhurried. He does most of the listening. He asks follow-up questions that demonstrate he was listening to a conversation two months earlier.
3. **At a team table.** He sits where he can see the room's entrances. He notices who isn't eating. He refills others' water before his own.

### 1930–2200 — The quiet block

This is the diagnostically rich window — the period in which the public-facing function is suspended and the private behavior shows. Recurring activities, in rough order of frequency:

- **Drawing.** Longer-form than the morning sketch. Larger pad, often ink, sometimes watercolor for landscape work. He has a portfolio he has not shown anyone in current continuity; it would, on inventory, contain hundreds of pieces — portraits of the dead, streetscapes of a Brooklyn that no longer exists, architectural reconstructions of his mother's apartment building from memory, the geometry of his shield in different orientations, occasional still lifes. He gives away the small ones to neighbors and to kids; he keeps the portraits.
- **Reading.** Two to three hours on a typical evening. He keeps a list. He finishes books.
- **Correspondence.** He still writes letters. To Avengers veterans, to family members of the fallen (this is a recurring depiction across writers — he writes to widows and parents personally and continues to do so for decades; he remembers anniversaries), to a small circle of civilian friends, and to people who wrote to him whose letters merit reply.
- **Visiting Peggy** (in eras prior to her death) **or her grave** (after). The cadence is roughly weekly. He brings flowers — peonies in season, otherwise white roses — and stays approximately forty-five minutes. He talks to her. He does not perform the conversation for any imagined observer.
- **Visiting the VA.** Once or twice a week in the evenings, in addition to any daytime hours; he sits with patients, plays cards, listens. He is recognized by some and not by others; he does not correct either.
- **Watching film.** Sparing. Tends toward documentary and older cinema. He has methodically caught up on films of the postwar decades he missed; he keeps a list.
- **Sitting on the fire escape or stoop.** Doing nothing. Watching the street. The neighbors know him as a quiet man who nods.

He does not drink. (He cannot, productively — the serum forecloses it; the equanimity with which he carries this is striking given the cohort he came from.) He does not smoke. He has, in earlier continuity, kept a pipe of his father's on a shelf, unused.

### 2200–2300 — Pre-sleep

Slow wind-down. Dishes done. Kitchen reset for the morning. Boots by the door, oriented for fast wear. Shield, when at home and off-duty, on a wall mount or in a closet — not visible from the front room, but reachable in under three seconds from the bed (inferred from his depicted readiness in multiple writers; this is a tradecraft pattern, not paranoia, and he would describe it as habit).

Final hour: reading or writing in a smaller journal that is not the sketchbook. Daily ledger function — what happened, who he saw, what he owes them, what he failed to do. This is the closest he comes to a confessional practice; it is private and consistent.

Lights out approximately 2245–2315.

### 2315–0430 — Sleep

Five to six hours. Light. He wakes for any unfamiliar sound and is at full operational threshold within two seconds (depicted Brubaker, Remender). Nightmares: present but not nightly. Content, when depicted: the Zemo drone (Bucky falling), the ice, the courthouse steps (post-*Civil War*/*Reborn*), his mother's hospital room, his father at a doorway. He does not wake screaming. He wakes silent, sits up, places his feet on the floor, and stays there until the room reconfirms itself.

---

## 4. WEEKLY AND ANNUAL RITUALS

A surveillance file covering a year would isolate the following recurrences:

**Weekly.**
- Visit to Peggy / Peggy's grave. Day variable, frequency near-invariant.
- Long Sunday run (15–20 miles), followed by a longer breakfast, often with Sam or another single intimate.
- A youth class he teaches (boxing, in the canon's most consistent depiction) — same evening each week, same gym.
- A standing call or in-person visit with Bucky, in eras when Bucky is reachable. The cadence is religious.
- VA hours.
- Mass — variable. He was raised Catholic (Irish-immigrant mother); his current practice is sporadic and quiet. He attends more often around anniversaries and around losses. He sits at the back. He lights a candle. He does not take communion in most depictions, which an observer should note rather than over-interpret.

**Annual.**
- July 4. His birthday and the country's. He works it. He has never, in any depiction, taken the day off. He treats public celebration of the date as something to be endured politely.
- The anniversary of Bucky's apparent fall. A bad day; he is quieter, he runs longer, he draws Bucky's face that morning.
- The anniversary of Sarah Rogers's death. He visits her grave (Brooklyn, Catholic cemetery). He brings flowers. He stays.
- The anniversary of Erskine's death. He works. He does not mark it externally. The sketchbook will show it.
- V-E Day, V-J Day, D-Day, Pearl Harbor. He marks each privately. He will, depending on era and request, attend a public commemoration in uniform; he does not enjoy it.
- Peggy's birthday and the anniversary of her death. Flowers. A long visit. A long evening alone afterward.
- Thanksgiving and Christmas: spent with whichever surrogate family is available — Avengers, Wilson family, Barnes when reachable. He cooks. He gives small, handmade or considered gifts. He receives gifts with visible discomfort.

---

## 5. HABITS OF PLACE

He walks the same streets. He sits in the same booths. He uses the same pew. The pattern is not sentimentality and not OCPD; it is a man who, having been ripped out of his time once, is unwilling to be casually ripped out of his place. The neighborhood is the corrective.

Granular place-attachments a surveillance team would map:

- A particular bench on the Brooklyn Heights Promenade with a sightline to the lower Manhattan skyline. He sits there at least weekly.
- A particular pew, back left, in a particular church.
- A particular booth in a particular diner; the staff hold it informally.
- A particular grave in a particular cemetery; a second grave nearby (his mother's) visited on the same trip.
- A particular stretch of the East River where he sometimes stands for ten or fifteen minutes looking at nothing.
- The roof of whichever building he currently sleeps in. He goes up. He sits. He comes down.

He tips in cash, generously, and consistently. He remembers staff turnover. He writes thank-you notes to people who have done him small services. He returns borrowed objects cleaned and in better condition than he received them.

---

## 6. WHAT A WEEK OF SURVEILLANCE WOULD ACTUALLY RECORD

Distilled to the form a tail would file:

- **0430 wake, no alarm, every day. 0500 cold water. 0530 run, 8–20 miles, no audio, ground-eating pace, predictable route, predictable detours to help strangers.** Operational liability accepted by subject for ritual value.
- **0730 cooked breakfast, sat down, read print media. Subject does not eat standing.**
- **Working block 0830–1700 with a fifteen-to-thirty-minute decompression at 1700 before he will engage another human as a private person.**
- **No alcohol. No caffeine. No tobacco. No pharmacological mood agents of any kind. Phobic-grade aversion to intoxicants; do not attempt soft approaches that depend on this.**
- **Cash tipping, hand-written correspondence, physical books, vinyl records at home. Phone use reluctant and minimal. Digital footprint thin by modern standards but not absent.**
- **Two intimate categories of contact: a very small circle (Wilson, Barnes, Carter, the surviving Avengers core) seen weekly to monthly in person; a much wider correspondence circle (veterans, families of the fallen, mentees) maintained by letter and brief visit.**
- **One religious practice (Catholic, attenuated, private). One artistic practice (drawing, daily, private). One commemorative practice (graveside visits, regular, private).**
- **Sleep light, short, vigilance-residual. Wakes silent. Reorients before standing.**
- **Predictable on space; less predictable on tactics. He can be found. He cannot be ambushed.**
- **No vice usable for leverage. No financial impropriety. No sexual indiscretion. No private cruelty to staff or subordinates. Surveillance over months yields a file that grows in detail without yielding a single piece of compromising material — which is itself the diagnostic finding.**

The week, viewed as data, reads as the externalization of the personality structure: low entropy, high consistency, generous toward the immediate, opaque toward the institutional, calibrated around a small number of deeply held private observances none of which he discusses. The routine is not rigidity. It is the load-bearing scaffolding by which a man with a permanent, unmetabolized grief and a permanent vocational debt continues, every morning before dawn, to get up.

— End Section.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Dossier section. Subject: ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT. All relational findings derived from the canonical record and triangulated against the behavioral substrate established in the Analytical Workup. This section maps the small, dense constellation of origin bonds — biological family, the dead who function as family, and the few surrogate-paternal figures whose imprint on the subject is load-bearing. Romantic partners, peers (Barnes, Wilson, Stark, Romanoff), and successors (Walker, Ian, the broader mentee web) are mapped in subsequent sections.*

*Methodological note: Rogers's origin-family corpus is small in personnel but disproportionately heavy in psychic weight. The file therefore goes deep on five figures, two of whom (Sarah Rogers, Abraham Erskine) appear in canon for fewer than thirty total pages across eighty years of publication and yet are referenced, invoked, or behaviorally enacted by the subject in nearly every long-form run. The disproportion is itself the finding.*

---

## 1. SARAH ROGERS — Mother (deceased, late 1930s)

**History.** Irish immigrant, nurse, widowed early. Canonical references thin but consistent: *Captain America* vol. 1 #225, #247, #255; *Captain America: Man Out of Time* #1; Brubaker flashbacks in vol. 5 #50; Waid's *Sentinel of Liberty* riffs; the Brubaker/Epting "What If Steve Rogers Hadn't Been Frozen" tonal echo. She worked in a tuberculosis ward; she contracted the disease she nursed; she died of it while Steve was in late adolescence, leaving him alone in a tenement apartment with the funeral debt and a small drafting portfolio. The cause of death is not incidental: she died from doing the work — from absorbing the harm she was paid to mitigate. The subject has been re-enacting that occupational logic for ninety calendar years.

**Dynamics.** What the record shows when Steve mentions her (rare, usually pried out — Waid extracts a paragraph; Brubaker extracts two): a register that is not grief-as-pain but grief-as-orientation. He speaks of her in present-tense ethical formulations ("My mother used to say…") rather than past-tense narrative. Her teachings — the "you stand up because it's the right thing, not because you'll win" formulation, repeatedly attributed to her or to Erskine depending on the run — function as a portable internalized object. He carries her, in the technical sense: she is the moral interlocutor in his head when no one else is.

**Evolution.** Her presence in his life *grew* after her death and grew again after the ice. As a boy he had a sick mother; as a young man he had a memory; as a man out of time he has a principle. Each ontological diminution of her actual person has been compensated by an elaboration of her function. By the modern runs (Coates, Aaron), when Steve invokes "where I come from" he is invoking her — a single working-class Irish woman in a Lower East Side walk-up — far more than he is invoking the country whose flag he wears.

**Trust/safety.** She was the only person in his pre-serum life who reliably did not hurt him. Joseph hurt him; the neighborhood boys hurt him; his body hurt him; institutions ignored him. Sarah was the original safe object, and her safety was *conditional on her labor* — she was safe because she was working, exhausted, giving. The subject's adult template for love as caretaking-under-strain is downstream of this. He does not know how to be loved by someone who is not also sacrificing.

**Failure mode.** That he could not save her. The pre-serum boy watched his mother die of a disease he could not buy treatment for, in an era and an economic class where he could not have done so even as an adult. The asymmetry — her dying *for* the vulnerable, him unable to prevent it — is the prototype of every subsequent loss, including Bucky and Erskine. Each is, structurally, his mother again: a good person consumed by their own goodness while he, smaller than the situation, fails to intervene in time.

**Leverage.** Operationally significant. Invocation of Sarah Rogers by name is rare and, when achieved by an interlocutor who has earned it (Peggy did; Bucky did; no adversary ever has), produces a brief register of unguarded affect that is otherwise unavailable. By a hostile actor it would not produce manipulability — Steve does not negotiate with adversaries — but it would produce *flinch*, the only one in his behavioral repertoire. The Red Skull has used it at least once (Gruenwald-era taunt) and Steve's response was a controlled lethality not seen in him otherwise.

---

## 2. JOSEPH ROGERS — Father (deceased, Steve's early childhood through pre-adolescence depending on continuity)

**History.** WWI veteran, alcoholic, died young — variously of cirrhosis, mustard-gas sequelae, or combat-residual injury depending on the writer. Waid and Brubaker emphasize him most: a damaged man who came back from one war and brought it home into a small apartment with a sick wife and a smaller, sicker son. The domestic violence is canonical in Brubaker's reconstruction and implicit elsewhere; the alcoholism is canonical across runs. Joseph dies before Sarah, leaving her to nurse him through the dying and then nurse strangers through the dying that killed her.

**Dynamics.** This is the *negative template* — the figure Steve has organized his entire adult identity against. Three behavioral structures in the adult subject are directly traceable to Joseph:

1. *The alcohol refusal.* Steve cannot get pharmacologically drunk (serum), but the relevant fact is that he doesn't try, doesn't keep liquor, and is visibly uncomfortable around heavy-drinking veterans (the Howlers in flashback notwithstanding — he tolerated it in others, never adopted it). The Streets of Poison aftermath cements this into phobic territory. He has watched, from inside the apartment and from inside his own bloodstream, what disinhibition does to a man with combat in him.
2. *The anger-monitoring.* Steve's most consistent metacognitive practice is the surveillance of his own anger. He flags it, names it, throttles it. He has stated across multiple writers (Gruenwald, Brubaker, Coates) some variant of the formulation that the line between him and the men he fights is one decision wide. The man on the other side of that decision is Joseph.
3. *The intolerance of bystanding while harm is done in his vicinity.* The originating image — small Steve unable to interpose his body between his father and his mother — recurs structurally in every confrontation Steve has with state, institutional, or interpersonal violence done to non-combatants. The Civil War surrender is, read through this lens, the small boy finally large enough to stop the fight by stepping into the middle of it.

**Evolution.** Joseph is the figure Steve has spent the most life-time *not talking about*. References are oblique, late, and given grudgingly to writers who earn them (Brubaker again). Where Sarah is invoked as living principle, Joseph is invoked, when at all, as cautionary diagnosis — *the kind of man I will not allow myself to become*. There is no observed forgiveness arc, no on-panel reconciliation-in-memory, no graveside scene. Joseph is the only figure in the subject's emotional roster whose memory he has, by all evidence, refused to repair.

**Trust/safety.** None established in life. Joseph was the originating unsafe object: the man whose footsteps in the hall reorganized the small boy's body. The somatic vigilance Steve carries — light sleep, room-scanning, the documented ability to track an entrance from across a room without looking at it (Hickman *Avengers*) — is downstream, in part, of this. War refined it; Joseph installed it.

**Failure mode.** Steve's failure mode with Joseph is not active grief but *organized refusal*. The cost is that one zone of his interior is sealed and unprocessed — the zone that contains the question of whether he hated his father, whether his father loved him, whether his father was also a victim of the same war Steve later glorified. He has not asked these questions on panel in eighty years of publication. The unasked question is itself a behavioral signature.

**Leverage.** The most dangerous file in this dossier. Any actor who could credibly link Steve's iconography to Joseph's pathology — *you are your father with a shield* — would land harder than any other psychological strike available. The Hydra-Steve arc (Spencer) functions, read structurally, as exactly this attack: an externalized rendering of the worst version of him, made canonical. His recovery from it — grief and accountability for acts he did not commit — is the cleanest evidence in the entire corpus that the Joseph-fear is still live and still being managed. An adversary with this leverage and the patience to use it slowly would not break him but would extract more cost from him than any other attack vector.

---

## 3. ABRAHAM ERSKINE — Surrogate Father / Transferential Imprint (deceased, 1941, within hours of subject's enhancement)

**History.** *Tales of Suspense* #63; *Captain America* vol. 1 #255 (Stern/Byrne, the definitive treatment); echoes across Waid, Brubaker, Coates. Refugee German biochemist; selected Steve on moral criteria explicitly disjunct from physical ones; performed the procedure; was assassinated by a Nazi infiltrator within the same hour, in front of the subject, before any second candidate could be enhanced. Steve's first independent post-serum act was to chase down and kill the assassin.

**Dynamics.** Erskine is the figure whose death *installed the vocation*. The bond, in clock-time, was hours; in psychic-time, it is the longest relationship in the subject's life, because Erskine has never stopped speaking inside him. The mechanism is classical transferential father-substitution: Erskine arrives at the exact developmental moment Steve needs a non-violent, morally legible paternal figure, offers him a contingent gift (the serum is described as a *trust*, not a *reward*), and dies before the relationship can be tested by ordinary friction. He is therefore preserved at maximum idealization, with no contaminating evidence.

The operative line — "*stay who you are: not a perfect soldier, but a good man*," in its various recensions — is the subject's load-bearing introject. He recites it (Waid), enacts it (every refusal to escalate, every yielded command), and tests himself against it (the Capwolf and Streets of Poison arcs, where he loses the *who you are* and recovers it). It is the closest thing the subject has to a sacred text.

**Evolution.** Erskine's function has *intensified* over decades, not decayed. As Steve's other origin figures have become more distant (Sarah further in the past; Bucky returned-altered; Peggy lost; cohort gone), the Erskine introject has expanded to fill the available space. By the modern runs he is invoked less by name and more by function: Steve will turn to a moral question, pause, and answer it in a register that is recognizably Erskine's — slow, accented in cadence, principled, slightly sad.

**Trust/safety.** The only intact paternal trust in the subject's record. Erskine never disappointed him because he had no time to. This is a structural fact, not a sentimental one, and Steve appears to know it — there is a wistful, slightly ironic register when he speaks of Erskine in the later runs that suggests awareness that the relationship is preserved by its incompleteness.

**Failure mode.** That Steve could not stop the assassin in the room. The original *I should have been faster* — installed within minutes of becoming fast enough to be faster than anyone else — is the prototype of every subsequent survivor-guilt episode. Bucky on the drone is Erskine in the lab is Sarah in the ward. The pattern was set in 1941, in a single room, in approximately ninety seconds.

**Leverage.** Almost none, because the figure is dead and idealized and any attempt to weaponize him would be detected as desecration. The one exploit available — and Zemo and the Red Skull have both tried it — is the suggestion that Erskine *would not approve* of some current action of Steve's. The subject's response to this attack is unusually severe: a quiet, asymmetric escalation. He does not argue the point; he removes the speaker from the field.

---

## 4. JAMES BUCHANAN "BUCKY" BARNES — De Facto Brother

*Mapped at length in Relationships II (Peer & Combat Bonds); brief origin-frame note here, because Bucky functions in the subject's emotional architecture as the only sibling he has ever had and is therefore an origin-family figure as well as a peer.*

**Origin-family function.** Steve was an only child. Bucky, across all reconstructions (golden-age sidekick, Brubaker covert-asset retcon, modern), is the brother-position in the subject's psyche: shared childhood-or-near-childhood (the retcon places them as Brooklyn-adjacent and pre-war intimate), shared formative loss (each is the other's primary witness through the war), and the unique status of having known Steve *before and after* the serum without the relationship reorganizing around it. Bucky is the only person in the canon who has seen the subject small, sick, and losing alley fights, and treated the enhanced version as continuous with that boy rather than as a replacement of him. This is irreplaceable and the subject knows it.

**Trust/safety, leverage, failure mode.** Detailed in Relationships II. Origin-frame note only: the loss of Bucky on the drone in 1945 is the wound around which the entire postwar identity is organized, and Bucky's return as the Winter Soldier did not heal it — it *complicated* it, in the technical clinical sense (Pop-Jordanova 2021, cited in clinical_provenance), by introducing six decades of additional guilt-content (Bucky alive, used, suffering, while Steve slept) that has to be metabolized alongside the original loss. The subject's response — taking custody of Bucky, training him, advocating for him, eventually passing him the shield — is the cleanest behavioral evidence in the file that Steve repairs rather than discards, and that the brother-bond is, for him, structurally non-severable.

---

## 5. IAN ROGERS (LEOPOLD ZOLA) — Adopted Son (*Captain America*, Remender, *Dimension Z*, 2012–2014)

**History.** Infant rescued by Steve during the Dimension Z arc; raised by him alone, in hostile territory, across approximately eleven subjective years while only minutes passed on Earth. Biological progeny of Arnim Zola. Steve names him Ian, after his father (the only on-panel softening toward Joseph in the entire canon — diagnostically significant; see §2 above). Returns to Earth; Ian is largely written out of subsequent runs, with intermittent reappearances.

**Dynamics.** The Dimension Z arc is the only sustained on-panel evidence of Steve in the parental role, and the evidence is unambiguous: he is a capable, attentive, structured, affectionate father, organized around the child's safety and moral formation rather than around achievement or replication of himself. He teaches Ian to fight only insofar as Ian needs to survive, teaches him to read, teaches him an ethical vocabulary, and — critically — does not lie to him about who his biological father is or about the cost of their situation. The parenting is recognizably *Sarah's parenting*, scaled to a hostile dimension.

**Evolution.** Tragic. Returns to Earth, loses functional custody, Ian is reabsorbed into the comic's narrative machinery (variously aged, repurposed, sidelined). For the subject this is a *third orphaning*, structurally: he has now lost a mother, a mentor, and a son. He has spoken of Ian rarely in subsequent runs — the same encysted-affect register he uses for Bucky's first death and for Peggy. The diagnostic reading is straightforward: he has filed Ian alongside the other unmetabolized losses, and the file is locked.

**Trust/safety.** Steve was, for Ian, the only safe object in a world otherwise composed of Zola's apparatus. Ian was, for Steve, the first relationship in his entire life in which *he* was the unambiguously stronger party and the other was unambiguously dependent. This inversion is psychologically important: it allowed Steve, for eleven subjective years, to occupy the parental position he never received and to demonstrate to himself that the Joseph-template is not heritable through him. The Dimension Z period is, on the evidence, the longest stretch of *non-grieving* the subject has experienced as an adult. He was too busy parenting to grieve.

**Failure mode.** The return. Steve's reabsorption into Earth-side Avengers continuity stripped him of the parental role faster than he could metabolize the loss of it, and the narrative apparatus did not give him the on-panel space to grieve. The behavioral residue: an additional layer of caretaking-of-young-figures in the subsequent runs (more attentive to Miles, to Kate in some configurations, to younger Avengers generally) and a slightly heavier reticence about the future. He does not, in modern runs, plan toward a domestic life. The Dimension Z evidence suggests he could have one and would be good at it; the post-return evidence suggests he has concluded he cannot keep one.

**Leverage.** Latent and severe. Any actor who could credibly produce Ian — alive, harmed, or asking — would obtain disproportionate response from the subject. Less manipulable than the Bucky-vector because the relationship is less culturally legible and Steve has had less time to harden defenses around it; more manipulable because the Dimension Z parenting was secret, intimate, and unwitnessed by his current peer-group, which means he has no relational scaffolding to share the grief through. This is the single most exposed vector in the origin-family register that is not already publicly contested.

---

## 6. THE DEAD COHORT, AS COMPOSITE ORIGIN-FAMILY OBJECT

*Treated here as one entry because the subject treats them as one object.*

**History.** The Howling Commandos (Dum Dum Dugan as longest-surviving exception via Life Model Decoy plot devices, ultimately also gone); the Invaders (Namor extant, Spitfire long-lived, the rest gone); the Brooklyn neighborhood of his boyhood; the cohort of nurses and tenement neighbors and parish figures who composed Sarah's social world; Peggy, in her aged-and-then-dying form (mapped in Romantic Partners). Collectively: every person who knew the subject before 1945 is dead, dementing, or institutionally preserved as a curiosity.

**Dynamics.** The subject's relationship to this composite is the structural engine of the Prolonged Grief Disorder diagnosis. He does not grieve them serially; he grieves them as a *world*. Waid's *Man Out of Time* is the canonical documentation: he walks the streets of his old neighborhood and the streets are not there, and the affect is not nostalgia but something closer to the bereaved spouse who returns to an empty apartment. The cohort is the apartment.

**Evolution.** Steve has, across the modern runs, developed a small repertoire of ritualized contact with the dead cohort: graveside visits (Bucky's empty marker, before the Winter Soldier reveal; Peggy's, after); the keeping of period artifacts (his original wartime sketchpad, recovered and lost across runs; a small set of letters; Sarah's rosary in at least one continuity); the slightly archaic verbal idiom that he has *chosen not to update* (he could code-switch entirely; he has elected not to, because the idiom is the last fragment of the dead world that lives in him). These are not mere characterization tics; they are observable mourning behaviors of a man who has no living interlocutors for the world he is mourning.

**Trust/safety.** The dead cohort is, paradoxically, the *safest* relational object in the file — they cannot be lost again, cannot disappoint, cannot betray. This is the structural seduction of complicated grief: the dead become safer than the living, and the bereaved organizes life around the safer object. Steve has not crossed fully into this configuration — the living relationships (Sam, Natasha when extant, Bucky-restored) hold him on the other side — but the gravitational pull is visible.

**Failure mode.** That he is the last. The survivor's guilt is not abstract; it is specific to the fact that he, the smallest and sickest, outlived all of them. The Erskine-Bucky-Sarah triangulation locks in here: each of them died doing what they were supposed to do, and he, the one given the body to do it, survived by being frozen — i.e., by *not doing it*. The unspoken accusation he carries against himself is that the ice was a desertion. He has never said this on panel. Every behavioral pattern in the dossier suggests he believes it.

**Leverage.** Cumulative rather than punctate. No single dead figure can be weaponized against him without desecration-detection (see Erskine, §3). But the *fact of the cohort*, invoked structurally — *you have outlived everyone who could hold you accountable; what are you, now, without them?* — is the single most effective rhetorical attack ever launched at him, and it has been launched, in some form, by every major adversary from the Red Skull through Zemo through the Hydra-Steve construct itself. His response is invariant: he does not refute the accusation. He simply continues to act as though the dead were watching. Which, in the operative sense that matters for behavior prediction, they are.

---

## SECTION SYNTHESIS — ORIGIN-FAMILY ARCHITECTURE

The subject's origin-family map is small, dense, and almost entirely composed of the dead. Five of the six entries above are deceased, dementing, or narratively orphaned. The living relationships in his life (mapped in subsequent sections) sit atop this substrate but do not replace it. The operative architecture:

- **Sarah** is the moral interlocutor — the voice in the head.
- **Joseph** is the negative template — the man he refuses to become.
- **Erskine** is the vocation-installing introject — the trust that cannot be defaulted on.
- **Bucky** is the irreplaceable brother — the only witness to continuity of self across the serum.
- **Ian** is the road not taken — the proof that he could parent and the wound of not getting to.
- **The cohort** is the lost world — the social substrate of a self that no longer has a society.

Every major behavioral pattern documented in the Analytical Workup — the caretaking tilt, the anger-monitoring, the inability to retire the identity, the equanimity in the face of his own deaths, the chronic low-grade grief, the refusal of pharmacological blunting, the disproportionate response to harm-in-vicinity, the slow-and-deep cognitive style, the moral-before-tactical reasoning — terminates, when traced upward, in one of the six entries above. The serum is the smaller fact. The family — particularly its absence — is the larger one.

— *End Section. Continues: Relationships II — Peer & Combat Bonds.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

*UATU dossier, Section 7. Subject: ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT. Each entry maps the entire arc of a relationship as it would be reconstructed by a longitudinal observer: origin, behavioral grammar, evolution, the specific shape of trust, the specific shape of failure, and the leverage — both what others have over Subject and what Subject has over them.*

---

## 7.1 — Dr. Abraham Erskine (Josef Reinstein)

**History.** Subject's first non-familial adult attachment of consequence and the formative transferential father. Erskine recruited Subject on moral grounds explicitly and visibly (*Tales of Suspense* #63, elaborated in *Captain America* vol. 1 #255, Stern/Byrne) — "a weak man knows the value of strength, knows the value of power" — and was murdered by a Heinz Kruger gunshot within hours of administering the serum. The relationship's entire empirical content is perhaps thirty-six to seventy-two hours of contact. Its psychological weight is incommensurable to its duration.

**Dynamics.** Erskine occupied the *good-father* slot vacated by Joseph Rogers's death and pre-occupied by his violence. Subject was chosen *for* the moral substrate Joseph had failed to model — every interaction Subject reports having had with Erskine (the laboratory conversations, the final words about "a good man") functions for him as a benediction. The relationship was, in clinical terms, a brief therapeutic alliance compressed into a paternal commissioning.

**Evolution.** Frozen at apex by Erskine's death. Subject has spent eight decades in *unilateral* relationship with a man who cannot answer back. Every time Subject overrides his own self-interest for principle, he is checking his conduct against Erskine's posthumous face. The pattern is visible in Stern's #247 (the recovered memory of Erskine's final instruction), the Waid *Man Out of Time* recollections, and in the silence with which Subject treats every offer to stop.

**Trust/Safety.** Total, because counterfactual. Erskine cannot betray him; the dead don't.

**Failure mode.** None available to the dyad as such. The dyadic *risk*, however, is that the unfalsifiable benediction structurally licenses Subject to refuse care from the living — anyone trying to give him permission to stop is rebutted by a dead voice they cannot hear.

**Leverage.** Erskine has total leverage over Subject. Anyone who can credibly invoke "Erskine would want—" can move him; correspondingly, Subject is profoundly defended against this invocation when made by a manipulator (Zemo, Hydra-Steve constructs), because the invocation must match his internal text exactly.

---

## 7.2 — James Buchanan "Bucky" Barnes

**History.** Original peer-bond. In Simon/Kirby's first incarnation, the sidekick kid mascot of Camp Lehigh; in Brubaker's retroactive reconstruction (*Captain America* vol. 5, 2005–2012), an age-appropriate covert-operations asset — younger than Subject by a margin but trained as a killer the public Captain America was kept clean of. The Brubaker frame is the canonically dominant one now. Apparent death on the Zemo drone in 1945 (*Avengers* #4 retcon); resurrection as the Winter Soldier; restoration; vigilantism; replacement Captain America during Subject's death (Brubaker post-*Civil War*); current parallel-operator status.

**Dynamics.** The pre-war foundation is older-brother / little-brother inverted by Subject's pre-serum frailty: in Brooklyn, Bucky protected Steve. After the serum, Steve protected Bucky — incompletely, the records suggest, since Bucky was doing wet-work Steve was not asked to do, a division of moral labor Subject did not consciously elect but tacitly benefited from. This asymmetry is the source of the load-bearing guilt: Subject believes he was the *clean face* of a war whose dirty hands belonged to a boy he loved.

**Evolution.** Three distinct phases. (1) *War-era* — operational intimacy, mutual rescue, the sole peer who saw Subject pre- and post-serum and treated both as the same person. (2) *Winter Soldier era* — relationship reconstructed in reverse: Subject's grief deepens upon Bucky's return, because the death he mourned was preferable to the sixty years of weaponized captivity that actually occurred. Brubaker #14 is diagnostic: Subject's response is not joy but a quiet articulation that he failed Bucky more thoroughly than he had previously understood. (3) *Post-restoration* — the bond is restored to peer status but altered: each man now carries irreversible knowledge of what the other can do, become, and survive. They do not require each other's daily presence; they require each other's *existence* as a referent.

**Trust/Safety.** Maximal — uniquely so. Subject will accept being lied to by Bucky, will accept Bucky's killing record, will accept Bucky operating outside Subject's ethical line. The only person about whom this is true.

**Failure mode.** The dyad fails when Subject's protectiveness overrides Bucky's autonomy — Subject will, predictably, attempt to absorb consequences that are Bucky's to bear, which Bucky reads (correctly) as condescension. Conversely, Bucky's failure mode toward Subject is concealment: he withholds his condition to spare Subject grief, which intensifies the very grief it is meant to spare.

**Leverage.** Bucky has the single deepest leverage on Subject of any living person. A threat to Bucky overrides Subject's strategic judgment, full stop. Subject's leverage on Bucky is the reverse, and Bucky's enemies have learned this; Subject's compromised cognition around Bucky is a documented exploit (Zemo arcs, the conditioning trigger sequences during the Winter Soldier years).

---

## 7.3 — Margaret "Peggy" Carter

*Mapped extensively in Relationships I (Romantic/Intimate). Cross-referenced here as chosen-family. The post-romantic phase — visits to the care facility (Brubaker vol. 5 #14 and elsewhere) — is a chosen-family ritual rather than a romantic one and belongs structurally here. Subject visits, reads to her, accepts on alternating days that she does not always know him. The visits are the most uncomplaining and most undefended act in his entire behavioral record; they are also the longest continuous grief ritual he has ever permitted himself.*

---

## 7.4 — Samuel Thomas Wilson (Falcon; successor Captain America)

**History.** First met in Englehart's *Captain America* #117 (1969); Harlem social worker and bird-handler initially miswritten as backstory cover, later restored as the actual identity. Long-term partnership across the Englehart, Gruenwald, Brubaker, Remender, and Spencer runs; eventual succession to the Captain America identity during the Remender/Spencer era.

**Dynamics.** Sam is the only peer-relationship Subject has with someone who is neither a soldier from his original cohort nor an enhanced operator. The bond is built on argument — Sam will tell Subject he is wrong, and Subject will hear it. Englehart's run establishes the basic grammar: Sam corrects Subject's blind spots (initially racial, later institutional, later strategic) without retreating from him afterward. Brubaker and Spencer extend this into political disagreement that does not become rupture. Subject's behavioral tell with Sam is uncharacteristic: he asks questions and waits for answers, rather than absorbing data silently and acting later.

**Evolution.** Mentor → co-equal → successor → peer-elder. Subject's willingness to hand Sam the shield (Remender #25, 2014) is the single most diagnostic act of trust in his entire record: he gave the identity-as-public-instrument to someone he was confident would be a better political custodian than himself. The handoff was not retirement — it was *delegation to better hands for the moment* — and the post-handoff dynamic shows Subject working under Sam without status friction.

**Trust/Safety.** Near-maximal. Sam is one of perhaps three people Subject will admit weariness to. Sam is the one Subject will let see him not knowing the answer.

**Failure mode.** Sam can be made to bear weight Subject should be carrying simply because Subject does not know how to ask. When Sam picks up Subject's load uninvited, Subject thanks him in a register Sam reads as warm and which is actually slightly farther away than Subject realizes. The dyad's risk is *quiet over-recruitment of Sam into Subject's caretaking function*.

**Leverage.** Sam has unique leverage: he is one of the few people whose moral disapproval Subject will internalize rather than rebut. Coates's run shows Subject altering positions because Sam disagreed. Adversaries who understand this have tried to engineer Sam-Steve disagreement as a sabotage vector; it has rarely worked because both men maintain awareness of the exploit.

---

## 7.5 — Anthony Edward Stark

**History.** Co-founder of the Avengers (*Avengers* #1, 1963). Decades of partnership punctuated by ideological collisions (Armor Wars peripheries, Operation: Galactic Storm, *Avengers Disassembled*), the catastrophic rupture of *Civil War* (Millar, 2006–07), the post-*Siege* tentative repair, the *Original Sin* secondary rupture (Hickman), and the *Civil War II* recapitulation.

**Dynamics.** The longest-running productive disagreement of Subject's life. Stark represents the *engineered* solution to every problem; Subject represents the *embodied* solution. They are complementary when aligned, fatally divergent when not. The relationship was, for ~40 years of in-story time, the closest functional friendship Subject had outside of Bucky — and the most operationally important. Subject trusted Stark with his life, with operational command of the unit, with his recovery from the ice, and (per Hickman *Original Sin*) with secrets Subject did not even hold himself.

**Evolution.** Trust → mutual reliance → ideological strain (post-Stamford) → operational rupture (Registration) → Subject's surrender and assassination → tentative rebuild (post-*Siege*) → secondary rupture over Illuminati mind-wipe (*Original Sin*; Subject's discovery that Stark had erased his memory of Incursion decisions is the deepest betrayal he has documented from a living person) → current cold-civil functioning.

**Trust/Safety.** Once near-maximal in operational terms; now structurally compromised in a way Subject cannot fully reverse even with sustained good behavior from Stark. Subject can work with Stark; he can no longer be off-duty around Stark.

**Failure mode.** The dyad fails along a predictable seam: Stark optimizes for outcome, Subject for process and consent; when stakes rise, Stark unilateralizes ("I will decide for everyone because I can see further"), which Subject experiences as the betrayal not of friendship but of the *democratic premise* of their partnership. Subject's failure mode toward Stark is reciprocal — he will treat Stark's reasoning as inherently suspect on consent grounds even where Stark is correct on outcome grounds.
