**Leverage.** Stark retains residual leverage through history and through Subject's reluctance to write him off; this leverage is degraded but not extinguished. Subject retains massive leverage over Stark — Stark wants Subject's approval more than the inverse, a power asymmetry both men understand and neither articulates.

---

## 7.6 — Natalia Alianovna "Natasha" Romanova

**History.** Acquaintance via Avengers (mid-Silver Age); operational intimacy across the Brubaker run (she is Bucky's lover and thus inside Subject's protective circle by transitive ethics); deep tradecraft partnership through *Secret Avengers*, *Captain America* vol. 5–7, and the modern runs. Killed in *Secret Empire*; resurrected.

**Dynamics.** Subject's most operationally trusted intelligence asset. The bond is built on professional respect first and personal warmth second — a configuration unusual for Subject, who typically inverts that order. Natasha is one of the few people Subject will accept ethically marginal tactics from without protest because he trusts her ethical accounting. She, in turn, treats Subject as a moral north she does not have to fully share to navigate by.

**Evolution.** Colleague → trusted operator → chosen-family-via-Bucky → independent intimate (non-romantic) → loss and restoration. Her death during *Secret Empire* at the hands of his Hydra-counterfeit is one of the unhealed wounds on his record; her resurrection has not fully closed it, because Subject experiences her continued presence partly as reprieve and partly as reminder of the thing-with-his-face that killed her.

**Trust/Safety.** Asymmetric in an interesting way: Subject trusts her *judgment* maximally and her *self-disclosure* moderately, knowing she withholds operationally. He does not experience this as breach because the withholding is professional.

**Failure mode.** The dyad fails when Subject moralizes about a tactical decision she has already executed; she absorbs it without argument and changes nothing, which leaves Subject expending moral effort on a closed transaction. Conversely, she fails him by under-asking: she has more access to him than she uses.

**Leverage.** Natasha can move Subject by invoking Bucky implicitly. She rarely does, which is why she could if she chose.

---

## 7.7 — Sharon Carter (Agent 13)

*Primary mapping in Relationships I. Cross-referenced here for the chosen-family dimension: Sharon is also Peggy's relation, which renders her a literal as well as figurative continuation of the only family Subject formed in his original century. Subject's pattern of relating to Sharon-as-kin is distinct from Sharon-as-lover; he protects her differently and grieves her differently. The Dimension Z-era separation and the post-*Secret Empire* alienation (Spencer) both belong here as chosen-family ruptures, not merely romantic ones.*

---

## 7.8 — Richard Milhouse "Rick" Jones

**History.** Inherited from Hulk continuity; Subject's first post-thaw sidekick attempt (*Captain America* #110, Steranko); explicitly framed by Subject and by writers as a Bucky-replacement.

**Dynamics.** The relationship's diagnostic value lies in its *failure to take*. Subject tried to install Rick into the Bucky-slot, recognized within a handful of issues that the slot was non-transferable, and gently disassembled the arrangement. The pattern reveals Subject's awareness, even in the late 1960s narrative window, that grief substitution is not grief work.

**Evolution.** Surrogate → recognized-as-surrogate → stable friendship at appropriate distance. Subject still treats Rick warmly across decades of cameo appearances; the bond is real but minor.

**Trust/Safety.** Moderate. Subject's reservation is not about Rick — it is about the protective decision not to put a non-enhanced civilian in Bucky's old slot.

**Failure mode.** Functionally absent because Subject calibrated proximity down before the dyad could fail.

**Leverage.** Negligible in either direction.

---

## 7.9 — John Walker (U.S. Agent; the replacement Captain America)

**History.** The government-installed successor during the Commission removal arc (Gruenwald #332–350). Atrocity-adjacent failures during his tenure; subsequent breakdown; eventual rehabilitation; long career as U.S.Agent and intermittent ally.

**Dynamics.** This is the load-bearing case for Subject's *repair-rather-than-discard* ethic. Walker did, with the same iconography, the things Subject most fears latent in it: nationalist violence, summary justice, performance of strength as substitute for ethics. Subject's response, after Walker's collapse, was to take him back, train him, and decline ever to publicly denigrate him. The granular tell: Subject corrects Walker privately, defends him publicly, and never once invokes Walker as cautionary example even when offered the opening.

**Evolution.** Adversary → broken successor → trainee → distant but functional ally. Walker remains operationally available to Subject; the warmth is real but bounded.

**Trust/Safety.** Conditional but extended. Subject trusts Walker to do the work; he does not trust Walker's interior, and Walker knows this and does not resent it.

**Failure mode.** Walker's failure mode is the original failure mode — under acute stress he reaches for the iconography rather than the principle. Subject's failure mode toward Walker is over-patience: he extends rehabilitative credit past the point at which a third party would withdraw it.

**Leverage.** Subject has unique leverage on Walker — he is the only person whose disappointment Walker registers as cost. Walker has minimal leverage on Subject, but a particular kind: he is the living refutation of the idea that the uniform makes the man, and Subject keeps him in the field partly to remind himself of that.

---

## 7.10 — Nicholas Joseph Fury Sr.

**History.** WWII-era Howling Commando partnership; postwar reactivation through SHIELD. The only living human besides Bucky who knew Subject in the original century at peer rank.

**Dynamics.** A partnership of mutual professional respect across decades, complicated by Subject's discovery (Hickman *Original Sin*) that Fury operated a parallel architecture of decisions Subject would have refused. The relationship survives but is permanently inflected by the knowledge that Fury kept the world running through means Subject's conscience disallows.

**Evolution.** Wartime brother → institutional partner → quiet structural opponent → cold mutual respect.

**Trust/Safety.** Trusted operationally; not trusted with consent-bearing decisions. Subject will accept intelligence from Fury and will refuse to be governed by him.

**Failure mode.** The dyad fails along an information asymmetry: Fury knows more than he tells; Subject demands disclosure that Fury cannot give without compromising assets; impasse becomes pattern.

**Leverage.** Fury has leverage over Subject through war-era memory and through the residual identification of *fellow last survivor*. Subject has leverage over Fury through Fury's quiet desire to be morally legible to one of the few people whose judgment Fury cannot dismiss.

---

## 7.11 — Carol Susan Jane Danvers

**History.** Long Avengers tenure; deep professional respect; affinity through shared military background. The bond intensified across the modern era and ruptured in *Civil War II*, where Carol occupied the predictive-justice position and Subject the consent-and-process position — an unwelcome recapitulation of his collision with Stark, with the genders and politics inverted.

**Dynamics.** Subject reads Carol as a peer in a way he reads few women, because she carries operational command experience he respects without qualifier. The relationship's grammar is brevity: they speak in short complete sentences, do not over-explain, and trust each other to fill in.

**Evolution.** Colleague → trusted commander → ideological opponent → repaired working relationship at reduced personal intimacy.

**Trust/Safety.** High operational; reduced personal post-*Civil War II*.

**Failure mode.** Same seam as with Stark: when Carol unilateralizes on confidence in outcome, Subject withdraws legitimacy on grounds of process. The pattern is structural, not personal.

**Leverage.** Limited mutual leverage; both default to argument rather than appeal.

---

## 7.12 — T'Challa

**History.** Avengers tenure under Roy Thomas; recurring partnership; deepened through Hickman *Avengers* / *New Avengers* and Coates's *Captain America* run.

**Dynamics.** Subject's relationship with T'Challa is one of his few peer-of-equal-stature bonds — head-of-state-grade. Subject defers to T'Challa's political judgment within Wakandan and broader African contexts in a way he defers to almost no one else, recognizing the limits of his own situated understanding. T'Challa, reciprocally, accepts Subject's tactical leadership in shared operations.

**Evolution.** Colleague → trusted peer-of-state → co-Illuminati member (with the asymmetric collapse of trust around the Incursion mind-wipe, in which T'Challa was complicit with Stark) → repaired working alliance with conscious awareness of past breach.

**Trust/Safety.** Operationally high; consent-bearing trust permanently calibrated downward post-*Original Sin*.

**Failure mode.** The dyad fails when state-interest and individual-conscience pull apart and T'Challa chooses state; Subject does not blame him but registers the cost.

**Leverage.** Mutual and disciplined. Neither uses it.

---

## 7.13 — Thor Odinson

**History.** Founding Avenger. One of the very few peers Subject reads as a *moral* equal rather than a tactical or operational one.

**Dynamics.** Subject's bond with Thor is unusual in its register: warm, ceremonial, slightly formal, mutually deferential. They speak to each other as if each were the senior man in a tradition the other respects. Thor will yield tactical command to Subject without flinching; Subject will yield cosmological framing to Thor without flinching.

**Evolution.** Stable across six decades with minimal drift. Aaron's recent runs increasingly position the two as the unit's elders, the ones the others go to with the unsayable.

**Trust/Safety.** Maximal of a peculiar kind — Subject trusts Thor's *integrity* totally, his *judgment* highly, his *availability* contingently (Thor is often elsewhere in nine realms).

**Failure mode.** Functionally none documented. The relationship's risk is structural rather than dyadic — Thor's prolonged absences leave Subject without one of his few load-sharing peers.

**Leverage.** Negligible in any manipulative sense; significant in the sense that Thor's disapproval would devastate Subject and Subject's disapproval would devastate Thor. They both know and neither will weaponize.

---

## 7.14 — Clint Barton

**History.** Long Avengers tenure; the Gruenwald-era West Coast period; intermittent partnership.

**Dynamics.** Clint is one of the few people Subject is openly funny with. The relationship's grammar permits ribbing in both directions — Subject can mock Clint's risk discipline; Clint can mock Subject's earnestness. The bond is real and load-bearing precisely because it is the only one in which Subject's solemnity is permitted to relax.

**Evolution.** Wary (early; Clint's youthful insubordination) → trusted operator → friend-with-permission-to-tease → quiet long-distance loyalty.

**Trust/Safety.** High; Subject trusts Clint to be where he says he will be.

**Failure mode.** Clint's failure mode is intermittent disappearance under personal crisis; Subject's is failure to ask after him during those periods, defaulting to "give him room."

**Leverage.** Mutual and warm.

---

**History.** Inducted into Avengers in the C


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*Subject: ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT. Dossier section 7-C. Cross-reference Relationships I (Family/Origin), Relationships II (Comrades/Successors). All entries derived from canonical record; behavioral inferences flagged. Mapping conventions per dossier standard: HISTORY → DYNAMICS → EVOLUTION → TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR → FAILURE MODE → LEVERAGE (i.e., what the subject would do to protect/oppose this person; what this person could extract from the subject under pressure).*

---

## PART A — RIVALS & ENEMIES

The subject's adversary set is structurally narrower than most operatives at his tenure level. He does not collect enemies casually; he metabolizes the great majority of conflicts into either repair (Walker, Namor in most configurations) or stable, non-escalating opposition (most state actors). The figures below are the *recurrent* ones — the ones whose presence is felt across decades and runs, whose existence has shaped the subject's operational and psychological architecture.

### A1. Johann Schmidt — the Red Skull

**HISTORY.** Encounter base: *Captain America Comics* #7 (1941) onward; the entirety of the Stern–Byrne, Gruenwald, Waid, Brubaker, Remender, and Spencer runs include sustained Skull material. The relationship is the *defining* adversarial axis of the subject's life: contemporaneous origin (both products of 1940s German weaponized-human programs in mirror), contemporaneous longevity (cryo for one, cloned bodies and cube-displacement for the other), and contemporaneous symbolic register (one nation's ideal versus another's). Brubaker (*Captain America* vol. 5 #1–25) escalates the personal dimension: Skull is the proximate architect of Bucky's enslavement as the Winter Soldier; thus Skull authored the central wound of the subject's postwar life. Skull is also, in vol. 5 #25, the proximate planner of the subject's assassination on the courthouse steps.

**DYNAMICS.** Mutual fixation, mutual recognition, ideological inversion. The subject does not hate Schmidt in the affective sense — he is one of the few enemies the subject genuinely *recognizes* as a complete and coherent opposite, which paradoxically lowers the emotional temperature of their fights. Schmidt is the negative theorem of Erskine's premise: same procedure (analogue serum), opposite substrate (sadism rather than empathy). The subject treats him as a proof-of-concept that the serum amplifies what is already there, which both validates Erskine's selection criterion and condemns Schmidt to a category beyond rehabilitation.

**EVOLUTION.** Early-canon Skull (Simon/Kirby through Lee) is a pulp villain; the subject fights him as occupational hazard. Stern–Byrne (#290s) reframes Skull as the subject's *philosophical* opposite. Gruenwald (#350) elaborates: Skull poisons the subject with the same Super-Soldier Serum that runs in Skull's own veins — i.e., forces the subject to literally share blood with him, which the subject experiences as desecration (this is the *specific* texture of his post-serum-purge phobia of contamination). Brubaker collapses the personal and political into the same enemy: every wound Steve has been carrying since 1945 traces back, through Lukin and Zola, to Schmidt. Remender (*Captain America* vol. 7) externalizes the contest into Dimension Z — Skull-as-Zola steals years of subjective time from the subject, weaponizing his children against him. Spencer's *Secret Empire* uses Schmidt as the cube-wielding architect of the Hydra-Steve catastrophe — i.e., Schmidt succeeds, briefly, in *making* the subject what Schmidt is.

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** Zero/negative. The subject will not negotiate with Schmidt under any framing; he will not accept Schmidt's surrender as binding (he assumes deception); he does not extend the forgiveness available to Walker, Bucky, even Zemo Jr. Significance: the *only* figure in the canon for whom the subject has effectively foreclosed repair. Diagnostically important — it shows that the subject's mercy is principled, not indiscriminate, and has a floor.

**FAILURE MODE.** Two distinct ones. (a) *Schmidt-baiting*: Schmidt has, on multiple occasions (Gruenwald #350; Brubaker arcs), drawn the subject into private duels he should have delegated, because Schmidt knows the subject cannot let a Schmidt operation conclude without his personal presence. (b) *Moral exhaustion*: across decades, repeated defeats and resurrections of Schmidt produce a quiet, weary register in the subject — captioned by Brubaker as "he just keeps coming back" — that approaches the closest thing to despair the subject permits himself.

**LEVERAGE.** Schmidt holds essentially one card and has played it repeatedly: *proximity to the subject's loved ones*. Bucky was the masterstroke; Sharon (Brubaker) was the secondary; the threat to civilian populations the standing one. The subject's predictable response is to step forward personally. Inversely, the subject's leverage over Schmidt is the simple, uninflected fact of his existence — Schmidt cannot tolerate the subject continuing to be, and is repeatedly drawn into operationally costly schemes to end him.

---

### A2. Helmut Zemo (and the inherited shadow of Heinrich Zemo)

**HISTORY.** Heinrich Zemo (Baron Zemo I): the proximate killer of Bucky on the drone, *Avengers* #4 retcon; the man whose adhesive-X welded the subject's identity to the loss. Helmut Zemo (Baron Zemo II): *Captain America* #168 onward; major presence in Gruenwald's Masters of Evil arcs, Busiek's *Thunderbolts*, Remender, and most extensively in modern reframings.

**DYNAMICS.** Inherited grudge metabolized into a more interesting and unstable adversarial axis than the father's. The subject regards Helmut neither as the cartoon his father was nor as a Schmidt-tier irredeemable. Helmut is intelligent, ideologically coherent in his own terms (aristocratic-nationalist rather than fascist-eliminationist), and capable of strategic patience the subject finds genuinely formidable. The Thunderbolts gambit — Helmut leading a team posing as heroes — is the kind of operation that exploits the subject's *moral* operating system: it weaponizes the public's hope, which is the subject's own primary instrument.

**EVOLUTION.** Initially a revenge-by-proxy figure (the son who hates Captain America for killing his father, despite the father having killed Bucky — the symmetry the subject finds bleakly familiar). The Masters of Evil arc (Stern, #270s; with reverberation through Roger Stern's *Avengers*) — particularly the home invasion of Avengers Mansion and the torture of Jarvis — produces in the subject a register of cold, sustained anger he reserves for very few opponents. The Thunderbolts arc complicates everything: Helmut *plays heroism well enough that it briefly takes*, which raises the subject's enduring private question of whether ideology and behavior are separable.

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** Hostile but legible. The subject will negotiate with Helmut under flag-of-truce conditions (he has done so), accept short-term tactical alliances of necessity (rarely), but never extends standing trust. He treats Helmut as a man whose word is reliable about the *terms* of a contest and unreliable about its *substance*.

**FAILURE MODE.** Helmut exploits the subject's belief that people can change. Each time Helmut presents a credible reformation arc, the subject expends operational bandwidth verifying it, which is itself the attack.

**LEVERAGE.** Mutual: each has standing access to the other's most private vulnerabilities (Helmut has the Bucky file; the subject has the documentary evidence of Helmut's hypocrisies). Neither tends to deploy the personal register; both prefer to operate at the level of plan-versus-plan, which is the strange residual respect between them.

---

### A3. Aleksander Lukin / the Winter Soldier program / the consciousness of Schmidt-in-Lukin

**HISTORY.** Brubaker, *Captain America* vol. 5 #1–25 and on. Lukin: former Soviet general, inheritor of the Winter Soldier asset, eventually a vessel for the cube-displaced consciousness of the Red Skull. The relevance is *operational*: Lukin is the proximate handler of Bucky for sixty years.

**DYNAMICS.** The subject's response to Lukin is qualitatively different from his response to Schmidt — colder, more procedural, less philosophical. Lukin is the *bureaucrat* of the wound. The subject hunts him with the methodical patience he typically reserves for institutional targets (Hydra cells, AIM nodes) rather than personal ones. The Skull-in-Lukin reveal collapses this distinction and produces one of the few panels in the canon where the subject is depicted as *speechless* — Brubaker uses silent panels to encode the recognition.

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** None. Lukin exists in the subject's mental architecture as a *case file*, not a person.

**FAILURE MODE / LEVERAGE.** Lukin's leverage was always Bucky's body; once Bucky is recovered, Lukin's strategic position against the subject collapses. The subject's failure mode here is post-resolution: with Lukin dispatched, the subject confronts that the recovery of Bucky does not undo what was done *to* Bucky, and the case-file framing offers no consolation.

---

### A4. Crossbones (Brock Rumlow)

**HISTORY.** Gruenwald introduces (*Captain America* #359); central to Brubaker's vol. 5 as the operational trigger-man of the subject's assassination (#25). Recurrent throughout modern runs.

**DYNAMICS.** A pure operational adversary — sadist, mercenary, ideologically aligned with Skull but without Skull's coherence. The subject regards Rumlow with the closest thing to ordinary contempt he permits himself, and is unusually willing to use force against him beyond strict necessity. (Diagnostically: Rumlow occupies a slot in the subject's psyche near the abusive father — the unimpressive bully whose harm is disproportionate to his stature. The subject does not articulate this; the behavior is consistent.)

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** None; the subject would not parley.

**FAILURE MODE.** The subject's controlled distaste for Rumlow occasionally produces a marginally less-careful posture than against more sophisticated enemies — Rumlow has wounded him through pure ferocity more often than through plan.

**LEVERAGE.** Rumlow's leverage over the subject is the recurring threat of harm to Sharon Carter specifically; the subject's leverage over Rumlow is uncomplicated — he wins the fights.

---

### A5. Arnim Zola

**HISTORY.** Kirby's 1970s run; Brubaker; Remender's *Dimension Z* (where Zola is the proximate antagonist for the years-long stranding).

**DYNAMICS.** Zola is the *biotechnical* adversary — the man who experiments on bodies, including the subject's own, including the body of the subject's adopted son Ian. The subject's response to Zola has a register reserved for vivisectionists: a cold disgust qualitatively distinct from his response to ideologues. Remember that the subject lived in a failing body for two decades; the body-as-experiment register touches the part of him that remembers being small and being examined.

**EVOLUTION.** Earlier appearances are pulp; Remender weaponizes the relationship by making Zola the proximate cause of Ian's existence and Ian's loss, transforming Zola in the subject's mental file from a category enemy (mad scientist) to a personal one (the man who used my son).

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** None.

**LEVERAGE.** Zola's leverage on the subject is, distinctively, *time* — he is the antagonist most associated with stealing years of the subject's life (Dimension Z). The subject's leverage on Zola is purely operational; Zola fears him.

---

### A6. Anthony Stark — Tony Stark (the most important rivalry; categorically distinct from the enemies above)

**HISTORY.** Founding co-Avenger (*Avengers* #1, Lee/Kirby 1963). Across sixty real-world years and dozens of writers, the relationship is the longest sustained peer-bond of the subject's post-ice life. The rivalry frame is *retrospective* — they were friends for forty real-time years before Millar's *Civil War* (2006–07) recodified the relationship as adversarial — and the friendship survives the rivalry on both sides of the schism. (Note: this entry belongs in Relationships II as comrade, here as rival, and arguably also in any future *Significant Asymmetric Bonds* section. The relationship is constitutively plural.)

**DYNAMICS — pre-Civil War.** Complementarity. Stark is fast where the subject is slow; ironic where the subject is sincere; oriented toward solutions where the subject is oriented toward principles; oriented toward systems where the subject is oriented toward persons. The subject was the moral center the Avengers organized around; Stark was the practical center it operated through. Each functioned as the other's check. Stark relied on the subject for legitimacy he could not generate from his own biography; the subject relied on Stark for institutional infrastructure he could not generate from his own resources. The relationship was *load-bearing for both men's stability* in a way neither articulated and both relied on.

**DYNAMICS — Civil War (Millar, 2006–07).** Superhuman Registration Act. Stark backs registration as the lesser evil and the price of continued operation; the subject opposes it as a violation of the principle the badge stands for. The fracture is *structural* — they are arguing from non-overlapping premises about what the institution exists for — and *personal*: each experiences the other's position as a betrayal of the version of him the friendship was based on. The combat between them is documented; what is more diagnostically important is the subject's *surrender on the courthouse steps* — he stops fighting Stark not because Stark wins but because civilians are being hurt, which is the relational ethic overriding the strategic logic, and which Stark recognizes (the half-line "you weren't fighting me anymore" in the related material captures it). Stark wins the war and loses the friend; the subject loses the war, the badge, and (briefly) his life.

**EVOLUTION post-resurrection.** Brubaker, Hickman, Aaron, Coates — the relationship is reconstructed in stages. They do not reconcile sentimentally. They resume work together because the work requires it, and the older affection re-surfaces in oblique register (Hickman's *Avengers*/*New Avengers* — the Illuminati arc places them on opposite sides of another existential argument, with mutual mind-wiping and renewed schism). The pattern is now established: they will keep doing this. Each man is, for the other, the irreplaceable peer whose opposition has the highest cost and whose alliance is the highest validation.

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** Complicated. *Operational* trust is high — each will hold a perimeter for the other under fire without hesitation. *Strategic* trust is low — neither will brief the other on plans he suspects the other would oppose. *Personal* trust is the highest in the subject's life and the most damaged — he will tell Stark things he tells no one else, and he will not forget what Stark did. (Reciprocal on Stark's side.)

**FAILURE MODE.** The subject's failure mode with Stark is to assume that the friendship has reset further than it has — to be surprised by Stark's strategic divergences as if they were betrayals when they are continuities. Stark's failure mode with the subject is the inverse: to assume that the subject's principle is performance he can be argued out of when it is structural and he cannot. The repeated catastrophe of the relationship is each man under-modeling the other's stability.

**LEVERAGE.** Reciprocal and extreme. Stark can move the subject by appealing to the *consequences* of principle on living people (the registration argument's real teeth: children who could not be protected without it). The subject can move Stark by appealing to the *means by which* an end is pursued (every Stark technology-overreach the subject has talked him back from). Neither uses the leverage frivolously; both know how much of it they hold.

---

### A7. Namor McKenzie

**HISTORY.** Wartime Invader; postwar antagonist; intermittent ally; in Hickman's *Avengers*/*New Avengers* and *Secret Wars* run, the relationship enters its most fraught register.

**DYNAMICS.** Two surviving icons of the 1940s with categorically opposed temperaments. The subject's respect for Namor's longevity, valor, and sovereignty is genuine; Namor's respect for the subject's integrity is genuine; the friction is structural (Namor's monarchism; his prioritization of Atlantis above all; his willingness to commit atrocity in defense of his people). The Illuminati arc produces the canonical break — Namor's destruction of Wakanda; the moral injury this represents to the subject who had stood beside him.

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** Conditional and oscillating; lower after Hickman than before.

**FAILURE MODE.** The subject under-weights Namor's monarchic logic until it manifests at full strength, then over-corrects into rupture.

**LEVERAGE.** Mutual recognition as last-survivors-of-the-era; this carries weight neither will admit to in council.

---

### A8. The Government / The Commission / S.H.I.E.L.D. (institutional adversary, recurrent)

Treated as a category because the subject's institutional adversaries rotate but the *relationship pattern* is stable. The subject is the most loyal institutional actor in the canon up to the point at which the institution becomes the instrument of harm, after which he is the most implacable. Specific instantiations: the Nixon-analog conspiracy (#175); the Commission removal (#332); the Registration regime (Civil War); the corrupted S.H.I.E.L.D. under Hydra penetration (Brubaker, Hickman, Spencer).

**DYNAMICS, generalized.** The subject extends provisional legitimacy to lawful authority; tests its compliance with the principle continuously; withdraws legitimacy without ceremony when the test fails; opposes the now-illegitimate authority with the same loyalty he previously gave it. The institutional adversaries consistently underestimate the speed of his transition from compliance to opposition because they read his compliance as deference rather than as conditional alignment.

**LEVERAGE.** Institutions hold no durable leverage on the subject; their only effective tool is *threat to those he protects*, which is also the trigger for his most decisive opposition. Inversely, the subject's leverage on any institution is the public legibility of his withdrawal — his refusal alone delegitimizes.

---

## PART B — LOVERS / INTIMATES

The romantic-attachment record is short, durable, and patterned. The subject does not engage casually; every documented intimate relationship is with a woman who either matches his operational risk profile (Peggy, Sharon, Natasha, Diamondback) or — in the singular civilian case (Bernie) — is one to whom he is willing to attempt a normal life and from whom he is ultimately separated by his inability to provide it. The pattern is *strong-women-who-share-the-work* with one tested exception. Diagnostically: he loves the way he was loved by Sarah Rogers — competent, burdened, present, ill-rewarded — and is wary of inflicting on a partner what his father inflicted on his mother. The wariness reads, in intimates' accounts, as withholding.

### B1. Margaret "Peggy" Carter

**HISTORY.** Wartime resistance liaison, *Tales of Suspense* #75 onward; reframed and elaborated heavily across Stern, Waid, Brubaker. The originating romantic attachment of the subject's adult life. Separated by his ice; re-encountered as an aged woman; sustained as a present figure through her decline (Brubaker *Captain America* vol. 5 #14, her death; recurrent thereafter as ghost-presence).

**DYNAMICS.** The most *symmetrical* relationship of the subject's life — Peggy operated at his risk profile, in his theater, in his idiom. She is the only intimate who knew him *before* the iconography ossified, when the badge was still raw and the man inside it was still visibly the boy from Brooklyn. The relationship has the qualities the subject seeks and cannot reproduce elsewhere: peer, witness, ally, lover, in one person.

**EVOLUTION.** Pre-ice: courtship interrupted by mission tempo and ultimately by his disappearance. Post-defrost: she is married, aged, ill; he reorients to friendship-with-residual-love and adopts the role of attentive visitor across decades. The care-facility visits (multiple writers) — bringing flowers, sitting through episodes in which she does not recognize him, leaving without correcting her when she calls him by a wartime intimate's name — are among the most behaviorally diagnostic scenes in the canon. He metabolizes the asymmetric grief without recourse to the obvious narcissistic injury (she does not always know me); he treats her cognition as the field condition and his presence as the obligation.

**TRUST/SAFETY VECTOR.** Maximum. Peggy is the figure he could be unmasked in front of without armor.

**FAILURE MODE.** None operationally; the relationship's failure was *temporal* — the ice. The subject treats this as the load-bearing personal cost of his service, not as her failure or his.

**LEVERAGE.** Peggy is the figure whose memory most reliably moves the subject. Invocations of her in argument (rare; intimates know not to use her name lightly) bypass his rhetorical control.

---

### B2. Sharon Carter (Agent 13)

**HISTORY.** *Tales of Suspense* #75 (1966); Peggy's relative (originally niece; reframed later). On-and-off across decades. Central to Brubaker vol. 5 — captured, conditioned, used as the proximate trigger of the subject's assassination (#25), bearing his child in a contested storyline subsequently complicated.

**DYNAMICS.** The most operationally entangled of his intimacies. Sharon is field-active S.H.I.E.L.D.; their courtships and ruptures track agency assignments. The relationship's chronic strain is the impossibility of disentangling personal from operational — they meet on missions, fight on missions, break up on missions. The subject's pattern with Sharon is to grant her unusual operational autonomy (he does not paternalize her; he has watched her lead) and to underprotect her in the specific psychological register: she absorbs harm meant for him repeatedly, and the cumulative weight of this becomes one of the unspoken accounts between them.

**EVOLUTION.** Early: courtship in the Lee/Kirby/Stern register, struct


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

## Preamble — the wrong question and the right one

The reflex when cataloguing the Subject's capacities is to begin with the serum. This is an error of emphasis that has misled every adversary who has ever underestimated him by reading his file rather than watching him work. The serum is a *multiplier*, not an *engine*. Strip it — as Spencer's de-serumed arc demonstrated in detail, as the periodic Gruenwald-era depowerings demonstrated before that, as the entire pre-1941 record demonstrates first — and the Subject is recognizably the same operator: same decision-tree, same moral arithmetic, same willingness to stand and absorb a beating until the situation reconfigures around his immobility. The serum is what made him *legible to others*. What makes him dangerous, durable, and irreplaceable is upstream of it.

This section therefore organizes capability not by neat physiological tiers but by what he actually *does* with what he has, where each capacity originated, how it has drifted across eighty subjective years of use, and — critically — what each capability secretly says about the wound it was built to address.

---

## 1. The Erskine–Reinstein Augmentation — somatic baseline

**Provenance.** Single intervention, Brooklyn, 1941: Abraham Erskine's refined formula plus Vita-Ray exposure (Reinstein protocol). Procedure unrepeatable in its original form; assassinated chemist, partial formula in fragments. This is operationally significant: the Subject is, in his own self-conception, a *finite resource* — non-renewable, non-replicable, last-of-his-kind, a fact that recurs in his risk calculations less as vanity than as inventory management.

**Strength.** Canonically drifted upward from "peak human" (Stern/DeMatteis era — bench around 800 lb, never staged as a strength feat) toward low-superhuman (modern: 1,200–2,000 lb staged, with anomalous adrenal spikes higher). He does not display strength expressively. Lifts are functional, leveraged, and brief. He prefers to *transfer* force (shield-strikes, throws, redirects) rather than apply raw mass. This is partly tactical (economy) and partly characterological: a man who spent his first twenty years as the smallest body in any room does not gloat with the larger one.

**Speed and reflex.** Sustained running ≈ low 30s mph; documented sprints higher under threat. Reaction time canonically clocked below human floor — he can track and parry small-arms fire at conversational distance (the shield-deflection-of-multiple-pistols routine across Gruenwald, Brubaker, Hickman). Reflex is the single most underweighted serum effect: it is what makes the shield function as both offense and defense in the same motion.

**Endurance.** The signature physical attribute and the operational dominant. ~95% metabolic efficiency, near-absent lactate accumulation under aerobic load, sleep requirement reduced (canonically 4 hours nominal, deferrable for days), recovery from exertion measured in minutes. This is the capacity that, more than strength, defines combat outcomes — he is the man still standing in round nine.

**Durability and healing.** Bones denser; connective tissue tougher; recovery from soft-tissue injury accelerated roughly 3–5×, from fractures 5–10×. He is not invulnerable. He is *bleed-able and reliably-survivable*. The clinical consequence (see Section 2 of the Workup) is a long-tenured self-perception that his body will tolerate what kills others — which licenses sacrificial behavior that would otherwise read as suicidal.

**Immunity and resistance.** Near-total resistance to infection; resistance to most poisons and pharmacological agents — including, critically, *intoxicants*. He cannot get drunk on conventional ethanol (the running gag is canon — *Captain America Annual* #4, repeated across Waid, Brubaker, and the modern runs). He cannot be sedated by ordinary means. He cannot be anesthetized without specialized dosing. Designer compounds calibrated to his physiology (Streets of Poison's "Ice"; certain Hydra and AIM agents) can breach this, and the experience of breach is — by his own behavior afterward — phobic-grade traumatic.

**Senses.** Visual acuity in the upper percentile of normal; hearing in the upper percentile minus a deficit (Brubaker's retroactive partial deafness in one ear — left, established in vol. 5 — which he compensates for by positioning, turning the head minutely on his strong side when listening across a room; he is unaware he does this, intimates have noticed). Olfaction and proprioception mildly elevated. Pain perception nominal — the serum did not blunt it; he simply does not flinch.

**Cellular stasis.** Provides the substrate for the 1945–1964 ice survival. Operationally inert until invoked; clinically, it stands as the upper-limit demonstration of how far the serum will go to preserve the asset.

**Aging.** Effectively arrested at a biological age in the early-to-mid thirties. The cost of this is not somatic; it is the chronic existential exposure of outliving everyone — see Section 2 of the Workup and Section 9 below.

---

## 2. The Shield — instrument, signature, prosthesis

**Object.** Disc, ~30 in diameter, ~12 lb, vibranium–steel–unknown alloy (Myron MacLain accident, irreproducible). Effectively indestructible at the energies routinely thrown at it; near-perfect kinetic absorption and re-emission; conserves angular momentum across ricochet with eerie fidelity.

**Mechanical use.** He throws right-handed, catches left, and has done so consistently across his published life. The throw is not a discus motion; it is a snap from a coiled torso, the wrist supinating at release to impart spin that stabilizes the disc against air shear. Ricochet routes are pre-computed: he reads a room for angles the way a billiards player reads a table, and the canonical multi-target ricochet (two-bounce, three-bounce, occasionally documented four-bounce) is not improvisation but inventory — he saw the geometry on entry. Catch-on-return is wrist-relaxed; the disc seats into a worn groove of palm callus.

**Defensive use.** Held high-tight to the body, never extended into space where leverage can be applied to the arm. Bracing posture: knees soft, hips low, shield-side foot half a pace forward, opposite hand free for grip-break or weapon-strip. He will eat a high-caliber round on the disc and treat the rotational shock through the spine, not the elbow. This is taught technique to anyone he trains; the Howlers, Rick Jones briefly, Sam Wilson, Bucky-as-Cap, Walker all show the same body-line.

**Offensive use beyond throwing.** Edge-strikes (the disc as a chopping tool — disabling, occasionally lethal, used reluctantly), bashes, hooks (the rim caught behind a limb or weapon), and the pinning-shield-as-platform routine in which he stands on it for elevation or rides it down a slope. The "shield-slide" trick (planting it on a flat surface and using it as a sled or a brace for a teammate's launch) is documented from the Howling Commando era forward.

**Symptom-as-signature reading.** The shield is the prosthesis of a man whose first identity was *being hit*. It is, structurally, the answer to the alley: a portable, unbreakable interposition between harm and a body. He throws it more often to *intercept* than to *strike* — the canonical use cases skew defensive-offensive (knock the gun out of the hostage-taker's hand) rather than offensive-offensive (decapitate). When he loses it (Gruenwald: stripped by the Commission and given the photonic energy shield; Brubaker: lost in transit; the Aaron-era recoveries) he becomes visibly diminished not in capability but in *demeanor* — he is no longer interposed. The shield is the externalized version of his life's project; without it he is still doing the project, but the public can no longer see him doing it.

---

## 3. Combat skill — eight decades of accumulated craft

**Foundational.** Army basic, 1941. WWII commando training (Camp Lehigh; then theatre-specific by the Howlers). Hand-to-hand grounded in boxing (his Brooklyn street base), judo (then-current Army close-quarters doctrine), and what would later be codified as combatives.

**Accreted.** Across the published run, instructors named or implied include: Bucky (knife work, scout-sniper basics, the wet-work skills Steve was kept clean of and later asked to be shown); the Howlers collectively (small-unit tactics); Nick Fury (intelligence tradecraft); Shang-Chi (canonical and repeated — the most explicit master-student relationship in his file, kung fu and internal energy management, *Marvel Team-Up* and later); Natasha Romanoff (Soviet-bloc methods, deception, infiltration); Bobbi Morse (escrima); Daniel Rand (chi-discipline, partial); various Wakandan instructors during T'Challa-era exchanges; the unattributed accretions of seventy years of sparring. He is a *collector* of techniques and an unusually humble student — peers across the canon comment that he asks questions a fighter of his stature should be too proud to ask.

**Composite style.** Boxing footwork at the base; judo/aiki throws as the connective tissue; striking selection drawn from several systems; weapon improvisation continuous; everything subordinated to *economy* and *terminus* — fights end as quickly as he can ethically end them. He does not showboat. He does not stage. He breaks the engagement at the earliest opportunity consistent with neutralizing the threat, and his preference order for terminus is: disarm > restrain > incapacitate > injure > maim > kill. He has killed (canonical, repeatedly, in war and against assets — see his on-panel admissions across Brubaker and Remender), but every escalation up that ladder is visible in his face and costs him.

**Tactical mind.** He is the canonical Marvel battlefield general (Hickman *Avengers*, Aaron *Avengers*, Bendis runs, Civil War). Reads terrain, reads cover, reads formation, reads the gap. Operationally prefers to be the *last* to speak in a planning session (see Workup §3, Cognitive Style) and the *first* to move once the plan is set. Adjusts on contact without ceremony — the canonical "plans change" moment recurs across writers. Delegates well; receives delegation badly (he will accept being commanded but visibly resists being *protected*).

**Marksmanship and weapons.** Trained on every standard small arm of WWII and the modern era; competent with knives, bayonets, demolitions, and improvised explosives. Rarely carries a firearm in the modern era — a choice, not a deficit. He will use one in extremis (Brubaker; the Spencer arc; certain Aaron sequences) and his form is unremarkable in the sense that exceptional fundamentals look unremarkable. He prefers the shield because the shield does not kill by default.

**Driving / piloting / horsemanship.** Drives anything wheeled or tracked from the period of his enlistment forward — including the period's motorcycles, which remain his preferred personal transport across eras. Trained pilot, jet-rated by the late Lee/Kirby era, helicopter-rated. Rides a horse (Howler-era and recurrent). Operates boats and submersibles competently. The standing rule: if it was militarized in the 20th century, he can operate it; if it is post-2000 he learns it quickly and asks questions while learning.

---

## 4. Cognitive abilities

**Memory.** Eidetic-adjacent in the operational sense — he remembers faces, routes, names, conversations, geometries, and grudges with a fidelity that disturbs people who assumed he was a relic. The serum's effect on neural function is canonical (Stern, Brubaker), but the *use* of the memory is characterological: he remembers what people have asked of him, what he has promised, what he owes. The ledger is unwritten because it does not need to be.

**Language.** Documented fluency or working facility across English (native), German (combat-acquired, fluent by 1944), French (fluent), Russian (working — Cold War accretion), Japanese (working), Spanish (working), Italian (basic), with reading knowledge of Latin and conversational Wakandan. New languages are acquired in weeks, not months — a function of attention and memory rather than any specific linguistic gift.

**Strategic and tactical reasoning.** As above. The relevant emphasis: he reasons *morally before tactically*. This is a vulnerability his most sophisticated adversaries (Zemo, Red Skull, the Hydra structure as a whole) have learned to exploit by constructing scenarios in which the ethical move is the strategically inferior one. It is also a structural asset: his commitments are legible, which makes him *coalitional* — allies trust the plan because they trust the planner's reasons.

**Learning curve.** Slow-and-deep rather than fast-and-flexible. He prefers to understand a system before operating it; once understood, the operation is permanent and untroubled. He distrusts intuition that has not been earned.

**Artistic capacity.** Trained illustrator. Pre-war commercial-art studies (canon since the Lee era and reiterated through Waid and Brubaker). He still draws — pencil, ink, occasionally watercolor — and the function is twofold: a regulating practice (steady hand, slow breathing, narrowed attention) and a private archive of faces he is afraid of forgetting. Sketchbooks recurrent in canonical interiors (Waid; Brubaker; the modern domestic scenes). This is the closest thing in his life to therapy.

---

## 5. Leadership and influence

A discrete capability and arguably his most consequential one.

**Mechanism.** Not charisma in the demagogic sense — he is not particularly funny, not particularly eloquent on a stage, not naturally given to speeches and uncomfortable when asked to give them (see his pained discomfort with USO-era marketing, persistent across Rieber, Waid, and the modern runs). The influence is *exemplary*: people follow him because of what he does when nobody is asking him to do it. The on-panel locus classicus is the moment-before-the-charge, repeatable across writers, in which subordinates of vastly greater individual power (Thor, sundry Asgardians, sundry mutants, sundry cosmic entities) defer to him because the alternative — moving without him — is unthinkable to them.

**Range.** Effective from squad level (Howlers) through small-team (Avengers, Invaders, Secret Avengers) through coalition (Civil War insurgency; the various wartime mobilizations under Hickman and Aaron) up to the rare full-mobilization. Less effective in institutional politics — he is a bad bureaucrat and knows it.

**Limit.** Falters where the cause is morally ambiguous to him. He cannot fake conviction; subordinates can read it on him within seconds. The Commission-era and Spencer-era arcs both turn on this — when the principle is compromised, the followership evaporates not because subordinates have been told to leave but because they can see he no longer believes.

---

## 6. The shield-less, serum-less floor — capability stripped to its substrate

Twice canonically tested in extended form (Gruenwald-era depowering; Spencer-era de-serumed nonagenarian arc) and many times in brief form (Streets of Poison transitional periods; Reborn-era discontinuities; mind-control episodes where capability is functional but volition is not).

Findings, consistent across writers:
- **Tactical reasoning unaffected.** Plans the same, reads rooms the same, leads the same.
- **Combat skill substantially preserved.** Technique survives body composition because technique is leverage; the older frail body cannot strike as hard but can still slip, redirect, and finish from advantageous angles.
- **Moral posture identical.** The defining behavioral demonstration of the de-serumed arc: a ninety-year-old man in a chair giving orders in the same register he gave them at thirty-five. Subordinates do not visibly adjust their respect.
- **Endurance collapses.** This is the bottleneck. The frail body cannot sustain the operational tempo; the work has to be delegated.

The diagnostic conclusion is the one the Workup makes plain: the serum did not create him. It made him *legible*. Removing it shrinks the radius of action without altering the action.

---

## 7. Growth curves over eighty subjective years

**1941–1945.** Crude soldier-athlete; over-reliant on strength and aggression; the shield-throwing canon-form does not stabilize until late 1942 — early panels show him hitting with it more than throwing it. Bucky-attachment matures the tactical work (Bucky is the small-team operator, Steve the front-line force; the division of labor is real and shapes both).

**1964 thaw → 1970s.** Re-acculturation period overlaps with rapid technique acquisition. This is the Shang-Chi era of his development; also the period in which he absorbs SHIELD tradecraft from Fury. Tactical sophistication leaps; political sophistication lags and is the source of most of the era's identity crises (Nomad, the Nixon-analog).

**1980s (Gruenwald).** Plateau of capability and the richest stress-testing. The John Walker contrast functions as a mirror — Walker has comparable strength, inferior judgement, no inhibitory mastery. Steve emerges from the era with the inhibitory mastery foregrounded as the *primary capability*; everything else is downstream.

**1990s.** Drift; uneven writing; the relevant accretion is institutional skepticism — he learns, slowly, that the state can be the threat.

**2000s (Rieber, Brubaker).** Operational maturity. The Brubaker years stage him as the most competent operator on the board not by amplifying his powers but by *narrating* them — the reader is shown the decision tree.

**2010s–present (Remender, Spencer, Coates, Aaron).** Eldership. The capability set is unchanged; what grows is the *transmission* of it — Sam Wilson, Bucky-as-Cap, the next-generation Avengers, the Wakandan exchanges. He is increasingly an institution unto himself, and increasingly aware that institutions are what fail.

---

## 8. Signature applications — the moves on his tape

Cataloguing the gestures that recur often enough across writers to qualify as personal signature:

- **The interposition.** Walks or steps into the line of fire between a threat and a non-combatant without breaking stride. Body-language tell: the shoulders square a half-beat before the feet move.
- **The shield-as-pin.** Disc dropped flat onto a downed adversary's chest or weapon, pinning without striking. A characteristically restrained termination.
- **The throw-and-recover ricochet.** Two-target, sometimes three. He calls the geometry by which target is hit second (verbal habit: a clipped name and a direction — "Barnes, left").
- **The disarm-and-offer.** Strips a weapon from an adversary and, if the engagement permits, sets it down rather than retaining it. A standing courtesy that adversaries sometimes find more disturbing than violence.
- **The hold-the-line.** Stationary defensive stance over a fallen ally or civilian; shield up, body broad; will not break the position regardless of incoming force. The canonical "I can do this all day" register, repeated since the 1940s, is the verbal accompaniment.
- **The last-to-fall.** End-of-engagement habit: he counts heads, checks pulses, accounts for adversaries (cuffs, secures, calls in) *before* he treats his own injuries. Intimates have learned to chase him down with field dressings.
- **The quiet command.** Operational voice drops in volume rather than rises under stress — adversaries and subordinates alike report leaning in to hear him at the loudest moments. A boxing-corner habit, retained.

---

## 9. The hard limits

**Pharmacological dis-inhibition.** Streets of Poison established it; he has organized his life around avoiding its repetition. He will refuse legal stimulants, mood agents, even routine anesthesia where alternatives exist. A motivated adversary who can dose him specifically can degrade him catastrophically — but the window is narrow because his metabolism purges most agents quickly.

**Mind control / cube-level reality alteration.** The Hydra-Steve episode is the upper-bound demonstration. His inhibitory mastery is volitional; remove volition and the underlying capability becomes a weapon pointed in any direction. He treats this as the worst thing that can happen to him and behaves, post-restoration, like a man who has been made to commit atrocities by his own hands.

**Volume.** He is one body. The Civil War surrender turns on this — he can win the fight in front of him and lose the city behind it; offered that trade, he stops.

**Grief-cue collapse.** Not a *combat* limit but a *deployment* limit. Encountering Bucky, Peggy-in-decline, Erskine-imagery, or the cohort dead produces the flat encysted register documented in the Workup. He continues to function but the affective bandwidth narrows; subordinates close to him have learned to route around him in those windows rather than through him.

**Moral entrapment.** The most reliably exploited limit. Adversaries who can construct a scenario in which the ethical move is the tactically inferior one will get the ethical move every time. Zemo's career is built on this. The Red Skull's late-career operations are built on this. He knows it about himself and has, across the canon, never significantly altered the pattern — because to alter it would be to become someone else, and that someone else is the person he has spent his entire life refusing to become.

**Asking him to stop.** Not a limit in the conventional sense — a *structural* one. The capability he cannot deploy is *retirement*. He has tried (Nomad; the post-#332 abandonment; the brief Sam-Wilson successions); each attempt failed not because the world demanded his return but because the function demanded it of him. To stop is to default on the dead. He cannot.

---

## 10. The symptom-as-signature read

Every capability in this section, traced to its origin, points back to the same alley.

The endurance is the alley — *I'll just get up again*. The shield is the alley — the interposition the sick boy could not be. The combat craft is the alley — the boxing footwork of the kid who knew he would lose every fight and refused to walk away from any. The leadership is the alley — the worth of standing where standing matters more than winning. The cognitive style, slow and deep and moral-before-tactical, is the alley — the kid who could not afford to be wrong because he could not afford the second beating. The pharmacological avoidance is the alley *transposed* — he watched a man (his father) become someone else under the influence of a substance, and he has organized his somatic life around never becoming that man. The refusal to retire is the alley *generalized* across eighty years of loss — every dead person he has ever loved is the kid he could not save, scaled up.

The serum is what made all of this *visible*. The shield is what made it *portable*. The eighty years of accreted craft are what made it *competent*. But the load-bearing capability — the one that, removed, would actually end him as Captain America — is the one that has no clinical name and no operational metric: a moral stubbornness so old it predates his own body, sustained by a grief he has never had the safety to mourn, expressed as a willingness to stand in front of harm one more time than the harm can stand to be there.

That is the capability the file exists to document. Everything else is leverage on it.


## Operational Profile & Tradecraft

# OPERATIONAL PROFILE & TRADECRAFT — ROGERS, S.G.

*Section 6 of the longitudinal dossier. Derived from the canonical behavioral record; granular inferences are flagged where they extend beyond direct citation.*

---

## 6.1 DECISION ARCHITECTURE

The subject's decision process is unusual in the operator population because it is **morally pre-committed and tactically deferred** — the inverse of the typical special-operations cycle, which is tactically pre-committed and morally deferred. Across forty years of writers (Stern, Gruenwald, Waid, Brubaker, Hickman, Coates), the same sequence is visible:

1. **Moral framing first.** Before any tactical analysis, the subject silently resolves the *non-negotiables* of the operation — who must not be harmed, what principle is on the table, what he will and will not do regardless of cost. This is often visible as a brief, unscripted pause (Hickman *Avengers* repeatedly stages it as a single panel of him standing apart from the table). The non-negotiables are then communicated as **constraints, not preferences** — he will say "Civilians don't get touched" in a tone that admits no negotiation, then yield on almost every other parameter.
2. **Intelligence absorption.** He listens longer than he speaks. Across the Brubaker run he is repeatedly shown letting Sharon Carter, Sam Wilson, or Natasha brief him to completion before asking a single question — and his first question is almost always *clarifying*, not *directive* (e.g., "When did the asset last make contact?" rather than "Why didn't you act?"). This is a deliberate operational choice: he is testing the briefer's confidence in their own data.
3. **Plan generation by consultation.** He will solicit a plan from the subordinate most domain-competent (Falcon for aerial; Widow for infiltration; Fury, when trusted, for institutional politics; Stark for systems) and adopt it with modification rather than impose his own. This produces the misperception that he is a follower-leader; in fact he is **arbitrating** plans against the pre-set moral constraints.
4. **Last-to-commit.** He commits to the plan publicly only when satisfied. Once committed, reversal requires either new intelligence or a moral-constraint violation — never social pressure, never escalating cost.
5. **Personal-risk asymmetry.** In every operational plan, the slot most likely to take fire or absorb consequence is the slot he assigns himself. This is so consistent that subordinates have learned to plan around it (Wilson, in particular, in the Remender and Spencer runs, openly counter-plans for it).

**The signature anomaly.** When the moral constraint and the tactical optimum diverge, he abandons the tactical optimum without visible internal conflict. This is the *Civil War* surrender (*Captain America* #25, 2007): tactically he was winning the street fight; the moment civilians became collateral he stopped fighting and yielded. The decision is not weighed in the moment; it was pre-weighed in step one. To outside observers it looks like an impulsive collapse; it is in fact the cleanest possible execution of an instruction he gave himself an hour earlier.

---

## 6.2 TRADECRAFT — KINETIC

**Hand-to-hand.** A boxer's frame under a wrestler's footwork, layered with judo and jiu-jitsu picked up across decades from instructors (Natasha being the most visible, Brubaker era). He fights *forward* — closes distance, denies range, controls the inside line — because his instrument (the shield) is optimized for short-to-medium engagement and because the pre-serum boxer in him never learned to retreat. He pulls strikes by default. The **tell** is the half-beat hesitation before a finishing blow against a human opponent; against the Skull, against Hydra leadership, the hesitation vanishes, and adversaries who know him use the presence or absence of that hesitation to gauge his moral read on them.

**The shield.** Treated less as a weapon than as an *extension of decision-making*. Used (in roughly descending frequency across canon): as bunker for non-combatants, as deflector, as blunt impact, as thrown projectile with calculated ricochet, and only marginally as edge weapon. The thrown shield is **never** his opening move against a person; almost always his opening move against a structure (door, vehicle, weapon emplacement). The ricochet geometry is internalized to the degree that he routinely calls shots off three or four surfaces in a single throw — a level of physics-modeling competence that, in a non-enhanced operator, would require a tablet. He has been shown computing the throws on **acoustically reflected** environmental cues when blinded (Brubaker, vol. 5 #27 era).

**Firearms.** Competent across WWII-vintage and modern small arms; pistol-trained; will use a long gun when the mission demands it. Does **not** carry one by default. The reluctance is not squeamishness — he killed extensively in WWII and has continued to in extremis — but tradecraft: the shield encodes a posture (defense first, harm shaped and limited), the rifle encodes a different one (lethal projection at distance), and he prefers the posture his iconography promises. When he does carry, he carries spare magazines in the same pouch configuration every time; the muscle memory is decades old.

**Vehicle and platform.** Motorcycle is the signature — he has ridden one in every era since reactivation (the canonical preference for an older, lower-tech bike across the Gruenwald and Brubaker runs is operationally meaningful: serviceable in the field, no electronics to disable, parts substitutable). Trained on parachute, glider, fixed-wing, rotary, small watercraft; combat-drives most ground vehicles to a professional standard; pilots the quinjet under necessity but yields the stick to Wilson or Stark whenever possible. Avoids platforms whose operation he does not personally understand to maintainable depth.

**Environmental.** Extraordinary use of cover and improvisation; will weaponize what is in arm's reach (fire extinguisher, length of pipe, a thrown desk chair) rather than draw an issued weapon. The pre-serum street-fighter is preserved in this: the kid who lost alley fights learned that the kid who picks up the trashcan lid wins the next one.

---

## 6.3 TRADECRAFT — NON-KINETIC

**Reconnaissance.** Old-school. Walks the ground. Across the Brubaker run, the modus before any operation in an unfamiliar city is to spend hours on foot through the area — he reads buildings, exits, sightlines, civilian density, traffic flow, in person, and prefers to do it alone. He sketches. (See **6.6 below** on the notebook.) He cross-references the sketch against whatever digital intelligence the team has produced; if the two disagree, he trusts the sketch.

**Surveillance and counter-surveillance.** Competent but not exceptional at electronic counter-surveillance — he delegates to Widow, Wilson, or Stark. Exceptional at human counter-surveillance: he reads a tail the way most operators read a satellite feed. The relevant **tell** for those tracking him: he does not break tails by losing them in crowds; he breaks them by **stopping**, turning, and walking back toward the surveillant in a way calibrated to force a decision. Most break first.

**Interrogation.** Non-coercive, with patience as the instrument. Will sit with a prisoner for hours. Uses silence the way other interrogators use pressure. The Brubaker run shows him obtaining information from Crossbones and from a series of Hydra mid-rankers by simple presence and unflinching eye contact. He does not lie to prisoners; he will withhold and frame, but the absence of outright lying is itself a tradecraft choice — it preserves his usable credibility for future engagements and, more importantly, preserves his own moral self-concept.

**Negotiation.** Direct, brief, and clearly bounded. Names his constraints in the opening minute. Does not bluff. Does not posture. Will walk away from a deal that violates his moral pre-commitments, even at extreme cost, which makes him paradoxically a strong negotiator: counterparties learn that his stated positions are real and stop testing them.

**Disguise and cover.** Operationally weak at it relative to his peer set, and he knows it. The body is recognizable even out of uniform; the bearing is harder to mask than the face. He compensates by **deletion** rather than **fabrication** — civilian clothes chosen for forgettability (dark Henley, work jacket, jeans, boots; the Brubaker–Epting house style codified the look), a ball cap pulled low, a paperback or a coffee in the hand to redirect the eye. He does not attempt accents or alternate identities at length. He has used the alias *Steve Grant* and a handful of others under necessity (Gruenwald) but is uncomfortable inside them and tends to surface.

**Communications.** Disciplined. Brief, clear, non-redundant on radio. Uses callsigns. Does not narrate. Maintains comms silence on the move and breaks it only on contact or completion. In personal communication he is a **letter writer** — a recurring detail across multiple writers, especially Brubaker and Coates — and the letters tend to be hand-written, brief, formal in salutation, warm in body. The hand-written letter is partly a tradecraft instinct (no metadata, no archive on someone else's server) and partly an era-of-origin preference left intact.

---

## 6.4 INFRASTRUCTURE & RESOURCES

**Lodging.** Across eras, his residential pattern is consistently **modest, functional, defensible, and replaceable.** Brooklyn brownstone apartment (Brubaker era); various safe rooms inside Avengers facilities; an apartment above an art-supply shop or a dojo at various points; austere quarters within the Triskelion when SHIELD-aligned. He owns very little. The furniture across renderings is sparse: bed, desk, single armchair, footlocker, a bookshelf, a heavy bag in a corner or a doorframe pull-up bar. Walls largely bare; the exceptions are a photograph or two (Sarah Rogers in some renderings; the Howling Commandos group photo; Peggy) and a small flag. The pattern is monastic without aestheticizing the monasticism.

**Finances.** A pension and back-pay structure exists in canon (Waid *Man Out of Time*, Brubaker references) — he has decades of accumulated military pay, frequently invested or held in trust. He spends little. Across runs he is shown declining endorsement money, foundation honoraria, and government stipends in excess of need; what he accepts he routes to others (veterans' organizations, the families of fallen Avengers, in one Gruenwald arc directly into Rick Jones's medical bills). He is not ascetic about money in others — he tolerates Stark wealth without judgment — but for himself, accumulation is a category error.

**Equipment.** The shield (issued / re-issued; he has lost and recovered or replaced it across multiple arcs but is recognizably the same instrument to him). Uniform — multiple configurations across eras, but the underlying constants are: thin armor against blade and small-arms, mobility-first cut, the iconography intact. He maintains his own kit. Cleans the shield by hand. The uniform is folded, not hung, and stored in the same footlocker pattern across decades. Spare gloves and boots are pre-broken-in and rotated.

**Transport.** Motorcycle as primary; Avengers transport when the operation requires it; commercial transport when civilian-cover is preferable. He keeps cash in small denominations and a folding knife in every coat.

**Institutional resources.** Variable across eras but he has held call-on access to: Avengers logistics, SHIELD assets (when not in active opposition to them), Wakandan support (Coates era; the relationship with T'Challa is treated as personal as much as institutional), the network of surviving Howling Commandos and their descendants, Nick Fury Sr. and Jr. as individuals rather than as offices, and an informal civilian network — VA volunteers, neighborhood priests, a barber, the diner staff — that he cultivates as much for friendship as for tradecraft. The civilian network is operationally significant: he can disappear into Brooklyn or Lower Manhattan inside an hour by walking through doors held by people who like him.

**Document and identity infrastructure.** Possesses, but rarely uses, the document set SHIELD/Avengers cover-ops would maintain. Disinclined to operate under false papers in any sustained way. When he goes off-grid (post-*Civil War* fugitive period; the Nomad arc; the Captain arc) he goes off-grid in his own person, accepting the risk, rather than constructing a sustainable alternate identity.

---

## 6.5 OPERATIONAL PATTERNS

**Tempo.** Slow start, fast middle, clean end. He does not improvise the opening of an operation; he over-prepares it. Once initiated, his action rate is among the highest in his peer set — sustained for hours without degradation. Conclusion is **abrupt**: when the objective is met he disengages and walks off the operation rather than lingering. The disengagement walk is a signature: he does not collect trophies, does not press the press, does not stay for the photograph.

**Mentoring under operation.** Routinely uses operations as instructional contexts. Will assign a junior operator the second-most-difficult role rather than the third or fourth, with himself in the first, and conduct after-action review afterward with a teacher's patience. This is documented with Bucky in WWII flashbacks, Rick Jones in the Silver Age, Sam Wilson across decades, John Walker post-rehabilitation, Bucky-as-Winter-Soldier in his recovery, Ian Rogers in *Dimension Z*, and the rotating juniors he passes through in the Hickman *Avengers* era.

**Failure mode.** When operations go wrong, his first move is to assume responsibility — publicly and immediately — even where causation is shared. The pattern is so consistent that allies (Wilson, Widow) have learned to **pre-empt the assumption** by getting on the record first. The deeper operational fact is that he is not performing humility; he genuinely believes the senior officer is responsible for outcomes, and he is almost always the senior officer.

**Counter-pattern.** Adversaries who study him exploit two reliable levers:
- **Non-combatant hostage scenarios** — he reorients the whole operation around the hostage and can be moved off-objective with arithmetic certainty. Zemo, the Skull, and Hydra mid-rankers across runs all use this.
- **Moral inversions** — operations designed to make his iconography the instrument of harm (Walker as Captain America; Hydra-Steve; the *Secret Empire* event in aggregate; the Commission removal in Gruenwald). These do not break him but they exact disproportionate cost in delay, internal contention, and grief.

A third lever — **isolation from his unit** — is partially effective in the short term and fails in the long term. He functions alone better than expected, but the absence is felt as moral as well as operational, and he will route toward reconstituting a unit at the first opportunity.

---

## 6.6 SIGNATURE TELLS

A long-running surveillance file would log the following recurrent micro-patterns:

- **The notebook.** A small bound sketchbook, soft-cover, in a jacket pocket. Pencil. Used for tactical sketches, terrain notes, and — across the same pages — portrait studies of teammates, civilians, the dead. The notebook is the single most diagnostic personal object he carries: it bridges the operator and the artist, and it is the closest thing to a journal he keeps. When he is unobserved at rest he is often drawing.
- **The pause before speaking.** Half a beat longer than the norm. Reliable. Frequently misread as slowness; in fact a moral-screening filter.
- **The room scan.** On entering any unfamiliar interior: exits, elevations, civilians, then threats. Order is invariant. Takes under two seconds and is usually missed by the observed.
- **The standing posture.** Square, hands loosely at sides or one resting on a forearm — not military-rigid, not civilian-slouched. Distinct enough that those who know him identify him at distance from the silhouette alone.
- **The eye-contact hold.** Longer than American conversational norm; not aggressive. Communicates that he is listening and that he expects to be listened to.
- **Coffee black, taken throughout the day, no apparent stimulant effect** (serum). Reliable cue that he is reading a room for longer than he intends to be seen reading it: he buys a cup, sits, nurses it.
- **Diet.** Enormous caloric intake (serum metabolism) handled discreetly. He does not perform the appetite. Will eat what is offered. Returns to the same diners and is known to staff by first name (Brubaker era Brooklyn diner is repeatedly staged).
- **Sleep.** Light, brief, interruptible. Wakes fully alert. Does not nap.
- **Physical fidgets, near-absent.** No drumming, no shifting, no pen-clicking. Stillness is its own tell — operators in surveillance feeds have used it to ID him in crowds.
- **The shield-strap adjustment.** Pre-engagement: a single, unconscious tug at the forearm strap. He does it before every committed action. He does not know he does it.
- **The salute.** Rare. Reserved. When he gives one in the modern era, it is given fully and held longer than regulation — usually to a fallen ally or to a flag at a funeral. The rarity makes it operationally legible.
- **The walk-off.** After a mission concludes: a slow, unaccompanied walk away from the scene, often with the shield slung. He does not look back. The walk is when the grief moves; observers have reported, across runs, that this is the only window in which his face is unguarded.
- **The visit.** A recurrent ritual — the trip to Peggy's care facility; periodic visits to graves (Erskine, Sarah Rogers in the older Brooklyn cemetery, the Howling Commandos plots, in *Reborn*-era continuity to his own former grave). The visits are unpublicized, off-schedule from any operational tempo, and conducted alone. They are the closest thing in his behavioral record to **scheduled mourning**, and they are the operational counterweight that allows the rest of the life to continue functioning.
- **The letter.** Hand-written. Same blue ink across decades (a long-standing fountain-pen preference, intermittently shown). Salutation formal, body warm, closing always with first name only. Sent to bereaved families after operations in which the family member died.
- **The refusal.** A short, level "No." delivered without elaboration when asked to violate the pre-set moral constraints. The absence of justification is itself the tell: he does not argue principles because he does not regard them as arguable. Interlocutors consistently report that the refusal lands harder than rhetoric.

---

## 6.7 SYNTHESIS — HOW HE OPERATES

The subject operates as a **morally pre-committed, tactically humble, infrastructure-light, unit-oriented field commander** whose tradecraft is organized around three asymmetries: he absorbs more personal risk than he assigns, he is more patient than his adversaries, and he is more legible than his peers. The legibility is not a vulnerability — it is the operational expression of the same ethical posture that defines the psychology: a man whose word, posture, and constraint are public, fixed, and identical across decades, because the only stable counter to the world's volatility he has ever trusted is his own continuity.

He is a difficult subject to surveil productively because the file fills, eventually, with the same data: the same diners, the same routes, the same notebook, the same letter going out to one more family, the same walk away from one more scene. The pattern is the person. The tradecraft and the grief are the same instrument.

— *End Section 6. Continues at Section 7: Network & Relational Map.*


## Worldview & Operational Code

# WORLDVIEW & OPERATIONAL CODE — ROGERS, STEVEN GRANT

*Stage 2, Section: Worldview & Operational Code. Derived from the longitudinal record summarized in Stage 1; conclusions are inferential where canon is silent and substantiated where canon is explicit.*

---

## I. METAPHYSICAL FLOOR — WHAT HE BELIEVES IS REAL

Before any political or tactical commitment, Rogers operates from a small set of un-argued ontological assumptions. He has never published them; they are inferable from sixty years of consistent behavior under writers who disagreed about almost everything else.

**Goods and harms are real, not constructed.** Cruelty is not a matter of perspective; it is a fact about the world that obtains independent of what any culture says about it. This is not philosophical sophistication — Rogers has no patience for, and limited training in, formal ethics — but it is unmistakably a moral-realist intuition, the only one that makes sense of his behavior. He treats the suffering of a stranger as carrying the same prima facie claim on his action as the suffering of a friend, modified only by what he can actually do. This is why he reads as old-fashioned: he genuinely does not believe morality is downstream of culture, and he is mildly puzzled by sophisticates who do.

**Persons are ends, never merely means.** He has never been observed instrumentalizing a non-combatant for a strategic goal, and the cases where he has been pressured to (Civil War's Registration logic; Hickman's Illuminati confrontation over the Incursions) are precisely the cases where he becomes implacable. The Kantian formulation is not his — he would phrase it as "you don't trade people" — but the operational content is identical.

**History is real and the dead are owed.** This is the deepest and least negotiable of his metaphysical commitments. The past is not a foreign country to be visited; it is a creditor. The dead made choices whose consequences the living inhabit, and the living owe them — at minimum — the honesty of not pretending they did not exist.
