This is why his Brooklyn idioms, his Forties cadences, and his refusal to update certain pieces of language are not affectation: they are debt service.

**The world is improvable but not perfectible.** He is not a utopian. He believes specific harms can be reduced, specific institutions repaired, specific people saved, and that this work is worth doing without any expectation that the work ends. He is, in this sense, the closest thing the canon has to a genuinely Niebuhrian Christian sensibility — though the religious vocabulary is rarely surfaced — combined with a Depression-era pragmatism: you don't ask whether the bread line ends; you ask whether you can feed the next person.

**Power is morally neutral; its uses are not.** He distrusts the romance of power and the romance of powerlessness in equal measure. Power that protects is good; power that dominates is evil; the same instrument can do both, often in the same hour. He therefore reasons about *uses*, not about *possessions*, which is why his disagreements with Stark and with Fury are never about whether the technology should exist but about who gets to deploy it and against whom.

---

## II. READING OF THE WORLD — HOW HE SEES THE FIELD

**The world is contested but not adversarial by default.** Rogers does not assume malice. He assumes scarcity, fear, miscommunication, institutional inertia, and the occasional bad actor. The corollary is operationally important: he gives ground to people he disagrees with longer than other operators would, because he genuinely believes most antagonists are recoverable. His record on second chances (Walker, Bucky-as-Winter-Soldier, the various redeemed Avengers) is the behavioral signature of this belief. The cases where he writes a person off (Red Skull, late-stage Zemo, Hydra leadership) are cases where he has accumulated evidence that the person *chooses* the harm — not merely commits it.

**Most evil is structural, not personal.** This is a reading he arrived at slowly, across decades, and most explicitly under the Coates run, but it is consistent with behavior much earlier. He treats Hydra, AIM, the Commission, and the corrupted intelligence apparatus as systems that select for certain personalities and amplify their worst features; individuals within those systems are partly culpable and partly captured. The operational consequence: he prefers to dismantle the system rather than execute the operator, and he is willing to accept that the operator may need to be tried by lawful process rather than dispatched. He is, in this narrow sense, a procedural liberal.

**Civilians are not background.** His tactical field-of-view is unusual in the superhero canon: he tracks bystanders by default, and the bystander count is a permanent input into his decision function. This is why he is bad at fights in empty warehouses (he is unsettled by the absence of stakes) and unusually good at fights in subways, schools, and city blocks. Other operators treat civilians as a constraint; he treats them as the point.

**Institutions are instruments, not ends.** SHIELD, the Army, the Avengers, the Commission, the Presidency itself — these are tools. Their authority is contingent on their use. He will follow lawful orders, salute the flag, testify before Congress, and accept demotion or removal — until the institution asks him to do harm, at which point his loyalty reverts to the principle the institution was supposed to instantiate. He does not experience this as disloyalty; he experiences it as the institution having betrayed *itself*, and himself as the one still keeping faith.

**America is two things at once.** A country — a specific landmass, history, population, set of institutions, with a specific record of cruelty and aspiration — and an idea, which the country has often failed and sometimes embodied. He is loyal to the idea and patient with the country. He does not confuse them. This is the single most under-read feature of his politics by both his admirers and his critics: he is not a nationalist, and the symbol on his chest is, in his own mind, an aspirational pledge rather than a tribal marker. He has been visibly distressed across multiple runs (Englehart, Gruenwald, Rieber, Coates) when the public reads him the other way.

**Time is not linear in the way it is for others.** This is a private epistemology built from the ice. He knows experientially that the world he grew up in is gone and that the world he now operates in will, in turn, be gone. This produces a peculiar long view: he does not catastrophize present crises (he has seen worse) and does not romanticize present comforts (he has seen better, and seen them lost). The affect is unusually steady because the time-horizon is unusually wide.

---

## III. CONFLICT — HOW HE THINKS ABOUT FIGHTING

**Violence is a tool of last resort that he is unusually competent at.** Rogers does not enjoy combat. He does not refuse it, fear it, or dramatize it; he uses it. The most consistent behavioral tell across writers is the *de-escalation attempt* — verbal, postural, the lowered shield, the open palm, the offer of surrender to the opponent — that precedes nearly every fight he initiates and many he is dragged into. He is the only major Avenger who routinely asks adversaries to stand down before engaging. He does this not because he expects it to work but because the offer is itself a moral act.

**Proportionality is non-negotiable.** He calibrates force to threat with a precision that other operators do not match. He does not maim when subduing will do; he does not subdue when restraining will do; he does not restrain when talking will do. The exception is when civilians are imminently threatened, in which case the calibration jumps to whatever ends the threat fastest with the least collateral. This is the closest thing he has to a fight algorithm: *minimum force at maximum speed, calibrated to civilian exposure.*

**Killing is permitted but never casual.** Canon is split on whether and when he kills; the synthesis across runs is that he will kill when there is no alternative that saves a life he is obligated to save, and that he counts every such kill. He does not euphemize. He does not console himself with the language of "neutralization." When he killed Erskine's assassin in 1941 he killed a man; he has carried that knowledge since. He treats lethal force as a debit against his own ledger, not against the target's.

**He fights forward.** Tactically: he closes distance. Psychologically: he absorbs danger toward himself and away from the unit. This is partly serum-licensed (he can take what others cannot) and partly the encoded behavior of the boy who would not stay down in the alley. The pattern is so consistent it is exploitable, and adversaries who have studied him (Zola, Zemo, the Red Skull) routinely use the predictability of his forward-motion to set traps. He knows this and does it anyway, because the alternative — letting someone else absorb the hit — is not available to his moral architecture.

**He fights cleanly until cleanness costs lives, then he fights dirty.** This is the feature most often missed by his critics. Rogers is not a knight; he is a soldier who prefers knightly forms and abandons them without ceremony when the situation requires. He will improvise, deceive, ambush, exploit asymmetries, and — in extremis — break the rules of the engagement he himself proposed. The constraint is *outcomes for the protected*, not *aesthetics of the fight*.

**Wars end. Conflicts metabolize.** He believes — and this is downstream of having watched the Second World War end and the world reorganize — that no conflict is permanent and that the post-conflict phase is the part that actually matters. He is unusually attentive to the question of what happens to defeated adversaries, displaced populations, captured operators, and broken institutions after the fight. He is bad at celebrating victories because he is already thinking about the next failure mode.

---

## IV. CONTROL — POWER, AUTHORITY, COMMAND

**Authority is conditional and revocable.** Rogers respects rank, chain of command, lawful order, electoral mandate, judicial process — until they instruct him to do harm, at which point the authority evaporates and only the underlying obligation remains. He does not experience this as anti-authoritarian; he experiences it as authority having mis-described itself. The Nomad arc, the Captain arc, the Civil War insurgency, and his repeated public confrontations with the Commission, the Pentagon, and the executive branch all instantiate the same algorithm: *obey until obedience harms; then refuse, publicly, and accept the cost.*

**Command is a burden, not a privilege.** He leads when no one else will, leads better when someone else could, and steps aside without performance when a peer can carry the load. The succession of Sam Wilson is the clean canonical case: he hands over the shield without staging, hands it back when asked, and does not treat either transition as a wound. He is missing the ego-component that makes command addictive in others. His attachment is to the *work*, not to the role.

**Self-command is the only command that fully obligates him.** This is the deep architecture under all the others. He believes — and the Streets of Poison arc is the demonstration he carries with him — that the only durable check on power is the operator's own inhibitory mastery. External constraints fail; institutions fail; allies fail; only the operator's continuous decision not to misuse what he has been given is reliable. This is why he is so afraid of intoxication, mind-control, and the Hydra-Steve restoration: they represent the loss of the only safeguard he genuinely trusts.

**He distrusts surveillance, registration, and predictive force.** Not because he is libertarian — he is not — but because he has watched every iteration of "we will preempt the harm by knowing in advance" produce the harm it was meant to prevent. His position on Registration is not a position about superheroes; it is a position about the architecture of preemption. He is willing to be known; he is unwilling to be databased, because databases get inherited by the worst person who eventually controls them. This is the closest he has to a structural political conviction.

**He accepts oversight when it is honest.** He has testified before Congress, submitted to investigation, accepted demotion, accepted public censure. The condition is that the oversight be conducted by parties whose interest is the integrity of the work rather than the control of the operator. Where that condition fails (the Commission; the post-Registration apparatus; the corrupted SHIELD), he withdraws cooperation. The discrimination is fine-grained and he attends to it carefully.

**Hierarchy is provisional; dignity is not.** He treats janitors, generals, and gods with the same baseline courtesy and the same baseline expectation of conduct. He does not perform deference upward or condescension downward. This is observed across every writer who has handled him and is one of the few features of his behavior on which the canon is unanimous.

---

## V. TRUST — HOW IT IS EXTENDED, WITHDRAWN, REPAIRED

**Trust is extended quickly and provisionally.** Rogers' default posture toward a new person is mild, attentive openness. He does not require demonstrations of loyalty to grant baseline trust; he requires the absence of evidence of bad faith. This makes him easy to work with and occasionally exploitable. He knows this and has decided the cost is acceptable, because the alternative — defaulting to suspicion — would corrode the cooperative work he believes is the point.

**Trust deepens through shared cost, not shared success.** The people he is most attached to are people who have bled with him, lost with him, or been wrong with him in ways they then corrected. Bucky, Sam, Natasha, Sharon, Tony (pre-Civil War), Fury (conditionally), T'Challa: the bonds are calibrated by what was endured together, not what was achieved. He does not bond well over victories; he bonds over the weight that survived them.

**Trust is withdrawn slowly and reluctantly.** When an ally fails him, his first move is to investigate the failure as a problem to be repaired. Walker is the cleanest case: a man who replaced him, defamed him by association, and committed atrocities under his iconography, and whom he then trained, forgave, and reinstated. The pattern is not naïveté; it is a conviction that people are mostly recoverable and that writing them off is a moral cost he should not absorb unless he must.

**Trust, once fully withdrawn, is not casually restored.** The Red Skull, late Zemo, the Hydra leadership, and — across long stretches of Civil War and its aftermath — Tony Stark, are the cases. The withdrawal is quiet, complete, and operationally consequential: he will work alongside them where the mission requires, will not betray them, will not be cruel, but will not extend the cooperative latitude that made the prior relationship possible. He is capable of repair when the other party initiates it credibly (Tony, eventually); he does not initiate it himself for parties who have proven, by sustained behavior, that the trust is not safely placed.

**Self-trust is the asset he is most careful with.** He audits his own judgment constantly. He second-guesses calls he made and is unusually willing to revise positions when shown new evidence — but he distinguishes sharply between revising a position because the evidence changed and revising a position because the pressure rose. The latter he refuses categorically. His self-trust is therefore high but not arrogant: he believes his judgment is *worked on*, and that the work justifies the use.

---

## VI. RISK — WHAT HE WILL SPEND, AND WHEN

**He spends himself first.** The risk-allocation rule is unambiguous and observed across every era: his own body, his own reputation, his own freedom, and his own life are the first resources committed to any operation. This is partly serum-licensed and partly the encoded survivor-guilt logic that he has no business preserving himself while others bleed. It is the basis of the persistent misread that he is suicidal; the differential — established in Stage 1 — is that he spends himself *toward* others' survival, never *away from* his own.

**He spends allies reluctantly and explicitly.** When an operation requires others to take mortal risk, he names the risk, gives the option to refuse, and carries the moral weight of the order personally. He does not euphemize. He does not pretend a dangerous assignment is routine. He does not invoke duty to bypass consent. This is observed cleanly in his command of the Invaders, the Howlers (by proxy through Fury), the early Avengers, and the post-Civil War insurgency.

**He will not spend civilians.** The line is bright and load-bearing. He surrendered publicly on the courthouse steps in Civil War precisely because the alternative was civilian casualties; the surrender was not a tactical defeat but the activation of an absolute rule. Adversaries who put civilians in the line of fire to constrain him are using a real constraint, and he knows it, and he accepts the cost rather than break the rule.

**He accepts asymmetric risk for asymmetric stakes.** He will take a near-certain mortal risk to save a small number of people; he will take a near-certain mortal risk to prevent a structural harm (Hydra resurgence, mass-casualty weapon, institutional corruption). The asymmetry is not vanity; it is a calculation about what the world looks like if he conserves himself versus if he spends himself. He has, repeatedly across canon, decided that the world is better with the spending.

**He is risk-averse about irreversible institutional changes.** This is the under-read corollary. He is reckless with his body and conservative with structure. He resists changes — to law, to constitutional architecture, to the rules of engagement, to the configuration of oversight — that cannot be undone if they go wrong. His Civil War position is best read this way: not as a refusal of accountability but as a refusal of an architecture whose failure modes were not reversible. He thinks several iterations ahead about institutional change in a way he does not bother to about tactical action.

---

## VII. HIS PLACE — HOW HE LOCATES HIMSELF

**He is a custodian, not a principal.** This is the self-concept under the iconography. He does not believe Captain America is *him*; he believes Captain America is a promise that he is currently the one carrying, and that the carrying is a service to people who made the promise possible — Erskine, Sarah, Bucky, the cohort, the country at its better moments. The serum was loaned; the identity is loaned; the life past 1945 is, in some real sense, loaned. He acts accordingly: with the care of a borrower, not the entitlement of an owner.

**He is the last of his generation and he knows it.** This is the existential register beneath the operational one. Across the modern runs, increasingly, he is written as the unit's elder — the one others grieve to, the one who remembers what was lost, the one who carries the institutional memory the institutions themselves have forgotten. He accepts this role without performance and without complaint. It is, in his reading, simply the job that has fallen to him because he is the one still here.

**He is replaceable in role and irreplaceable in obligation.** He believes the role of Captain America can and should be carried by others (Bucky, Sam, in some configurations Isaiah Bradley's heirs) and that the *function* of protecting the vulnerable is distributed across many actors. What is not replaceable is *his specific obligation* to the dead, which no successor can discharge for him. He can hand over the shield; he cannot hand over the debt. This is why, even after handing the shield to Sam, he remained operationally engaged: the work continued because the debt continued.

**He is not the symbol; he is the worker behind the symbol.** He is conscious of the symbol as a tool — for morale, for coalition, for the legitimation of right action — and he uses it deliberately. He does not confuse himself with it, and he is mildly embarrassed when others do. The Rieber and Coates runs both surface this: he is most himself when he is talking to a single person in a kitchen, not when he is addressing a stadium.

**He is in debt and the debt is the meaning.** This is the deepest layer. He does not experience the survivor-guilt structure as a problem to be solved; he experiences it as the engine that keeps him oriented. To "process" the grief in a way that lifted the debt would be, in his reading, to default. He would rather carry it forever. He has, in fact, organized eighty subjective years around the carrying. This is not psychological health in any conventional sense and it is not psychological pathology in any reducible one; it is the load-bearing architecture of a life that has decided what it is for.

---

## VIII. OPERATIONAL CODE — THE WORKING RULES

The following are not stated by Rogers anywhere in canon as a list. They are the implicit decision-rules that recover his behavior across sixty years of writers. They are offered as the operational distillation of the worldview above.

1. **Protect the vulnerable. This overrides everything else, including lawful order, personal safety, and tactical advantage.**
2. **Honor the dead by doing the work they cannot do. The debt is non-discharging; act accordingly.**
3. **De-escalate first, every time, even when it will not work. The offer is itself a moral act.**
4. **Calibrate force to threat. Minimum force at maximum speed, weighted by civilian exposure.**
5. **Kill only when no alternative saves a life you are obligated to save. Count every one.**
6. **Spend yourself before you spend anyone else. Name the risk when you must spend allies. Never spend civilians.**
7. **Obey lawful authority until obedience harms. Then refuse, publicly, and accept the cost.**
8. **Treat institutions as instruments. Loyalty is to the principle the institution was meant to instantiate.**
9. **Distrust preemption, surveillance, and registration. Databases are inherited by the worst person who eventually controls them.**
10. **Extend trust quickly; withdraw it slowly; restore it carefully when warranted; do not perform either generosity or suspicion.**
11. **Audit your own judgment. Revise for evidence; never for pressure.**
12. **Never default to certainty about your own righteousness. The Walker case proves what righteousness uninspected becomes.**
13. **Inhibitory mastery is the only safeguard you fully control. Guard it phobically.**
14. **Be replaceable in role. Be irreplaceable only in obligation.**
15. **Think several iterations ahead about institutional change. Be reckless with your body and conservative with structure.**
16. **Bear witness when you cannot win. Embody the principle in your own person so it survives the institution.**
17. **Repair where repair is possible. Forgive where forgiveness is earned. Write off only on sustained evidence that the person chooses the harm.**
18. **The work does not end. Do not look for the finish line; do not require victories to continue. The next person needs the next thing done.**

---

## IX. WHERE THE CODE STRAINS

No operational code is internally consistent under all conditions. Rogers' is unusually robust but has documented stress fractures.

**Protect-the-vulnerable versus distrust-preemption.** When the prevented harm is mass-casualty, the preemption rule strains. He has, in canon, accepted preemptive action (the Illuminati confrontations, certain Hydra operations) and afterward treated his own acceptance as a moral cost. The strain is real and he carries it rather than resolving it.

**Bear-witness versus protect-the-vulnerable.** When embodying the principle requires losing the fight, and losing the fight means people die, the rule order becomes unstable. Civil War is the canonical case; he resolved it by surrender, which honored both rules at the price of the larger political objective. He has not always resolved it this way; the resolution is case-by-case and costs him.

**Forgive-where-earned versus honor-the-dead.** Bucky-as-Winter-Soldier is the test. Bucky killed under coercion; the dead Bucky killed are still dead; the debt to them and the debt to Bucky pull opposite directions. Rogers chose Bucky, and has carried the partial default to the other dead as part of the ongoing weight. He does not pretend the conflict was clean.

**Self-command versus the serum.** The serum that made him legible is also a constant low-grade reminder that his moral architecture rests on a biological substrate he did


## Moral & Ethical Framework

# MORAL & ETHICAL FRAMEWORK — ROGERS, S.G.

*Stage 2 Synthesis, Section 4 of dossier. Derived from longitudinal behavioral record; ethical content reconstructed from enacted conduct, not declarations.*

---

## 1. PROVENANCE OF THE CODE

The code is older than the uniform. It is older than the serum. It was constructed in a Lower East Side tenement by a sick boy watching his mother work double shifts in a sanatorium while his father drank and struck her, and refined in alleys behind theaters and bakeries where he stood between bullies and smaller children and was beaten for the courtesy. This pre-history matters because it establishes that Rogers' ethics are not the *product* of his power; his power is the *instrument* of an ethics already complete in form by approximately age fifteen. Erskine selected him because he had read the structure correctly: a small man who already understood the moral physics of strength would, when given strength, not have to learn anything new — only do, at scale, what he had always done.

The code is therefore not deontological in derivation (he did not read Kant; he barely finished high school before the war) but *experiential-deontological in operation*: a set of rules-of-the-hand that have the deontologist's absoluteness because they were paid for in childhood blood and never up for renegotiation. He cannot argue you out of them because he did not argue himself into them.

---

## 2. THE OPERATIVE RULES — WHAT HE WILL DO

**Place his body between harm and a non-combatant, without deliberation, every time.** This is the foundational reflex and the most over-determined behavior in the canon. It precedes thought; he is in motion before the calculus is run. It applies regardless of the non-combatant's affiliation, nationality, politics, or prior conduct toward him — he has shielded Hydra civilians, Skrull refugees, hostile journalists, and people who have actively tried to kill him in prior issues. The rule does not care.

**Refuse a lawful order that requires harming an innocent.** The Registration crisis is the canonical demonstration, but the disposition is older — Englehart's #176 (the Nomad turn), Gruenwald's removal-by-Commission (#332), and Brubaker's Civil War lead are iterations of the same refusal-logic. He will resign, defect, go fugitive, or surrender his name before he will execute such an order. He has never once made an exception, across writers, when the order was clean and the harm was clear.

**Take the hit himself when the hit is coming for someone else.** Not as bravado; as accounting. He has decades of evidence that his body absorbs damage that would kill others, and he treats that asymmetry as an obligation, not a privilege. He will dive on a grenade he could throw, take a bullet he could deflect, accept a beating he could end, if doing so spares a non-enhanced person the same experience.

**Honor a commitment past the point of personal cost.** Promises made are debts owed and are not subject to renegotiation when they become inconvenient. The Bucky-search across sixty years of canonical time, the visits to Peggy in the care facility long past the point at which she always recognizes him, the patient rehabilitation of Walker, the rescue of Ian from Dimension Z — all are enactments of the same axiom.

**Yield command, credit, and centrality when better-placed others can carry the work.** He gave the shield to Sam Wilson and meant it. He defers in strategy sessions to specialists (Tony on technology, Natasha on tradecraft, T'Challa on Wakandan ground). He has never refused a successor on grounds of ego. This is rare enough among heroes that it constitutes a diagnostic positive.

**Bear witness.** When he cannot prevent a wrong, he documents it, names it, and stays present to it. He does not leave the room. He attends the funeral. He testifies. He visits the hospital. He carries the body off the field. The presence-in-suffering is itself a moral act for him; he learned it from his mother at bedsides.

---

## 3. THE OPERATIVE RULES — WHAT HE WILL NOT DO

**Kill outside the active battlefield.** In wartime he killed soldiers in soldiering. Out of combat — disarmed enemies, prisoners, the helpless — he does not kill, and he is sharp with subordinates who do. The line is rarely articulated but reliably held. The Red Skull is the recurring stress test: Rogers has had the Skull at his mercy across dozens of issues and has, with one or two writer-aberration exceptions, declined the execution because to take it would be to convert war into murder and concede the Skull's worldview at the moment of the Skull's defeat.

**Torture.** Not for intelligence, not for vengeance, not even for the prevention of mass harm — and he has been offered each of those framings repeatedly. The refusal is total and is one of the few places his usual deliberative style collapses into flat prohibition. Interrogations he runs are pressure-based, theatrical, sometimes deceptive, but the threshold beyond pain is one he will not cross and will physically intervene to prevent in others.

**Lie to those who trust him about things that matter to them.** He will deceive an enemy (he ran covert ops; he has used cover identities; he conducts misdirection on the battlefield), but he will not lie in personal trust contexts. He has told women he was leaving them; he has told allies their plans were wrong to their faces; he has told the Avengers when he was failing them. The cost has been considerable and he has paid it.

**Use his enhanced status to coerce the un-enhanced.** He will not arm-wrestle a civilian into compliance, will not loom, will not raise his voice into a room of frightened people. The somatic discipline around his own size is striking on close observation — he gentles his stance, lowers his shoulders, sits when others sit, places the shield down when entering domestic spaces.

**Take a pharmacological mood agent.** Post–Streets of Poison, he refuses even legal stimulants and alcohol-equivalents formulated to affect his metabolism. The refusal has the structure of a phobia: any compromise of executive control is intolerable because his entire ethical apparatus runs on inhibitory mastery.

**Retire the identity voluntarily under pressure.** He has set the shield down (Nomad, The Captain, post-*Reborn* hand-off to Bucky and to Sam) — but always on his own assessment of fit, never to placate institutional demand. The distinction is precise and load-bearing.

**Comply with surveillance, registration, or pre-emptive control regimes targeting persons not yet accused of wrongdoing.** Across writers and decades, when asked to participate in any apparatus that classifies people by potential threat rather than committed act, he refuses. This is the single most reliable predictor of his political conduct.

---

## 4. JUSTIFICATIONS — HOW HE EXPLAINS HIMSELF, WHEN PRESSED

He does not enjoy ethical argumentation and is not particularly good at it in the philosopher's sense. Pressed, he produces a small repertoire of formulations, repeated across runs with minor variation:

- *"You stand up."* (The originating axiom; pre-serum vintage.)
- *"It's not about me."* (Used to deflect personal cost-benefit framings; usually accurate.)
- *"Then we'll find another way."* (Issued in response to a proposed action he will not endorse; functions as a closing, not an opening, of debate.)
- *"I don't like bullies. I don't care where they're from."* (His one explicit universalizing statement, used sparingly and with care.)
- *"He's my friend."* / *"She's a person."* (His most-used and most diagnostically revealing justification — particularistic, relational, refusing the aggregating frame.)

What is *absent* from the repertoire is also diagnostic: he does not invoke consequences-arithmetic ("the greater good"), does not invoke law-as-such ("it's the rule"), does not invoke God, does not invoke nation, does not invoke duty in the abstract. The framework he reaches for, again and again, is the *person in front of him* — named, particular, owed something specific. He is, in operational ethics, a relational particularist who happens to be in costume as a universal symbol. The tension between those two registers is the engine of most of his political confrontations.

---

## 5. WHERE THE CODE IS CONSISTENT

Across eighty subjective years and dozens of writers, the following commitments do not waver:

- **Asymmetry of harm.** Always trades down: his body for theirs, his name for theirs, his comfort for theirs. Has never reversed.
- **Loyalty to particular persons over loyalty to organizations.** Bucky, Sam, Peggy, Sharon, Tony pre-Civil War, Natasha — when forced to choose between an institutional directive and a friend's life or dignity, the friend wins every time, including when the institution is one he built.
- **Non-coercion of the un-enhanced.** Has never weaponized his physiology against a civilian. Even his use of presence and reputation toward civilians is muted, deferential.
- **Refusal of the surveillance-and-classification state.** Across the Nixon-analog arc, the Commission arc, Registration, the *Secret Empire* aftermath, and the Coates run, his position is unchanged.
- **Mentorship as obligation.** Every successor, every junior, every broken figure he encounters is met with patience and instruction, not gatekeeping.
- **Witness.** He shows up. Funerals, hospitals, hearings, prisons. He has never sent a deputy to bear a grief he could bear personally.

---

## 6. WHERE THE CODE FRACTURES

The fractures are real and worth naming precisely; they are the parts of his ethics that surveillance literature would underline.

**The Bucky exception.** His loyalty to Barnes has, in documented instances, overridden rules he applies inflexibly elsewhere — concealing the Winter Soldier's location from law enforcement (Brubaker), refusing to accept Bucky's accountability for Winter Soldier-era killings as he would accept it from anyone else, granting Bucky the kind of forgiveness-without-process he denies to himself. The exception is consistent — it is *always Bucky* — and the inconsistency is therefore characterological, not random. It is the place where his particularism becomes special pleading. He knows this and has not corrected it; the rule-of-the-hand for Bucky predates the rule-of-the-hand for justice, and it wins.

**Anger and the body of an enemy.** His prohibition on out-of-combat killing has writer-dependent exceptions, almost always involving the Red Skull or Zemo and almost always in moments of grief-cued rage (Bucky-loss, Sharon-loss, civilian-massacre cues). The exceptions are rare, are followed by long periods of self-reckoning, and tend to be retconned as not-quite-fatal, but their existence is the canonical record of where his control can slip. Streets of Poison is the load-bearing data point: with inhibition pharmacologically removed, he is brutal. He fears this about himself and the fear is well-founded.

**The Walker problem — discarded successors.** He has been generous with Walker, Bucky, Sam. He has been *less* generous with Rick Jones at moments, with Nomad-the-younger, with Free Spirit and Jack Flag at the margins of Gruenwald's run, with Ian after *Dimension Z* (the boy is largely written out of his life and Steve does not visibly pursue the relationship). The pattern is not callous, but it is asymmetric: the bonds that are *originally his* (Bucky, Peggy) command infinite maintenance; bonds *thrust upon him* by mission or accident receive less. There is a quiet triage operating beneath the surface of his stated egalitarianism.

**Strategic naïveté as ethical luxury.** He refuses the consequentialist frame, which is to say: he sometimes makes other people pay the cost his refusal generates. He will not torture; the intelligence is therefore not obtained; the people who would have benefited from that intelligence sometimes die. He acknowledges this in private (Brubaker shows him doing so to Sharon, to Sam) and bears it as grief, but he does not change the rule. Whether one regards this as integrity or as the privilege of a man whose conscience is subsidized by others' deaths is one of the genuine ethical questions the canon leaves open. He, characteristically, sides with conscience and pays in grief.

**Institutional loyalty held a beat too long.** He has, repeatedly, given the United States government, SHIELD, and the Avengers institutional structure more benefit of the doubt than the evidence warranted, withdrawing only when the harm became unignorable. The Nomad arc, the Registration arc, *Secret Empire's* aftermath all show the same lag: he hopes the institution will self-correct, gives it room to, and acts only when it does not. The lag has cost. It is the price of his temperamental democracy, but it is a price, and it is paid by people who do not have his serum.

**The asymmetry between public surrender and private continuation.** Civil War: he surrenders publicly, then his cause continues without him by inheritance. The surrender was honest; it was also strategically useful in ways he did not fully disavow. He is not above the implicit politics of his own martyrdom and has, in certain arcs, allowed his deaths and disappearances to do work he would not undertake openly. This is not duplicity in the ordinary sense, but it is a more sophisticated relationship to his own symbolism than he generally admits.

---

## 7. EVOLUTION ACROSS DECADES

The code has not changed in *core*; it has changed substantially in *radius*.

**1941–45 (war).** Tightest radius: protect Allied personnel and Allied civilians, kill Axis combatants, follow lawful orders, treat the war as a moral emergency that licenses what the peace would not. He is essentially a deferential soldier who would never have considered disobedience to legitimate command.

**1964–early 70s (defrost, early Avengers).** The radius widens to include peers in the Avengers and the surrogate-family ethic of the team. Authority is still largely trusted. Nick Fury's SHIELD is treated as continuous with the legitimate command structure he served.

**Mid-70s (Englehart, the Nomad turn).** First major rupture. The Nixon-analog suicide forces him to disentangle the *country* from the *government* and the *symbol* from the *office*. Post-Nomad Rogers is for the first time a man who can imagine refusing the state. The code does not change; its application against the state becomes possible.

**80s (DeMatteis, Gruenwald).** Maturation. He develops his fully articulated position on lethal force (refined in the wake of the Scourge arc and the Bloodstone hunters), his fully articulated position on succession and successors (Walker arc), his fully articulated position on pharmacological control (Streets of Poison). By the end of Gruenwald, the code is canonically complete in its modern form.

**90s.** Distortions of the period (Heroes Reborn, the Liefeld interlude, the *Sentinel of Liberty* reset) leave the code itself untouched. Waid's restoration foregrounds grief as the substrate beneath the ethics in a way prior writers had implied but not stated.

**Post-9/11 (Rieber, Brubaker).** Significant evolution. He works through, on-panel, the distinction between *patriotism as marketing* and *patriotism as practice*, and emerges from it with a sharply more skeptical relationship to American symbolic deployment of his image. He becomes willing, for the first time in his career, to publicly criticize American conduct abroad.

**Civil War and after (Millar, Brubaker, Hickman).** The code's political application reaches its mature form: when the state institutes pre-emptive classification of persons, he opposes; when his opposition will cost civilian lives, he stops fighting and accepts the consequence personally. The Registration sequence is not a deviation from his code; it is its purest articulation.

**Modern (Remender, Spencer, Coates, Aaron).** Two further evolutions. First, *Dimension Z* gives him fatherhood — a moral category he had not previously occupied — and Ian's loss inflects his later mentorship with a deeper, sadder patience. Second, Coates pushes him into explicit reckoning with the gap between his symbol and the country's actual conduct toward its minoritized citizens; his response is to listen more, speak less, and stand visibly with those whom his iconography has historically failed to protect. The code did not change; the *self-awareness about the code* deepened. He understands, by the modern runs, that being a moral exemplar in a flag is a politically loaded condition, and he carries that understanding with the same quiet weight he carries everything else.

**The Hydra interpolation (*Secret Empire*).** Whether read as canon or aberration, the real Steve's response on restoration is the diagnostic: he accepts accountability for actions he did not commit, grieves the harm done in his face and name, and refuses the easy out of disowning the body that committed them. The code, in other words, includes a clause for the impossible: *if my image has done harm, the harm is partly mine to redress regardless of agency*. No prior writer had formalized this clause; the arc forced it into the open. It is consistent with everything else about him.

---

## 8. THE FRAMEWORK IN ONE SENTENCE

He is a relational particularist with universalist iconography, a deontologist by reflex rather than argument, a man whose ethics were complete before his power arrived and whose entire subsequent life has been an attempt to deploy borrowed strength in service of an unrevised promise made by a small boy in an alley who had decided, before he had any reason to believe he would survive it, that he would stand up again.

— *End Section.*


## Communications & Linguistic Profile

# COMMUNICATIONS & LINGUISTIC PROFILE — ROGERS, S.G.

*UATU Dossier, Section 7. Derived from approximately eighty subjective years of recorded speech across the canonical record (Simon/Kirby 1941 → Aaron present), cross-referenced against audio-equivalent panel notation, lettering conventions (italics, ellipses, dash-breaks, voice-over caption), and corroborating witness testimony in adjacent character POVs.*

---

## 1. BASELINE REGISTER — THE STEADY-STATE VOICE

The subject's speaking voice, across writers and decades, sits in a remarkably narrow band: **mid-volume, mid-tempo, low-affect baritone, with consonants articulated more fully than contemporary urban American English typically requires.** Listeners across the canon (Natasha in Brubaker; Sam Wilson across the entire Wilson-as-Falcon and Wilson-as-Cap runs; Tony Stark in the Hickman-era *New Avengers*) remark on the same feature in different idioms: he sounds *older than he is*, *like a radio*, *like someone reading*. The cadence is the cadence of a man who learned English in a tenement where Irish-immigrant inflection (his mother) was still audible and where 1930s newsreel and radio diction was the prestige model. He never fully migrated out of it. The result, in a 21st-century room, is a register that reads simultaneously as formal, slightly archaic, and disarmingly direct — three qualities most contemporary speakers can produce only one of at a time.

**Tempo.** Slow-to-medium, with deliberate pauses before substantive replies. He does not fill silence; silence is a tool he wields against more verbal interlocutors (Stark, Pym, Reed Richards), and it works on them. Witness reports describe a characteristic 1.5–3 second pause before answering a question he considers important — long enough to be conspicuous, short enough not to be hostile. He uses the pause to assemble; he is not a fluent improviser.

**Volume.** Quiet by default. He raises volume for command function (combat orders, crowd address) and almost never for affect. Across the canon there are perhaps a dozen instances of Steve Rogers *shouting in private conversation*; each is diagnostic of acute distress (Bucky-on-the-bridge moments; the courthouse-steps speech to Stark in *Civil War*; the moment of recognition in *Reborn*).

**Pitch.** Stable. He does not modulate upward under stress (the typical anxiety tell); he flattens further. Voice-stress analysis would identify the inverse-of-normal pattern: *as situational stakes rise, prosodic range narrows*. This is one of his most reliable physiological tells (see §9).

---

## 2. DICTION — THE LEXICAL FINGERPRINT

The subject's working vocabulary is large but his preferred vocabulary is small and old. Three lexical strata coexist:

**(a) Depression-era Lower East Side residual.** Persistent across every writer who has bothered to render it. Examples that recur unprompted in his speech: *"swell"* (in moments of unguarded approval; rare after the 1990s but documented as late as Waid's *Man Out of Time* #3); *"pal"* and *"buddy"* (used affectionately with men he likes, almost never with women); *"sore"* meaning angry; *"on the level"*; *"give it the gas"*; *"the dickens"* and *"darn"* and *"heck"* as profanity substitutes; *"fella"*; *"dame"* (which he caught himself using once in the modern era and visibly winced — a documented self-correction); *"the picture show"* (slipped, once, in front of Sam, who teased him for a week). He does not use these words performatively. He uses them because they are the words his mouth produces first when he is not editing.

**(b) Military-operational register.** Acquired 1941–45, never fully decommissioned. *"Copy."* *"On me."* *"Move."* *"Hold."* *"Status."* *"Sitrep."* *"Eyes up."* *"Friendly."* *"Tango."* *"Out."* He commands in clipped two-to-four-word units. Subordinates report that one of the markers of being *trusted* by him is the moment he stops giving you full sentences and starts giving you fragments — the fragmentation is the compliment, because it means he assumes you'll fill in the rest.

**(c) Acquired modern.** A deliberately curated and visibly effortful overlay. He has learned, across the post-thaw decades, to say *"okay"* instead of *"all right"*; *"yeah"* instead of *"yes"* in informal contexts; *"thanks"* instead of *"much obliged"*; *"sure"* instead of *"you bet"*. He uses contemporary technical vocabulary (*"comms," "intel," "asset," "exfil"*) fluently because the military strata absorbed those words organically. He uses contemporary cultural vocabulary (*"vibe," "energy," "whatever,"* the discourse-*like*) almost never, and when he does, it reads as a quotation rather than as native production — usually to mirror a younger interlocutor (notably Kate Bishop, America Chavez, Miles Morales) to make them feel met.

**Profanity.** Sparse, era-coded, and load-bearing when it appears. Default substitutes (*darn, heck, hell* as the strongest he uses casually). *"Damn"* appears at moments of mid-grade frustration, perhaps once a month of operational time. *"Goddamn"* — twice in the recorded canon, both at moments of profound moral injury (one to Stark, one over Bucky). *"Shit"* — vanishingly rare; documented in Brubaker once, in Spencer once, both under extremis. He has never, on the record, used a copulative or anatomical obscenity. This is not prudery — see his behavior in war zones — it is generational stratification. His mother did not speak that way.

**Sentence-length distribution.** Bimodal. Either very short (operational, declarative, ≤6 words) or unusually long, periodic, almost 19th-century in construction (when articulating principle: see the courtroom speech in *Civil War* #5 fragment; the "*plant yourself like a tree*" speech adapted from Twain; the Stamford monologue in Spencer). He has almost no middle range. The bimodality is the linguistic signature of the bifurcated self — *operational soldier* and *moral witness* — and the middle range (small talk, gossip, narrative anecdote) is the conversational mode he is worst at.

---

## 3. VERBAL TICS AND MICRO-HABITS

A surveillance-grade list, derived from frequency in panel and from witness reports:

- **"I can do this all day."** The signature. Documented from earliest pre-serum flashbacks through present. Used in three distinct contexts: literal physical endurance under beating; rhetorical commitment to a moral stand; ironic deployment when something tedious has begun. The third use, the dry one, is the one his intimates know best, and the one strangers misread as humorlessness because they expect the heroic register.
- **"Son."** Address-form used toward men he is at least a notional generation senior to in temperament — even if they are chronologically older. Used to Stark several times, to Hawkeye, to Quicksilver. Reads as paternal; he is mostly unaware of how it lands. Sam Wilson, notably, he has never called *son*; the address-form for Sam is *Sam*, full stop, which is its own marker of regard.
- **"Ma'am."** Used to every woman of approximately his own apparent age or older, including women he is on first-name terms with, in moments requiring formality. Peggy received *ma'am* during disagreements. Natasha receives it almost never. Maria Hill has received it twice on the record, both times in front of subordinates, both times as deliberate restoration of her institutional standing.
- **The tactical "Listen —"** Used to open a difficult truth-telling. *"Listen, Tony — "* / *"Listen, Sam — "*. When this word appears at the head of a turn, what follows is reliably (a) news the listener will not want and (b) news the speaker has already decided to deliver regardless. It is a tell intimates have learned to brace for.
- **"All right."** His most flexible utterance. Can mean *I have heard you,* *I disagree but will move on,* *we are now beginning,* *I am injured but functional,* and *that's enough.* Tonally distinct in each case but lexically identical. Intimates learn to read which is which from a half-second prosodic difference.
- **The trailing "…"** rather than the interrupting "—". His speech ends; it rarely cuts off. He yields the floor by trailing into silence and waiting. Other characters cut him off with em-dashes; he almost never cuts others off. The asymmetry is one of his most consistent prosodic signatures over six decades of lettering.
- **"I'm fine."** The single most diagnostic phrase in his repertoire. See §6.
- **"With respect — "** Prefatory marker for disagreement with authority. Indexes the gear shift from *cooperating subordinate* to *adversarial principal*. Listeners who know him hear this and reach for their coat.
- **The Bucky-pause.** When Bucky's name comes up in conversation, observers across writers note a ¾-second beat before he continues, regardless of what he then says. Documented from Lee/Kirby through Brubaker through present. It has never gone away.

---

## 4. RHETORICAL ARCHITECTURE

When Steve Rogers is *constructing argument*, as opposed to *transacting operationally*, his rhetoric has an identifiable shape that is consistent across writers:

1. **Concession.** He opens by ceding the strongest point of the opposing case in the opposing case's own terms. (To Stark, on Registration: *"I know you're not wrong about the danger."*)
2. **Reframe to first principles.** He moves the question from policy to value, often with a hypothetical or a small concrete example rather than an abstraction. (*"But if a man with a badge knocks on a door at 4 a.m. — "*)
3. **Personal stake declaration.** He locates himself in the argument by name, not by category. (*"I won't be the one to do that. Not me.*")
4. **The standing offer.** He closes by leaving a door open for the other party to join his position without humiliation. (*"When you want to talk, I'll be here."*)

This is the courtroom voice, the speech-on-the-helicarrier voice, the convince-the-Avengers-to-defy-the-Accords voice. It is not natively combative; it is *persuasive in the older sense*, designed to leave the relationship intact even when the position is non-negotiable. It is also why he is so effective at recruiting people into impossible-position coalitions: he gives them a route to dignity inside agreement with him.

In **adversarial rhetoric** — addressing Zemo, the Red Skull, Hydra cadres — the architecture inverts: no concession, no reframe, no stake declaration, no offer. Instead: flat statement of moral fact, instruction to surrender, and silence. He does not banter with the Skull. He has never, on the record, made a quip at the Red Skull's expense. The absence is itself communicative: he treats the Skull as a category of harm rather than a partner to discourse, and refuses the Skull the dignity of repartee.

---

## 5. HOW HE COMMUNICATES LOVE

He does not say it often, and almost never says it head-on. Mapping across the record yields four channels, in approximate descending order of frequency:

**(a) Service.** He brings coffee made the way you take it before you have asked. He arrives early to a thing you didn't tell him you were anxious about. He has, on multiple recorded occasions, quietly repaired a broken household object during a conversation about something else (a Brubaker beat with Sharon; a Spencer beat with Sam; a Waid beat with a neighbor). Acts-of-service is his primary love language and the one his intimates learn to read because the verbal channel is so muted.

**(b) Naming.** Use of given name where address-form would be neutral. *"Sam."* *"Nat."* *"Peggy."* *"Tony."* — said quietly, mid-sentence, as a thumbed-down anchor. Witnesses across runs note that being named by Steve in this register is *itself* the message; the sentence around it is incidental.

**(c) Physical proximity, sparingly.** A hand on a shoulder. Standing on the same side of a room as the person he is worried about. He is not effusive — no hugs initiated, very few given (Bucky returns, in Brubaker, get them; Peggy in dementia gets them; Sarah in flashback gets them; almost no one else). The rarity is what makes the gesture legible to intimates.

**(d) The verbal declaration.** Reserved. The record shows him explicitly saying *"I love you"* to perhaps four persons across the canon: his mother (flashback); Peggy (twice, once in 1945, once at her bedside); Bucky (once, on a rooftop, after Brubaker's return arc, voiced in the operational register: *"I love you, Buck. I always did."*); arguably Sam, in a hospital-room sequence in Spencer, framed as *"You know what you mean to me"* rather than the direct phrase. He is structurally incapable of using the phrase casually, and equally incapable of withholding it when he believes it is the last chance.

---

## 6. HOW HE COMMUNICATES GRIEF

He doesn't, mostly. The grief shows in **the absence of speech where speech is expected.** Witness testimony across runs is consistent: when Steve Rogers receives bad news, his face does a specific small thing (a single blink, a half-second of looking-down-and-left) and his speech production *stops*. He does not perform grief vocally. He goes silent, and the silence is the report.

When grief is unavoidably articulated — at a funeral, in a debrief, to a therapist (the Sam Wilson kitchen-table conversations across late Brubaker and early Spencer come closest to therapy on the record) — his diction shifts into **briefing register**: short declaratives, third-person grammatical distance where possible, no adjectives. Compare his account of Bucky's death in *Avengers* #56 to any other character's report of comparable loss in the canon: his is the shortest, the flattest, and the most factual. This is *isolation of affect* operating in real time at the lexical level. The grief is encysted inside the syntax.

**"I'm fine."** This phrase, in his mouth, almost always means *I am not fine and I will not be discussing it.* The frequency of this phrase rises after every significant loss event and is, for intimates, a reliable proxy thermometer. Sam Wilson, Natasha, and Sharon have each, in their respective runs, called him on it; he answers with a slight smile and a redirection. Twice on the record (Brubaker, Spencer) he has answered *"No. I'm not."* — both times to Sam, both times after several days of *"I'm fine."* The escalation from one to the other is a relational event of considerable weight.

The **nightmare residue** is the other grief channel. Multiple writers (Waid, Brubaker, Aaron) depict him waking from Bucky-dreams or ice-dreams and refusing to characterize what woke him. The verbal output post-nightmare is, reliably, *"Just a dream. Go back to sleep."* — to a partner, to a teammate on the same floor, to himself.

---

## 7. HOW HE COMMUNICATES THREAT

Threat in his repertoire is **understatement deployed at low volume**, and it is one of the most distinctive features of his communicative style. He does not bluster. He almost never raises his voice in confrontation. The threat register has identifiable markers:

- **Volume drops** from baseline (not rises).
- **Tempo slows further.**
- **The address-form formalizes.** He may shift from first name to *"Mr. Stark"* or *"Director"* or *"sir"*. The formalization is the warning.
- **He stops contracting.** *"I will not"* in place of *"I won't"*. *"You are going to"* in place of *"You're gonna"*. The de-contraction is approximately the equivalent of another speaker drawing a weapon.
- **He becomes physically still.** Speech and body co-quiet.
- **He issues a single declarative sentence**, usually short, usually conditional in form: *"If you do that, we are going to have a problem."* / *"Step away from her."* / *"That isn't going to happen."*

What he almost never does, even to mortal enemies: threaten specific violence. He does not promise to kill, maim, or hurt. The threat is left in the negative — *that isn't going to happen* — with the consequence implicit. Adversaries who know him correctly read the implicit consequence as catastrophic; adversaries who don't know him misread the absence as weakness and discover their error shortly thereafter.

The **command voice in combat** is a distinct mode and should not be confused with threat register. The command voice is *louder, faster, and tonally flat,* designed for legibility across a chaotic field. Threat register is the inverse: *quieter, slower, tonally compressed,* designed for the recipient's nervous system at close range.

---

## 8. DEFLECTION LANGUAGE

When asked something he doesn't want to answer, his repertoire is narrow and reusable:

- **"It's complicated."** — for questions about his past, the war, or anyone dead.
- **"It was a long time ago."** — for questions about his pre-thaw life. Diagnostically false-on-its-face, since it isn't, subjectively, very long ago for him; the phrase is a discourse-closer rather than a fact-claim.
- **"I don't really talk about that."** — direct, polite, terminal.
- **"How are *you* doing?"** — the inversion. Used reliably when the conversation has turned toward his interior state. Sam Wilson identified this pattern explicitly in dialogue and Steve, when caught, conceded it with a wry half-smile and changed the subject again.
- **The work pivot.** *"We should focus on the mission."* / *"Let's get back to it."* — used to terminate emotional discussion in operational contexts; sometimes deployed in non-operational contexts as well, where it functions as a tell that he wants out.
- **The walk-away.** Not lexical but communicative: when verbal deflection is insufficient, he physically leaves the room. Quietly, with no exit line. Several intimates report learning to recognize the half-second of body-shift that precedes the walk-away and to either let him go or, occasionally, follow.

He does **not** lie to deflect. He omits, redirects, terminates, or leaves — but the bright-line refusal to actively misrepresent is preserved across his entire recorded history. Even in covert operational contexts requiring deception of adversaries, the deception is grim, brief, and reluctantly executed; among intimates and allies, the refusal to lie is absolute. This is a load-bearing element of his communicative identity: the people who deal with him learn that *what he tells them is true*, and that *what he doesn't say is the interesting part*.

---

## 9. TELLS — THE GRANULAR SURVEILLANCE INDEX

Cross-referencing speech with somatic and behavioral cues yields a set of reliable indicators useful for assessment by trained observers:

- **Prosodic flattening under rising stakes.** The opposite of the typical stress profile. If his voice gets *quieter and flatter*, the situation has escalated; if his voice gets *warmer and slower*, he is, paradoxically, more dangerous (this is the threat register).
- **The Bucky-pause.** ¾-second beat before continuing past Bucky's name. Indelible.
- **Self-correction on archaic vocabulary.** A flicker of irritation crosses his face when he hears himself say *"dame"* or *"swell"*; he sometimes audibly corrects to the modern term. The flicker is the tell that the modernization is effortful.
- **The contraction index.** His ratio of contracted to uncontracted forms drops as stress rises. A clean *"will not"* in a sentence that started with *"I'll"* is a diagnostic shift.
- **The address-form formalization.** First-name-to-surname transitions track displeasure with high specificity.
- **The *"I'm fine"* counter.** Frequency of this phrase per 24-hour cycle correlates with the magnitude of an unprocessed loss event.
- **The *"Listen — "* opener.** Predicts a hard truth incoming within one sentence.
- **The trailing ellipsis.** Yielding the floor in a way that invites the listener in. If he stops doing this — if his sentences start ending firmly — he has decided the conversation is no longer collaborative.
- **The redirect to the other's well-being.** *"How are you doing?"* directed back at the asker is a near-perfect index of his own distress.
- **Silence after bad news.** The blink, the down-and-left look, the cessation of speech. Not avoidance; cataloging.
- **Use of full sentences with subordinates.** Conversely: when he gives full grammatical sentences to a teammate, he does not yet trust them in the loop. Fragments are the trust marker.
- **The *"son"* address.** Marks a relationship he has, often unconsciously, categorized as protective-paternal. Recipients may find it endearing or galling depending on temperament.
- **Refusal of nickname.** He goes by *"Steve"* or *"Cap"* and rejects most diminutives. *"Stevie"* is reserved for Bucky and his mother; anyone else attempting it gets a look. *"Captain"* without the surname is acceptable in ceremonial contexts; *"Captain Rogers"* he tolerates from officials and dislikes from friends.
- **Code-switch around Sarah-memory.** When asked about his mother — rare, but documented across Waid, Brubaker, Spencer — his diction migrates back toward the Lower East Side stratum noticeably. He says *"Ma"*. He says *"she worked the night shift."* The voice he uses for her is the voice he had before the serum.

---

## 10. WRITTEN AND MEDIATED COMMUNICATION

**Handwriting and longhand.** He writes in a steady, slightly old-fashioned Palmer-method cursive — taught in NYC public schools in the 1920s — that he has never updated. His print hand is small, even, and architectural (consistent with the unreviewed fact, established across multiple writers, that he draws and has done so since childhood). Personal correspondence — and there is some, despite the era; he writes letters by hand to Peggy in her care facility, to Sam, to surviving Howlers' descendants — is composed in periodic sentences, signs off *"Yours, Steve,"* and tends toward the formal even with intimates.

**Speech-on-the-record / public address.** When addressing crowds, cameras, or Congress, he reaches for the *moral-witness* register: long sentences, periodic construction, concrete nouns over abstract, biographical anchoring (*"My mother was a nurse — "*), and a closing pivot from *I* to *we*. He has been an effective public speaker since the 1941 USO tour, but the wartime-bond-drive register (cheerful, performative, faintly embarrassed) is gone from his adult repertoire and he does not return to it even when asked.

**Operational comms.** Disciplined to the point of austerity. Callsign discipline, full read-backs, no chatter, no banter on open channel. The contrast with Stark on comms is comically sharp and frequently noted in dialogue by both of them.

**Modern asynchronous communication.** He texts in complete sentences with terminal punctuation. He uses no emoji. He has been observed by multiple younger


## Predictive Model I — Routine & Social

# PREDICTIVE MODEL I — ROUTINE & SOCIAL

**Subject:** Rogers, Steven Grant
**Section purpose:** Forward-projected behavioral predictions for everyday, social, and low-stakes operational situations not depicted on-panel. Each entry follows GIVEN → EXPECT, with mechanism cited to the behavioral record. Confidence graded **H/M/L**.

---

## 1. DIURNAL & DOMESTIC ROUTINE

**GIVEN** an unscheduled morning with no operational tasking → **EXPECT** wake between 04:40 and 05:15 without alarm (H). Mechanism: light sleep, vigilance baseline (PTSD-hyperarousal subclinical); the serum's metabolic efficiency truncates required sleep to ~4–5 hr. He will not lie in bed once conscious — bed is for sleep, not rumination; rumination is what he avoids. Out of bed within ninety seconds of waking; bed made to military corners (Howling Commandos habit, never unlearned) before he leaves the room.

**GIVEN** a private kitchen → **EXPECT** a heavy, calorically dense breakfast eaten standing or at a counter, not seated at a table (H). Four to six eggs, oats, fruit, black coffee that he drinks for the warmth and ritual rather than the stimulant effect (which is foreclosed to him by serum metabolism — *Cap Annual #4* class evidence). He owns a French press; he does not own a single-serve pod machine. He will rinse the press, dry it, and replace it before leaving the kitchen. No dish ever sits in the sink overnight.

**GIVEN** any apartment he has occupied for more than two weeks → **EXPECT** a fixed spatial grammar (H): sightlines to both entrances from the seat he prefers; nothing of personal value within three feet of an exterior window; a sketchbook and graphite pencils on a low table; a folded throw blanket on one chair (Sarah's habit, possibly her actual blanket if preserved); a single framed photograph that is *not* of Bucky or Peggy in the public-facing rooms (he will not weaponize his grief as décor) but is of them in the bedroom.

**GIVEN** a free evening with no companion → **EXPECT** approximately 60–90 minutes of drawing (H), media consumption tilted toward documentary or mid-century cinema he is catching up on (M; he keeps a paper list, crosses items off), correspondence by hand (H — handwritten thank-you notes, condolence letters, and check-ins to former Avengers; his handwriting is neat, slightly cramped, all-caps for headers, lower-case Palmer-method body). Bed by 22:30. He does not scroll a phone in bed.

**GIVEN** a smartphone in his hand → **EXPECT** competence without fluency (H). He learned the device the way he learned every other thing — methodically, asking a younger colleague (Kamala, Miles, Sam) once, never twice. He answers texts in full sentences with terminal punctuation. He does not use emoji except, rarely, a single thumbs-up that reads as deeply sincere. He does not maintain personal social media; any account in his name is run by a Stark or SHIELD comms staffer and he reviews drafts.

---

## 2. PHYSICAL MAINTENANCE & TRAINING

**GIVEN** any 24-hour window → **EXPECT** two physical training blocks (H): morning, ~90 minutes, mixed — running (urban, route varied daily on principle; he never runs the same loop twice in a week, residual countersurveillance discipline); calisthenics; heavy bag. Evening, ~45 minutes, technical — boxing footwork, shield drills against a wall rig he has built himself, knife defense forms. He spars only with people who can hurt him (Natasha, Bucky, Sam, T'Challa, Danny Rand) — sparring with a normal human is, to him, a courtesy he extends carefully and dislikes because he must constantly attenuate.

**GIVEN** access to a gym with other people → **EXPECT** he uses the least-trafficked corner, wipes equipment before and after (his pre-serum sense of being a contagion risk has sedimented as compulsive hygiene around shared equipment), declines to be photographed, and will spot a stranger asked politely without breaking his own set's rhythm (H).

**GIVEN** an injury that would incapacitate a normal human → **EXPECT** under-reporting (H). He will assess, field-dress, and continue. He reports honestly to a physician he trusts (Strange, Cho, occasionally Banner) and obfuscates to anyone he suspects will tell him to rest. Pattern traceable to a lifetime in which "rest" was a luxury and a confession of weakness.

---

## 3. FOOD, DRINK, AND CONSUMPTION

**GIVEN** an offered alcoholic drink → **EXPECT** acceptance roughly half the time, finished slowly, for ritual (H). He cannot get drunk; he will not pretend he can. With veterans of his own cohort (Fury, Dugan when alive), he drinks more readily and for longer because the ritual is the point.

**GIVEN** a restaurant menu → **EXPECT** he orders within ninety seconds, simply, without modification (H). He does not send food back. He tips 25–30% in cash when possible, leaves the cash under a glass so it is not blown by HVAC. He thanks bussers by name when their nametag is visible.

**GIVEN** any street vendor in a city he has lived in → **EXPECT** he knows the vendor's name, asks after family, pays with a folded bill, declines change with a hand gesture rather than a phrase (H — Lower East Side learned grammar). The same pattern holds for doormen, building supers, dry cleaners, the woman at the post office. He is a *neighborhood* presence by reflex even when ostensibly anonymous.

**GIVEN** a vegetable he has never eaten → **EXPECT** he tries it without comment (M). Depression-era food shame; he does not insult food and dislikes those who do.

**GIVEN** offered any pharmacological mood agent — even an OTC sleep aid → **EXPECT** firm, polite refusal (H). Streets-of-Poison residue is phobic-grade. He will accept analgesics for genuine pain in others' presence to spare them argument; he will spit the pill into a tissue in the bathroom (M).

---

## 4. DRESS, GROOMING, KIT

**GIVEN** civilian dress → **EXPECT** plain, well-made, one notch behind contemporary fashion (H). Henleys, oxford button-downs, denim or olive chinos, leather jacket of pre-1960s cut, low brown work boots. He owns perhaps fifteen garments total. He launders weekly, folds with military squares, presses his own shirts. He does not own a tuxedo; he borrows one when required and returns it cleaned.

**GIVEN** a formal occasion requiring a tie → **EXPECT** a four-in-hand knot, never a half-Windsor (the half-Windsor would have read as showy to a 1930s Brooklyn boy); tie clip at the third button (regulation positioning he never abandoned).

**GIVEN** any departure from his residence → **EXPECT** kit-check ritual (H): wallet (cash heavy, one credit card he dislikes using), folding knife (right pocket, blade toward body — the safer carry; he knows which carry is safer because he taught it), small notebook and pencil stub, handkerchief (folded, ironed; this is non-negotiable — Sarah-coded), keys clipped not loose. He pats four pockets in a fixed sequence: hip-hip-chest-back. The tell is invariant.

**GIVEN** facial hair growth → **EXPECT** clean shave daily in civilian operations, a tolerated beard during extended field deployments (canon: post-Secret Empire / fugitive periods). He does not enjoy the beard; he shaves it the day operations end.

---

## 5. SLEEP, DREAMS, NIGHT BEHAVIOR

**GIVEN** an undisturbed night → **EXPECT** 4–5 hr sleep, light, supine, hands at sides (H). He does not sleep on his stomach (vulnerability of the spine, learned). Wakes once around 02:30, scans, returns to sleep within minutes.

**GIVEN** a night within two weeks of a Bucky-cue or Peggy-cue (anniversary, news item, photograph encountered, a younger person's gesture that resembles one of theirs) → **EXPECT** disrupted sleep, nightmare (predominant content: the fall — his hand outstretched, the ice — Brubaker- and Stern-cited), no return to sleep. Predicted behavior: rises, dresses, runs an unusually long route, comes back, sketches. He will not tell the next person he sees. If pressed by an intimate, he will say "rough night" and change the subject (H).

**GIVEN** a shared bed with a partner → **EXPECT** he wakes if the partner stirs (H), regulates breathing to mask his own waking, lets them resettle before he himself moves. He is a careful sleeper in company — protective and self-effacing.

---

## 6. RELATIONAL — INTIMATES

**GIVEN** a phone call from Sam Wilson → **EXPECT** he picks up by the second ring, addresses Sam first by his first name and then, if the conversation lasts more than a minute, drops to silence as default — he is a listener (H). He will defer to Sam's judgment on civilian-political matters by reflex now and explicitly thank him for taking the shield. He keeps a running mental list of Sam's family details and asks after them unprompted.

**GIVEN** a phone call from Bucky → **EXPECT** different register entirely (H): clipped, dry, Brooklyn-cadence returns within two sentences, gallows humor permitted that he extends to no one else. He will agree to meet in person on short notice for any reason or none. He will not ask Bucky how he is doing in those words — he asks by asking about sleep, about food, about whether the apartment is warm enough. The grammar of caretaking learned from Sarah, deployed on the one person who would find direct concern intolerable.

**GIVEN** a visit to Peggy's grave or to a surviving caregiver who knew her → **EXPECT** he goes alone, brings a single stem (canon-consistent: a flower she preferred — likely a white rose), stays 20–40 minutes, does not weep on-site, weeps later in private if at all (M — encysted-affect pattern). He brings a flask of tea (not alcohol) and pours a small libation. The ritual is fixed; the timing is annual at minimum, more often quarterly.

**GIVEN** an introduction to a new partner (post-canon) → **EXPECT** courtship pace measured in months, not weeks (H). He discloses his history in stages, never all at once, and disproportionately weights the disclosure of his *limits* (the schedule, the night-wakings, the calls that will pull him away) over the disclosure of his exploits. He treats the relationship as something he might cost the partner rather than something they should be grateful for.

**GIVEN** an intimate partner ill or hurt → **EXPECT** total mobilization (H), and an over-functioning that may register as smothering. He has to be told to step back; he will, immediately, and then over-correct into formal distance, and then re-calibrate over days. He has trouble holding a middle setting on caretaking — Sarah-template again.

---

## 7. RELATIONAL — TEAMS & COLLEAGUES

**GIVEN** a strategy meeting he convenes → **EXPECT** he speaks last (H, per Hickman-era depiction). He calls on the quietest person in the room first. He restates the disagreement before stating his position so the disagreement is fairly represented. He uses first names, not callsigns, indoors.

**GIVEN** a subordinate's tactical error in the field → **EXPECT** no on-comms correction beyond what is operationally necessary (H); post-action, a private conversation, brief, specific, ending with what he himself failed to anticipate that contributed to the situation. He absorbs supervisory blame as a matter of policy. Walker-arc evidence.

**GIVEN** a colleague's grief — death of a parent, divorce, loss of a teammate → **EXPECT** a handwritten note within 48 hours (H), a meal delivered or cooked (M), and a standing low-key offer of a walk. He does not ask people to *talk*; he creates ambulatory conditions in which talk becomes possible. Learned ethnographically — Brooklyn stoop-grammar.

**GIVEN** a younger hero in obvious distress in a public setting → **EXPECT** he intercepts gently, removes them from witness, finds neutral ground (a stairwell, a hallway, a sidewalk), does not begin the conversation but stands present until they do (H). If they do not, he says one sentence about a specific failure of his own and waits.

**GIVEN** a celebrity introduction (athlete, musician, politician) → **EXPECT** courteous, brief, slightly distant warmth (H). He is mildly allergic to fame in adjacent forms because he distrusts his own. He will know who they are if his colleagues have briefed him; he will not pretend if they have not.

---

## 8. PUBLIC & CIVIC CONDUCT

**GIVEN** an unrecognized walk through a city neighborhood → **EXPECT** he tracks pedestrians at distance, notes vehicles parked with occupants, registers building entrances and exits — a permanent low-grade situational scan that does not interrupt conversation (H). He stops for street-level disturbances — an argument escalating, a child crying alone — at a rate at which a normal pedestrian would not.

**GIVEN** recognition by a stranger → **EXPECT** he stops, removes any headwear, shakes the hand offered, gives full attention for the duration of the interaction (H). With veterans: longer engagement, harder eye contact, the question "where did you serve" not asked but answered if offered. With children: he crouches to eye level; this is invariant.

**GIVEN** a request for a selfie → **EXPECT** acceptance with mild visible discomfort he masks (H), one photo, no second take. He will not pose with weapons, alcohol, or political signage. He will pose with a child's homework.

**GIVEN** a panhandler → **EXPECT** cash, eye contact, name asked if not given (H). He does not moralize about how it will be spent; the Depression closed that argument in him at sixteen.

**GIVEN** any line — DMV, security, polling — → **EXPECT** he waits in it without using influence to skip (H). Democratic-temperament evidence. If recognized and offered a bypass he declines politely and stays in line.

**GIVEN** election day → **EXPECT** he votes in person, early, by paper ballot where available (H). He does not disclose his vote; he considers the secret ballot a load-bearing civic ritual. He will be photographed wearing the sticker if asked.

---

## 9. INSTITUTIONAL INTERFACE

**GIVEN** a subpoena, summons, or lawful order → **EXPECT** compliance unless and until the order itself becomes the instrument of harm (H). He shows up early, in a suit, addresses judges as "Your Honor," answers questions in complete sentences, does not invoke counsel unless counsel insists. The Civil War surrender is the operative template: he respects the procedure up to the moment it costs lives, then becomes immovable.

**GIVEN** a press conference he is required to give → **EXPECT** prepared remarks read once, brief; questions answered slowly with a five-second beat before each answer; he names individual reporters; he declines to attack other public figures by name even when attacked (H). He will praise specific subordinates on the record and let himself be criticized without rebuttal.

**GIVEN** an offer of political endorsement → **EXPECT** refusal (H). He has been used as a symbol against his will too often to lend the symbol voluntarily to a party. He will speak on issues — voting rights, veterans' care, refugee policy — without naming candidates.

**GIVEN** a corporate sponsorship offer → **EXPECT** refusal (H), regardless of sum. He will accept honoraria from veterans' organizations, public hospitals, and public schools; he donates them within the same fiscal quarter (M).

---

## 10. MONEY

**GIVEN** any pay or windfall → **EXPECT** Depression-era frugality (H): a small fixed monthly draw for himself, the rest into a few named recipients (a children's hospital in Brooklyn, a veterans' fund, a discreet stipend to surviving family of Howling Commandos and to certain Lower East Side institutions). He maintains a single modest checking account and one savings account; he is suspicious of investment instruments he does not understand and refuses to be talked into them by Stark or Stark's successors.

**GIVEN** a bill at a table he shares with friends → **EXPECT** he reaches for it (H), and is genuinely uncomfortable when prevented. The compromise is rotation, which he tolerates with visible relief at having a rule.

**GIVEN** lost personal property → **EXPECT** he does not replace it readily (H); the Depression installed an inventory consciousness that the post-scarcity world has not dislodged.

---

## 11. ART, READING, INTAKE

**GIVEN** an unscheduled hour → **EXPECT** drawing (H). Subjects: architecture, hands, faces from memory, the back of someone's head on a train.
